# Glass Podium

# Chapter 1

The shuttle from Meiringen climbed the switchbacks in low gear, and Kai sat in the back with his bag between his knees and watched the road fold away behind each turn. Snow packed both shoulders of the road, and the guardrails were crusted white. The driver hadn't spoken since the valley floor, just worked the wheel through the hairpins with the practiced indifference of someone who drove this road four times a day and didn't need conversation to do it. A small mercy. Kai had used up his supply of pleasant talk on the Zurich connection, chatting with a woman who'd recognized him from a Meridian promo spot and wanted to know what it felt like. Running enhanced. Like running, he'd told her. But faster. She'd laughed as if he'd made a joke.

The altitude registered at the second hairpin. Not dramatic, just a tightness behind his sternum, breath becoming labor instead of habit at two thousand and change meters. His lungs would catch up in a few days. The spinal neural bridge had already caught up — he could feel it if he paid attention, a faint warmth between his shoulder blades where the bridge interface sat, the hardware recalibrating his oxygen distribution before his body had finished registering the deficit. The bridge was always ahead of him. That was the point.

Snow-covered firs lined the road, bowed under weight, thinning as the altitude climbed. The light was going. Late January in the Bernese Alps meant the sun started quitting around four, and the sky above the ridgeline was already the pale, exhausted grey of a winter afternoon giving up early. Kai pressed his forehead against the cold window and watched the tree line drop away.

The Kirchberg Institute appeared around the final curve. A campus of concrete and glass set into the mountainside — training halls, residential blocks, a medical wing with windows catching the last of the daylight. Not beautiful, but functional — the kind of place that assumed you were there to work and had designed every surface accordingly. Floodlights had already come on along the approach road, and the facility's glass facade reflected them back in flat panels of white that made the building look like it was generating its own weather.

The shuttle stopped at the main entrance. Kai stepped out and the cold hit his face, the dry metallic cold of high altitude — thinner than Indiana winter, sharper, with none of the damp heaviness that meant snow was actually coming. This cold was just the air being honest about its temperature. Negative twelve, maybe. His breath came out solid.

Inside, the lobby was warm, carpeted, aggressively neutral. A receptionist scanned his credentials and handed him a room key and an orientation packet in a folder embossed with the Meridian BioSystems logo. The folder was thick enough to suggest someone at corporate believed paperwork was a substitute for conversation.

"Dinner's at seven in the cafeteria. Dr. Qadir will see you for your initial calibration at eight."

"Great. Thanks."

"Welcome to Kirchberg, Mr. Brennan."

He followed corridor signs to the residential wing. The corridor was fluorescent-lit, with doors on both sides, each fitted with a small digital display showing room status. Most were blank — dark rooms, empty, or athletes who'd disabled their readouts. A few showed scrolling biometric data visible through half-open doors. Heart rate, graft integration, neural latency. The casual broadcast of what would have been private medical information anywhere else. Here it was like leaving your shoes outside your door.

He passed a woman stretching in her doorway, legs in a split that ran the full width of the frame with the mechanical precision of a compass opening. She nodded at him without interrupting, her calves showing the same engineered definition his did — bilateral symmetry too clean to be the product of genetics and training alone. Her eyes tracked him with the flat focus of someone whose visual processing was running at a higher refresh rate than his. He nodded back and kept walking.

The corridor cut through a connecting wing between residential and medical. Through a glass partition he could see a corner of what had to be the calibration bay — reclining chairs, monitor banks, the blue standby glow of diagnostic screens in a room that looked unoccupied. The hum was louder here, a bass note that lived in the walls and floor and the soles of his shoes, and Kai felt it in his sternum before he heard it with his ears. Further on, a second glass wall overlooked the pool deck: fifty meters of chlorinated water under flat white light, the surface so still it looked solid. A swimmer in a green cap sat on the far edge with her feet in the water, not moving, not preparing, just sitting there with the patience of someone waiting for something she expected to take a long time.

The room was spartan and adequate. It had a bed, a desk, a bathroom, and a window facing the eastern ridge. The wall-mounted diagnostic display was already active, scrolling data from his enhancement system: heart rate, core temperature, synthetic graft integration, neural bridge latency. All green, everything functioning. He dropped his bag on the bed and stood at the window. Snow fell in the floodlights' periphery. The mountains beyond were shapes now, losing detail to the dark. The facility's ventilation system cycled air with a low, constant drone that lived below conversation but above silence.

He'd been to training facilities before — Colorado Springs, the Meridian campus outside Portland, altitude work in Flagstaff. They'd all had the same basic architecture of purpose: track, gym, medical, cafeteria, sleep. But those places had natural athletes mixed in with the enhanced, and the mix created a visible gradient you could use to locate yourself. You knew where you stood. At Kirchberg, there was no gradient. Every body in the building was rebuilt. Enhancement wasn't the variable here; it was the baseline. The woman doing splits in her doorway hadn't been showing off. She'd been stretching before dinner.

Kai sat on the bed and ran his thumb along the inside of his left thigh, pressing until he found the seam. The synthetic muscle graft was invisible under the skin — no scar, no discoloration — but the texture changed at the interface where engineered fiber met natural tissue. A ridge, faint, running from mid-thigh to just above the knee. The right leg had the same seam in the same place. Below the knee, the calf grafts had their own. Bilateral symmetry. His legs looked better than they ever had when they were entirely his — the proportions a little too balanced, the muscle definition a little too even, legs a sculptor would give an athlete who existed only in reference photos.

Three years since the reconstruction. Two ACLs and a spinal compression fracture at twenty-seven, the kind of injury that ended natural careers because natural bodies didn't come back from it, not fully, not to world-record form. Meridian had offered the rebuild six weeks after the surgery. Full reconstruction: synthetic grafts in both legs, spinal neural bridge to replace the compromised nerve pathways, visual processing enhancement for reaction time. He'd signed in a hospital bed in Indianapolis with his mother sitting in the corner not saying anything and his agent on speaker saying everything.

He was faster now than he'd been at twenty-five, when the 200-meter record had been his and the body had been too.

***

The indoor track was a 200-meter oval under high ceilings with acoustic dampening that didn't quite work — footfalls carried, each one a clean report off the rubber surface followed by a fast, shallow echo. Kai walked in at nine the next morning and the sound of other athletes warming up was like listening to rain on a drum. Four runners were on the infield stretching and jogging short bursts, and a coach stood by the finish with a tablet and a stopwatch. The air was altitude-controlled and warmer than outside, and it smelled of rubber and the particular chemical cleanliness of a facility that was maintained rather than lived in.

He changed in the locker room and worked through his warm-up sequence: hip flexors, hamstrings, calf grafts. The grafts stretched differently than natural tissue. There was compliance but no complaint, a willingness in the synthetic fibers that felt cooperative rather than resistant. His real quads still ached when he pushed too deep. The grafted sections just extended, obliging, as though the muscle had already read the script.

The qualifying heat was a formality — five runners, posted times, a gate officer with a starter pistol and the unhurried manner of someone who would be doing this six more times today. Kai set his blocks at the line and sank into the crouch, hands on the rubber, weight forward on his fingertips. His visual processing enhancement activated without being asked — the lane ahead sharpened, depth of field narrowing, peripheral detail dimming to irrelevance. He hadn't triggered it. The system had read the posture, the setting, the context, and done what it always did at a start line.

The gun fired.

He came out of the blocks and his legs drove him into the curve. The synthetic muscle fired in a sequenced cascade — calf, quad, hip — and the power was clean and immediate and felt like his own effort in the same way that driving a car fast felt like running: the speed was real, the direction was yours, and somewhere in the middle was an engine doing the parts you couldn't. Through the curve, leaning, the centrifugal force managed by something in his hips and lower back that anticipated the angle before he thought about banking. The rubber passed beneath him in a blur of red-brown. Into the straight now, full speed, and this was where the quiet lived. There was no burn in his legs, no oxygen debt building in his chest, no negotiation between what the lungs could supply and what the muscles demanded. Just his body moving at a speed that should have felt like violence — feet barely touching the surface, arms pumping with a rhythm he wasn't setting — and felt instead like coasting. Like being carried very fast in a direction he'd agreed to.

He crossed the line. His body decelerated through the curve past the finish, and the transition from sprint to jog was seamless — no shock, no buckled stride, no sudden announcement of oxygen debt. The synthetic grafts downshifted smoothly, and his breathing barely changed. He used to finish the 200 and fold over, hands on knees, lungs screaming, vision spotted at the edges. That was the natural version. Now he ran the fastest 200 meters of his life and his body's first response was to inform him, politely, that it could go again.

The clock said 19.02.

Personal best by three hundredths. The other runners were still finishing. The gate officer noted the time on his tablet. A coach on the infield looked up, registered the number, nodded once, and went back to his stopwatch. One of the other runners — a hurdler from the Houston facility whose name Kai knew but couldn't find right now — jogged past and said, "Nice run," and Kai said, "Thanks," and that was the full extent of the event. A man had just run faster than anyone had run 200 meters indoors, and the appropriate institutional response was a nod and a noted time.

Kai stood at the edge of the track with his hands on his hips and tried to locate the race in his body. The usual markers weren't there. No residual burn in the quads, no trembling in the hamstrings, no gratitude from his lungs for the air they were now getting. His heart rate was settling into a rhythm that felt more like waking from a nap than recovering from a flat-out sprint. He'd been in the blocks ten seconds ago. Now he was standing here, breathing normally, and the distance between those two facts felt like someone had edited out the middle of a film and left him watching the credits.

He jogged the cool-down curve and his heart rate was already settling. The spinal bridge managed recovery the way a thermostat managed temperature — smooth, automatic, without consulting him. Sweat cooled on the back of his neck. The rubber under his shoes had a faint chemical bite to it, and he focused on that smell because it was something his senses were doing without optimization, just his nose being a nose, and even that felt thin, the sensation arriving and departing with less weight than it should have carried.

Nineteen-oh-two. Faster than anything he'd run in his natural career. Faster than the version of him that had set world records on legs made of what he'd been born with. The number was correct and the clock was correct and his legs had done the work and his lungs had done the work and his spine had done the work and all of it was functioning as designed and he had been present for the entire ten-point-something seconds of it.

He just couldn't tell which parts had been his idea.

He showered, changed, and ate lunch in the cafeteria. Institutional food — grilled chicken, rice, vegetables steamed past the point of having personality. The windows showed mountains in midday winter light, the sun a flat white presence behind a veil of cloud that hadn't committed to being overcast. The upper ridges held the light for a moment longer than the valleys, white against grey. Athletes ate in clusters. The conversations Kai overheard were ordinary: sleep quality, supplement protocols, someone's ongoing battle with the facility's hot water system. A woman two tables over was arguing quietly on her phone about a missed delivery. A young guy eating alone — twenty-one maybe, with a decathlete's broad-shouldered build and the slightly unfinished proportions of someone whose enhancement was recent enough that his body hadn't fully calibrated around it — was reading something on his tablet with the concentrated focus of a student who'd be tested on it later. He had an energy bar half-eaten beside his plate, forgotten in favor of whatever he was reading.

Kai's phone buzzed. His mother, texting from Lafayette. A photo of snow on the front porch — the specific yellow-grey Indiana snow that clung to everything, covering the porch railing and the mailbox and the edge of the welcome mat she'd had since he was in high school.

*Colder than you probably*

He typed: *Doubt it. 2100 meters up here*

*Is that high? I don't know what that is*

*About 7000 feet. Cold*

*How was the run?*

*Good. New personal best*

*That's wonderful honey. Call me this week ok?*

He set the phone down. His right hand was resting on the table and he noticed — in the distracted, half-attentive way of someone catching a familiar movement out of the corner of their eye — that his fingers had already closed around his water glass. He hadn't reached for it. The intent was there, the beginning of wanting a drink of water, but the hand had moved first. The synthetic muscle in his forearm had read the pattern and started the reach before the thought had finished forming.

He picked up the glass and drank. The water was cold. He set it down and looked at his hand.

It was nothing. A fraction of a second. The kind of thing that happened below the threshold of attention a hundred times a day. He would not have noticed it six months ago, or three months ago. He'd started noticing it in Portland, and then in Flagstaff, and now here, at a table in a cafeteria in the Swiss Alps, his hand wrapped around a glass of water it had decided to hold before he had.

***

The calibration bay was in the medical wing — a clean room with fluorescent tubes and the permanent low hum of processing equipment, a sound that lived in the walls, below the pitch of conversation but too present to be called silence. Kai had been told you stopped hearing it after a week. He wasn't there yet.

Yusuf Qadir was setting up when Kai arrived at eight. Short, dark-haired, glasses that were real prescription — one of the few people at the facility who'd declined enhancement for himself. He moved around the calibration rig with the comfort of someone who spent most of his waking hours in this room, adjusting monitor angles, tapping commands at a console, a grey diagnostic cable looped over one shoulder that he hadn't found a use for yet.

"Brennan. Come in, sit down." He gestured at the rig — a reclining chair boxed in by screens and connector ports, a setup that could have passed for a dentist's chair if dentists needed six monitors and a firmware interface. "How was the trip in?"

"Long."

"Always is. Zurich connection?"

"Yeah."

"God, yeah, that layover. Right, so." He patted the chair's headrest. "First calibration since Portland, which means I need a full baseline. About forty-five minutes, maybe an hour if your graft firmware's been running updates I don't know about. You good with that?"

"I'm good."

Kai settled into the chair. The surface was medical-grade vinyl, body-temperature, shaped to distribute his weight evenly across his back and legs. Yusuf attached sensor pads to his legs — inner thigh, outer calf, just above both ankles — his hands moving with the unhesitating precision of someone who'd done this ten thousand times and no longer thought about where to put them. He connected a cable to the spinal bridge access port at the small of Kai's back — a flat disc the size of a shirt button, set flush with the skin. The plug seated with a small mechanical click and Kai felt a brief, clean pressure at the base of his spine, a sensation that would have been uncomfortable if it lasted longer than a second.

"You'll feel a handshake. That's me saying hi to your bridge."

"Felt it."

"Brilliant. Right." Yusuf dropped into his chair and scrolled through the incoming data, his face lit by the console's blue-white glow. He had the focused, slightly rumpled look of an engineer at the end of a long day who still had three things left to do and was prioritizing by interest rather than urgency. "Any changes since Portland? Pain, numbness, anything with range of motion?"

"No. Everything's been fine."

"Good. Graft integration's solid, both legs. Your calf firmware pushed an update last week — did you catch that?"

"I felt a warmth in my lower legs for about ten minutes. Wednesday, I think."

"Wednesday, yeah, that tracks. Routine optimization pass. Nothing to worry about, it just recalibrated your calf response to some updated baseline data. Happens automatically." He scrolled further. "Bridge latency's within spec. Visual processing nominal. How's the visual been? Any artifacts?"

"Like what?"

"Halos around light sources, depth-of-field lag, colour banding. Some athletes get this thing where — it's hard to describe — the world goes slightly flat for a second? Like someone compressed the depth out of it."

"No, nothing like that."

"Good." Yusuf tapped a command and the sensor pads on Kai's legs came alive with a low vibration. "I'm going to map your graft response times. This bit's automatic — just try to stay relaxed."

He stayed still. A calibration pulse moved through his left calf — a contraction he hadn't initiated, the muscle tightening and releasing in a wave that ran from ankle to knee. Then the right calf. Then both quads, one after the other. Then the hamstrings. His body twitched and responded and twitched again, and Kai lay there looking at the ceiling tiles and let it happen. Each pulse was a small theft — his muscle doing something at someone else's instruction — and each time the muscle released, the control returned to him, and each time control returned he noticed the seam of the handover. The system giving him back what was already his.

This was the part that never made the interviews. The lying still. The being adjusted. The specific intimacy of letting another person tune the parts of your body you couldn't reach yourself, while you stared at the ceiling and felt your legs twitch on a schedule you didn't set.

Yusuf worked in focused silence for a while, scrolling data, occasionally leaning over to reposition a sensor pad. He had a habit of muttering to the data under his breath — not words, just small sounds of acknowledgment, the audible equivalent of nodding at something on a screen. When he moved a sensor on Kai's thigh, his fingers found the graft seam by touch, without looking.

"Right, so," Yusuf said after about twenty minutes.

His tone had shifted. Still easy, still conversational, but there was a weight under it now — the way a voice changes when the data says something the person was hoping it wouldn't.

"Your proprioception scores."

"What about them?"

"They're drifting." He angled the console screen toward Kai. Numbers, graphs, a timeline. Two trend lines — one blue, one orange — that had been tracking close together six months ago and were now separating. The gap between them was small but consistent, and it widened at the right edge of the graph.

"Blue line is your conscious motor intent. That's the signal your brain generates when you decide to move. Orange is actual motor execution — when the graft fires." He pointed at the gap. "In a healthy integrated system, these should overlap. Your brain says move, the graft moves, same moment. But over the last three months, the orange has been leading. Not by a lot. Forty, fifty milliseconds."

"So the graft is moving before I tell it to."

"Not exactly. Your system has predictive subroutines — they're supposed to be there, they're a feature, they reduce response latency. What they do is, they watch your motor patterns, learn your habits, and start prepping the muscle before the full conscious command comes through. You're reaching for a glass, the system recognizes the reach pattern halfway through your intent, and gets the muscle ready a few milliseconds early. In a sprint, that's what gives you those start times. It's meant to be invisible."

"And mine isn't invisible anymore."

"The window's widening. At ten milliseconds, nobody feels it. At fifty, some people start to notice. You're at fifty and trending up." He sat back, took off his glasses, rubbed the bridge of his nose with his thumb and forefinger. Put them back on. "Have you noticed anything?"

"Yeah."

"When?"

"Small things. My hand on a glass before I've decided to pick it up. A door handle. Reaching for my phone. It's like — the hand knows before I do. Not wrong, exactly. Just early."

"How long?"

"Couple months. Maybe longer." Kai looked at the graph on the screen, the two lines diverging. "It's hard to say when it started because, I mean, how do you time a decision? You don't. You decide and you move and they feel like the same thing. So when the move starts happening first, you don't notice right away. You just feel like you're very decisive."

Yusuf almost smiled at that. "Yeah. That's the design problem, isn't it. When the system predicts correctly, it just feels like you. It's only wrong predictions you'd catch. And from your data, it's not predicting wrong. It's predicting early."

Kai looked at the two lines on the screen. The blue one was his intent — his decisions, the electrical fact of wanting to move. The orange was his body doing it. And the orange was winning. Not by much. Not by enough to matter during a race or a training session or any other moment that moved too fast for the gap to register. But in the slow moments — the water glass, the door handle, the phone — fifty milliseconds was a lifetime. It was your hand doing what you were going to do, before you did it.

"Is it getting worse?"

"The trend is up. Consistent over three months. I'm going to log it as within normal drift parameters, because technically it is — Meridian's spec allows up to eighty milliseconds of predictive lead before they flag it. But I'm setting a tracker so we can compare session to session." He began disconnecting the sensor pads, peeling each one off with a small, precise pull. "If the window keeps opening, I'll talk to Meridian's firmware team about dialing the predictive threshold down."

"And if they don't want to dial it down."

"Then we have a different conversation." Yusuf wound the diagnostic cable around his hand in a neat loop and hung it on its hook by the console. "Look. Your system is working. It's working well. You ran a nineteen-oh-two today on an unfamiliar track at altitude after a transatlantic flight. The system is doing what it was built to do."

"I know."

"But it doesn't feel like yours."

"I didn't say that."

"Nah, you didn't." Yusuf tapped something final on the console and the screens went to standby, the blue light dimming to a low pulse. "Same time Thursday for a follow-up. Get some sleep. Altitude takes about three days to stop messing with your deep cycles."

"Thanks."

"Yeah. Night, Brennan."

Kai walked back to his room through the fluorescent corridor. The facility was quiet now — nine-thirty, athletes in their quarters, the common areas emptied, the cafeteria dark. His footsteps were the only sound except the building's ventilation — the same low drone he'd first heard through the glass partition that afternoon, ambient now, part of the facility's nervous system. He listened to his own gait on the hard floor: left foot, right foot, even and smooth, the synthetic grafts managing his stride with the consistency his natural legs had never quite achieved. He'd always been slightly dominant on the left side. Now he wasn't. The grafts had corrected the asymmetry without asking.

His room was dark except for the wall display's glow. Green readouts, all systems nominal, the system reporting on itself in the same quiet room where the person it was installed in was trying to fall asleep. He undressed, brushed his teeth, stood at the window. The mountains were invisible — just darkness and the faint reflection of the diagnostic display behind him, green numbers floating in the glass where the Bernese Alps should have been.

He thought about the graph. Two lines diverging. Forty to fifty milliseconds. A gap he hadn't been able to name until Yusuf drew it on a screen, and now it had a shape and a slope and the slope was up.

He reached for the light switch and his hand was already there.


# Chapter 2

Lena Morozova liked the pool at five-thirty in the morning, before the overhead lights came all the way up. The facility ran them on a schedule — dim at five, half-brightness by five-twenty, full fluorescent by six — and in that first stretch the water looked darker than it was, the lane lines softer, the ceiling lost above in shadow. She'd been swimming in pools like this since she was four years old. This one in Kirchberg was newer, cleaner, the temperature regulation tighter than the one in Zug where she'd grown up, but the fundamentals were the same: fifty meters of chlorinated water, the smell of it in the back of her throat before she even got her feet wet, lane ropes, a pace clock, the sound of her own breathing magnified under the surface into something that filled the world.

She changed in the women's room. The facility had individual changing cubicles with sensors that logged her weight, hydration level, and baseline heart rate as she stepped onto the scale, data that flowed directly to the coaching system. She didn't think about this. It had been part of her mornings for eighteen years.

The pool deck was cool under her bare feet. She walked to lane four — her lane, per the rotation schedule — and set her water bottle and pull buoy on the edge. Dara Lund was already at the wall of lane five, adjusting her goggles with small, precise movements. Three adjustments, always three: the left strap, the nose bridge, the right strap. Then she pulled her cap down over her ears and rolled her right shoulder twice, working something loose that had been recalibrated over the winter break. Dara had sent a card from Bergen at Christmas — not to Lena specifically, but to the floor's shared mailbox, a group card with a photo of her mother's garden in the snow. It was still pinned to the communal bulletin board in the hallway. No one had taken it down.

"Morning," Dara said.

"Morning."

They got in the water.

Lena's warm-up was four hundred meters freestyle at moderate pace, building through the second half. She pushed off the wall and the familiar compression closed around her: water temperature against her skin, the pressure of the surface above, the lane rope on her left and the pool floor below, her body settling into the stroke with the efficiency the neural link had been shaping since childhood. Shoulder rotation, catch, pull, hip-driven kick. The coaching files described it as "mechanically optimal." She'd read her own files since she was fifteen, the way some people read medical charts or school reports — clinical documents about someone who happened to be her. The relationship between what the files described and what she felt in the water was usually close enough that the distinction didn't matter.

The main set was eight two-hundreds at race pace, forty seconds rest. She pushed off for the first one and the link settled into the work: managing the pacing, the stroke architecture, the sequence of muscle recruitment that powered each pull. She was in there, somewhere inside the optimization, making the decisions she was aware of making — push harder here, hold the streamline tighter off the wall, breathe every three strokes instead of two because the altitude at Kirchberg thinned her oxygen uptake and the link compensated by extending the breath cycle, but she preferred the rhythm of three. The link handled the rest. What the rest was, exactly, she had never been able to articulate, because there had never been a version of her swimming without it.

Her times came in tight. 2:08.3, 2:08.1, 2:07.9, 2:08.0. The fourth was a slight fade, normal at this point in the training block, nothing that concerned her. She hit the wall, checked the clock, breathed, and pushed off for the fifth.

The first hundred meters were fine. Pull, kick, breathe, turn. Her legs felt heavy from the accumulated lactate but the heaviness was a known quantity, something the link factored into the pacing model. She had fifty meters to the wall and then the turn.

She came in. Tucked. Rotated.

The rotation was wrong.

Not wrong like a mistake — wrong like a different answer to the same question. Her body executed a tighter tuck with a half-degree axis shift that sent her deeper off the wall, her streamline longer, her dolphin kicks firing in a four-beat pattern instead of the three she'd been using since January. The push-off angle was steeper. The depth was greater. She came off the wall a full body-length deeper than she'd intended and her kicks drove her forward in a rhythm she recognized.

November. The firmware update they'd rolled back after three weeks because the deeper push-off cost too much oxygen on the back half of the eight-hundred. She'd trained with that variant and then the coaching team had removed it — or she'd thought they'd removed it. Her link had stored it. And now, without any conscious input from Lena Morozova, her link had selected it.

She broke the surface and stopped. Just stopped. Her legs went still. Her arms hung at her sides. She floated in the center of the lane at the halfway point, the set unfinished, the clock still counting on the wall display. The water moved around her the way water does when the body inside it gives up — small currents from Dara's wake in the next lane, the pool's recirculation pushing against her calves, the lane rope swaying with displaced turbulence.

She treaded water. Her heart rate was elevated from the set, not from the stop. The stop had been quiet. She had simply ceased to swim, the way you'd take your hands off a steering wheel. The sound was different now — her own breathing echoing in the high-ceilinged space, the slap of small waves against the gutter, the muffled churn of Dara's stroke two lanes over. Under the water, during the set, all of that compressed into rhythm. Above it, still, the pool was just a room with too much echo and the taste of chlorine on the back of her tongue.

Dara surfaced at the far wall, touched, and looked down the lane. She had her goggles up on her forehead, water streaming down her face. She waited for Lena to start again, and when Lena didn't, she pushed off and swam back.

"You okay?"

"Yes," Lena said. "Cramp."

"Calf or foot?"

"Calf. Left side. It's already loosening."

Dara nodded. She hung on the lane rope with one arm, her body still in the water, the cap pulled low and dark against her pale skin. "I had one two weeks ago during threshold work. Left calf, same thing. Yusuf said it was a hydration flag — the system sent me a fluid reminder every forty minutes for the rest of the day." She said this evenly, with genuine attention, the information delivered as information. Nothing in her face suggested that it was strange for a body's hydration schedule to be managed by a system that sent reminders like a phone calendar.

"Do you want me to get someone?"

"No, it's fine. Thank you."

"I'm going to do the last three," Dara said. She pulled the goggles back down, adjusted the left strap one more time with her thumb, and pushed off the wall.

Lena watched her go. Dara's stroke was long and clean — the kind of efficient that looked effortless because the effort had been engineered out of it years ago. The Bergen program was different from Meridian's, different calibration philosophy, but the result was similar: a body moving through water as though the water agreed to it.

Lena swam to the wall and got out. Her legs were fine. There was no cramp and there had never been a cramp. There was a flip turn that her body had executed from a pattern her link had chosen without her, and a second and a half of swimming in which the person inside Lena Morozova had not been the one swimming.

She picked up her water bottle, her pull buoy, and walked to the cool-down lane at the far end of the deck, past the observation window where the coaching staff sometimes watched morning sessions. The glass was dark this early — Margot wouldn't be in until eight. Lena got in and swam four hundred meters easy, and the four hundred meters were hers, every stroke a decision she could feel herself making, which was not something she usually noticed because it had never, until this morning, been something she needed to check. The water felt the same as it always did. That was the problem. The water during the flip turn had felt the same too.

---

The neural coaching suite was next to the pool — a small, windowless room with two reclining stations, monitoring displays along the far wall, and the faint residual chlorine that clung to everyone who walked in from the deck. Full fluorescent lighting. A clock. A coat rack with nothing on it. The low hum of processing hardware behind the display panels.

Margot Voss was already seated at the coaching station, scrolling through morning telemetry on the main screen. She had a paper coffee cup balanced on the arm of her chair and her feed interface on her right wrist — the flat silver band she used to connect to athletes during live coaching sessions. It was dark now. Reviews were recorded data, not live feed. Margot had one shoe off under the station, her stockinged foot hooked on the chair's lower rung, the posture of someone who spent most of her working mornings in this room.

"Morning," Margot said without turning around. "Set feel good?"

"First four, yes. I stopped the fifth."

"I saw. The cramp." She pulled up the session log. "Sit down."

Lena sat in the athlete's station. The chair was shaped for reclining, calibrated for a body that would lie still while the coaching system read its output. She didn't plug in for reviews — the data came from the link's recorded log, not a live connection.

"Show me the fifth repeat," Lena said.

Margot brought it up. Two data streams ran in parallel across the display: Lena's conscious motor commands on the left panel, the neural link's execution log on the right. For the first hundred meters, the streams tracked closely — deviations in the hundredths, the kind of noise that showed up in any integrated system. Margot's hand moved across the display, scrubbing through the data the way a sound engineer scrubs through a waveform, looking for the moment it went wrong.

At the flip turn, the streams diverged.

The left panel showed Lena's intended rotation: the standard tuck-and-push, thirty-eight-degree axis, shallow push-off, early breakout. The right panel showed the variant. Tighter tuck, deeper angle, four-beat dolphin kick. The execution log had fired a stored pattern from the November firmware — a rotation model Lena's coaching team had tested and discarded weeks ago.

The divergence window lasted 1.3 seconds.

"There," Lena said.

"Yeah, I see it." Margot highlighted the window. The gap between the two streams was visible on the display like two pencil lines that should have been one, splitting apart at the wall and not rejoining until after the breakout. "Your link pulled a stored optimization variant. It happens — the engine runs background comparisons during sets and sometimes it tests a model before you've selected it. The link found the November rotation scored highest in its current optimization cycle and defaulted to it."

"I didn't select it."

"I know. That's what I'm saying — the link ran the comparison and made the call. It's within acceptable drift parameters." Margot took a sip of her coffee. "This is what the optimization engine does. It's looking for the fastest version of every movement you make, all the time, and occasionally it gets ahead of your conscious command cycle."

"It felt different," Lena said.

"Different how?"

Lena considered. She wanted to be precise. "Like I was watching from slightly behind. Like the turn had already started by the time I was there to do it."

"That's consistent with the timing gap. The link executed one-point-three seconds ahead of your conscious motor command, so you experienced the turn as already in progress when your awareness caught up. The feeling of watching from behind is just the latency. It's not — you weren't displaced. You were there the whole time, just running a beat after the link's execution."

Margot said this the way she said most technical things: plainly, without cushioning, with a certainty that came from years of reading feed data and telemetry charts. She set the coffee down and pulled up a six-week comparison of Lena's drift parameters. Lines on a graph, data points, a range window shaded in pale green.

"Look. Your deviation window's been between point-eight and one-point-five for the entire training block. Today you hit one-point-three. That's mid-range. If it starts pushing past two seconds consistently, we'd flag it for recalibration. Right now this is your system doing what it's designed to do, and you noticed it more than usual." She glanced over. "Some days you notice more than others. That's normal."

Lena looked at the chart. The numbers were right. The range was stable. The explanation was technically complete. Margot was a good coach. She read data clearly and didn't condescend, and when she told you something was within parameters, she meant it.

But the flip turn had not felt like a timing gap. The turn had felt like her body already moving in a direction she had not chosen, the tuck completed before she arrived to do it, her legs pushing off a wall she hadn't told them to push off from. The water had been carrying her in a shape she recognized but hadn't selected — like hearing a song she knew playing from a room she hadn't entered.

She didn't say this. It was not the kind of thing that would show up on the telemetry display.

"Anything else feel off this morning?" Margot asked, closing the chart.

"No."

"Good. Times are solid for the four-hundred. Pace structure's where we want it. I'd hold set volume this week, maybe add a threshold set Friday." Margot paused. "If you want, I'll flag the turn for Yusuf. He can look at the optimization engine during your next calibration."

"Yes, that's fine. Flag it."

"Done." Margot made a note on the display. "Get some food. You're running low on the protein numbers."

Lena stood. Her hair was still damp from the pool, chlorine faint in it the way it always was — in her hair, in the creases of her hands, between her fingers. She'd been swimming in chlorinated water for eighteen years. The smell was not something she noticed anymore, except in moments like this, leaving a room, when it came off her skin and she caught it in the air like a trace of somewhere she'd just been.

---

Her quarters were on the third floor of the residential wing: single bed, desk, bathroom, a window that faced the mountain ridge. The wall-mounted diagnostic display showed her enhancement status in soft green light — neural link connectivity, system load, calibration schedule, a ticker of biomarkers that scrolled slowly and meant nothing to her unless Yusuf pointed at a specific value and said this one's running hot, we'll adjust it Tuesday.

She showered. The water was different from the pool — hotter, softer, no chlorine bite — and she stood under it longer than she needed to because the heat loosened her shoulders. She dressed. She went down to the cafeteria and ate dinner: pasta with chicken, a salad she moved around the plate without finishing, a glass of water. Other athletes were there — a table of sprinters comparing session notes, some of the gymnasts, two of the decathletes whose names she knew from briefing sheets but not from conversation. She ate alone, at a two-person table near the windows. Not by choice exactly and not by design. She simply hadn't sat down next to anyone, and no one had sat down next to her, and eighteen years of institutional dining had taught her that this was ordinary, this was how evenings went sometimes — you ate and you went back to your room.

She was in her quarters by seven. Outside the window, the mountains were dark shapes against a sky that still held a trace of light at the western ridgeline. February in the Bernese Alps. The sun dropped behind the peaks at four-thirty and didn't come back for fourteen hours. The facility's exterior floods lit the snow on the nearest slope in flat white, and beyond that was darkness and the suggestion of geography too large to see.

She sat on the bed with a book. She'd been reading it for two weeks — a novel in German, a recommendation from a girl named Anne she'd gone to school with in Zug. The school attached to the Meridian developmental facility, where the students were program athletes and the academic calendar was built around training blocks. Anne had stopped competing at sixteen and gone to university in Bern. They traded messages every few months. The book was about a woman who inherits a house in the Jura mountains and finds it full of her dead aunt's correspondence — letters, postcards, train tickets, decades of someone else's filed-away life. Lena had been reading it slowly, not because the German was difficult but because the story moved at the pace of someone sorting through other people's things, and that pace required a kind of patience she had to work at.

She opened to page 147. The chapter where the woman discovers letters from a stranger. The first paragraph described the woman sitting at her dead aunt's desk, sorting through envelopes, afternoon light coming through the window at an angle that made the dust visible in the air. Schreibtisch. Umschläge. Licht. Staub.

Lena read the paragraph.

She read it again.

The words were there — German words she understood, her eyes tracking left to right along the lines. Schreibtisch. Desk. Umschläge. Envelopes. Licht. Light. The words sat in her vision without attaching to anything behind them. She could see the shapes of them, could identify each one individually, but they wouldn't assemble. She read the paragraph a third time and could not have said what it contained.

She was not distracted. She was not replaying the flip turn, or the telemetry, or Margot's voice saying acceptable parameters. She was not anxious. She was not thinking about anything at all. There was a place where her attention should have been and the place was empty — not dark, not uncomfortable, not present in any way she could describe. A room with no one in it. A sentence without a subject.

She sat on the bed with the book open on her lap, page 147, the diagnostic display glowing green on the wall, the dark window, the dark mountains. The radiator under the window ticked once and then was quiet. Her hands rested on the edges of the book, thumbs on the page margins, holding it open as though she were about to turn to the next page. She didn't turn the page. She sat there.

Twenty minutes passed.

She knew this because the display clock read 19:23 when she'd opened the book and 19:44 when she looked at it again. Twenty-one minutes. She had been sitting in this room for twenty-one minutes and could not account for any of them.

It was not like falling asleep. She hadn't slept — her body was in the same position, the book open to the same page, her eyes open, the room unchanged around her. The radiator hadn't ticked again, or it had and she hadn't heard it. It was not like daydreaming, where the mind drifts and you can usually reconstruct the chain of association that carried you away. There was no chain. There was nothing. Twenty-one minutes of her life had passed and she had not been present for them, and her body had been here the whole time, sitting on the bed, being a body, while whoever usually inhabited it was somewhere else — or nowhere.

She put the book down on the mattress, face open, the spine cracking softly. She reached behind her head and touched the neural link port at the base of her skull. A small smooth disc, two centimeters, warm from the skin around it. It was always warm. She pressed her fingertip against it gently, the way you'd test a bruise. The port had been installed in the Zug facility when she was four, one medical appointment among a hundred that year, and it had been part of her skull longer than most of the memories she carried inside it.

She went to the bathroom. Ran cold water and splashed her face. In the mirror: herself. The same face she'd always had — brown eyes, the faint mark on her left temple from a fall on the pool deck when she was twelve, her jaw set in the expression she wore when she was deciding whether something was a problem or not. She looked fine. She looked like a twenty-two-year-old swimmer at the end of a training day, tired in the ordinary way.

She went back to the bed. Picked up the book. Found her place. The woman at the desk, the envelopes, the light, the dust.

The words connected this time. The woman found a letter from a man named Aurel, and the letter was about train schedules — the 7:14 from Delémont to Basel, a delay at Laufen, an apology for arriving late to something that wasn't specified. The woman didn't understand why her dead aunt had kept it. Neither did Lena, but the not-understanding was pleasant and ordinary, the kind of small question a novel sets up so you carry it forward to the next page.

She read three more pages. Her comprehension was normal. She was in the room, in the bed, in the book, present the way presence usually worked — so automatically you didn't notice it happening.

She marked her page and turned off the bedside lamp. The diagnostic display shifted to night mode: dimmer, the ticker slowing to a pulse, the green light throwing a faint shifting pattern on the ceiling as the data refreshed. She lay in the dark and looked at it and tried to account for the twenty-one minutes.

Not what she'd been thinking. She hadn't been thinking.

Not what she'd felt. She hadn't felt.

The flip turn was data. A 1.3-second deviation window. A number on Margot's chart. A thing with an explanation and a range and a green shaded zone that meant acceptable. She could tell Yusuf about it, and Yusuf would look at the optimization engine, and the number would stay where it was or it wouldn't, and either way there would be a chart.

The twenty-one minutes were not data. There was no telemetry for an absence. There was no screen she could point to and say here, this is where I wasn't. She'd told Dara it was a cramp. She'd told Margot nothing, because there was nothing on the display to show her.

The wind moved against the building. The display pulsed green on the ceiling, refreshing, refreshing, a system that was always awake even when she wasn't watching.

Sleep came in the usual way — the gradual loosening, her thoughts thinning, her body settling into the mattress. She wouldn't have been able to say exactly when it arrived, which was how sleep was supposed to work: a transition you didn't notice because you weren't there for it.

She'd already had one of those today, and she'd been awake.


# Chapter 3

The calibration bay ran at a constant seventeen degrees Celsius, two degrees below facility standard. Not for the athletes' benefit — for the equipment. The spectral analyzers drifted at higher temperatures, and Yusuf had fought Building Services for three months to get dedicated climate control installed. He'd won. The bay was cold, the analyzers were stable, and he wore a fleece under his lab coat from October to April.

He arrived at six forty-five, same time every morning. The processing stacks were already running — they never stopped, just cycled between active calibration and standby analysis — and their hum was the sound his working life was built on. A low, constant sixty hertz, just below the threshold where most people consciously registered it. He'd stopped hearing it during his first week at the Kirchberg Institute, three years ago. Now it was the sound of the room being awake.

He set his coffee on the workstation ledge — the same chipped mug, Imperial College crest half-gone — and opened the day's schedule. Six athletes before noon. Four after lunch, then a block for data review and reporting. His official reporting. The other kind happened in the gaps.

The first athlete at seven was Anya Petrov, a javelin specialist from the Russian program. Standard monthly recalibration — her right shoulder graft had been stable for two seasons, but the altitude affected the synthetic muscle's oxygen transfer rates and needed periodic adjustment. She came in already stretching, one earbud still in, pulled it out and dropped it in her lap as she settled into the calibration rig. The rig looked like a dentist's chair designed by people who found dentists insufficiently invasive: padded recline, articulated arm supports, a curved overhead array of sensor plates that read thermal output, electromagnetic fluctuation, and nerve-firing patterns through the skin.

"Morning, Anya. How's the shoulder?"

"Good. A little tight after yesterday's throws."

"Scale of one to ten."

"Three. Maybe four when I reach overhead."

Yusuf pulled up her integration profile on the workstation. The graft's performance envelope scrolled across the display in green and amber bands. "Right, I'm going to run a tension sweep first. You'll feel pressure cycling through the deltoid area, like someone kneading it. About ninety seconds. Try to stay relaxed."

He initiated the sweep. Through the sensor array, the graft responded — synthetic muscle fibers adjusting their resting tension as the calibration signal passed through them. The process was intimate in a way he'd never quite gotten used to. He was inside someone's body, reading signals that the person couldn't feel, making adjustments that would change how their shoulder moved for the next month. His hands were steady on the interface — they always were, the one part of him that never registered what was happening as strange. The athletes lay still and trusted him with this. They had to. There was no other way.

Anya's shoulder responded normally. He adjusted the oxygen transfer parameters for altitude, logged the calibration values, and she was done by seven twenty.

"Feels good," she said, rolling the shoulder experimentally. "Looser."

"Should hold for four weeks. If the tightness comes back before then, flag it."

She left, earbud already back in. Yusuf returned to the workstation and opened a second window — one that didn't connect to the Kirchberg reporting server — and copied the full raw data stream from Anya's shoulder scan. The official log recorded the calibration values and the adjustment parameters. The raw stream contained everything the hardware had seen: nerve-firing patterns, microvascular flow, hormone concentration at the graft site, the complete biological signature of the tissue surrounding the synthetic muscle. A thirty-second calibration produced more biological data than a hospital stay.

He saved the raw stream, minimized the second window, and pulled up the next appointment.

Tomás Reyes arrived at seven thirty. Twenty-one, Ecuadorian, first Apex cycle — a decathlete whose enhancement package was only three years integrated. He sat in the rig and looked up at the overhead sensor array the way most athletes had stopped doing years ago: with genuine curiosity, as if the equipment were something worth seeing rather than something to tolerate.

"How's the integration feeling?"

"Good. Yeah. I think." Reyes flexed his right hand, opened and closed it. "My grip strength's been weird in the mornings. Like the hand's already awake before I am."

Yusuf pulled up his profile. Right forearm graft, bilateral calf integration, spinal bridge — standard decathlon build. The grip issue could be residual calibration drift from altitude adjustment, or it could be the hand's autonomous subsystem running its morning diagnostic cycle at a threshold Reyes could feel. "How long has that been happening?"

"Maybe a week? I wasn't sure if it was, like, normal."

"Could be altitude. Let me take a look." Yusuf initiated the hand's integration scan. Through the display, the graft's synthetic fibers lit up in cross-section — a dense weave of artificial muscle overlaying the natural forearm musculature, filaments fine as suture thread anchored to tendons and the radial bone. The resting tension was slightly elevated. Not alarming. Probably altitude. "I'm going to drop your forearm's resting tension about two percent. You'll feel the hand relax a bit. Might feel weak for an hour while it recalibrates."

"Okay." Reyes settled back. Trusting, in the specific way young athletes trusted their support teams — not from evaluation of evidence, but because the alternative was unmanageable. You didn't question the person adjusting your nervous system while your nervous system was in their hands.

Yusuf made the adjustment. The tension scan normalized. He logged the calibration, noted the grip complaint and the altitude-probable cause in the official file, and Reyes was done by seven fifty-five.

"Thanks, doc."

"Not a doctor. But you're welcome."

Reyes left. Yusuf pulled up his raw data — not the calibration summary he'd logged, but the full integration stream. The resting tension elevation wasn't just the forearm. The calf grafts showed a similar pattern: a low-grade increase in autonomous activity that the standard calibration smoothed away without comment. He copied it to the second window.

The morning continued. A Norwegian cross-country skier with a hip graft that needed seasonal adjustment. A Chinese diver whose visual processing enhancement was producing a fractional depth-perception offset — a known altitude artifact, easily corrected. A French sprinter with stable readings across the board, in and out in twelve minutes. Between each session Yusuf returned to the second window. The raw data accumulated. Not the calibrated, cleaned summaries that went to the Kirchberg servers, but the unprocessed biological output — heart-rate variability, nerve-firing latency, hormone concentrations, the full spectrum of information that the calibration hardware recorded and the official reports reduced to a handful of approved metrics.

He was not doing anything that the system prevented. The raw data existed on the facility's servers and his workstation access was unrestricted. The prohibition was institutional rather than technical: his role was to calibrate, report calibration values, and flag anomalies for the medical team. He was not authorized to retain raw biometric data for analysis outside his calibration duties. The difference between what the system allowed and what the institution permitted was the gap he worked in. He'd been working in it for eleven months.

After lunch — a cheese sandwich eaten at the workstation, crumbs on the keyboard he pushed into the gap between the keys — he pulled up Kai Brennan's full dataset from the previous evening's calibration. Not just the proprioception drift. The complete integration profile: motor nerve latency across both legs, spinal bridge throughput, visual processing load, and the overnight drift data that had flagged that morning. The proprioception gap — the system initiating micro-corrections ahead of Brennan's conscious intent — hadn't settled after calibration. It had continued, a slow creep in the predictive-to-conscious timing, as if the system were learning to stay ahead of him rather than falling back into alignment.

Yusuf cross-referenced it with anomalies he'd been tracking for the past three weeks. Reyes's forearm and calf elevation. The Norwegian skier's hip graft, which showed the same pattern of slightly elevated autonomous activity he'd attributed to seasonal adjustment. A Brazilian hurdler whose sensory integration had produced a single two-second latency spike during a training session six days ago — flagged by the overnight logs, reviewed, attributed to a momentary processing load peak, filed.

Four athletes. Four different enhancement packages, different sports, different integration histories. But all on the same calibration network. And the pattern, when he laid the raw data side by side, was legible: a correlated increase in autonomous system activity, simultaneous within a forty-eight-hour window, across hardware that had no functional reason to synchronize.

He pushed his glasses up his nose — they'd slid, as they did every afternoon when the fleece warmed and the bridge of his nose got damp — and looked at the display. The fluorescent light above the workstation buzzed at a frequency slightly off from the processing stacks' hum, producing a thin interference pattern he'd long ago stopped noticing.

The correlation wasn't conclusive. Four data points wasn't a trend; it was a suggestion. The shared calibration network was designed to propagate optimized parameters across connected systems — that was its purpose. Correlated behavior wasn't inherently anomalous. He'd need a wider sample, a longer time window, and a controlled comparison with athletes on different networks to determine whether the drift was a genuine systemic issue or a seasonal artifact that altitude and temperature were exaggerating.

But it looked wrong. Not in a way he could articulate in an official report — not yet, not with four points and a sensation in his chest that wouldn't pass for data — but wrong in the way an engineer recognized before the numbers confirmed it. The pattern was too clean. Autonomous activity didn't synchronize across different hardware architectures unless something was coordinating it.

He wrote the afternoon's official reports. Brennan: proprioception drift noted, continued monitoring recommended, within acceptable parameters. The Norwegian: hip adjustment completed, seasonal norms. Reyes: grip tension corrected, altitude probable. The Brazilian wasn't his patient today, but he reviewed the filed report from last week and confirmed the latency spike had been logged as a momentary processing artifact.

Everything in order. Everything filed. Everything technically accurate and institutionally complete.

He saved the cross-referenced correlation analysis to the second window and closed it.

---

The calibration bay emptied by six in the evening. The last athlete — a German middle-distance runner, standard monthly — left at five forty, and Yusuf spent twenty minutes on shutdown protocols. Not a full shutdown; the processing stacks ran continuously. But the active hardware needed to be powered down, the sensor arrays cleaned and returned to standby. He did this himself. His two junior engineers, Sara and Luc, handled morning setup. Yusuf handled evening close. He preferred the bay empty. He preferred the quiet that wasn't quiet — the sixty-hertz hum, the faint click of cooling systems cycling, the sound of a room that was working but not watched.

When the rigs were down, he retrieved his personal device from his bag. A data drive smaller than his thumb, matte black, encrypted with a key that existed only in his memory and one physical backup in a drawer in his quarters. He'd bought it in Zurich two years ago, from a specialist electronics shop in the Niederdorf that served researchers and paranoiacs in equal measure. The encryption was standard academic-grade — nothing exotic, nothing that would draw attention if the device were found, but enough to make the data inaccessible without the key.

He plugged it into the workstation's auxiliary port. The second window opened. The day's collection: Brennan's proprioception stream, Reyes's grip and calf data, the Norwegian's hip integration values, the full afternoon's raw biometric pulls, and the cross-referenced correlation analysis. He transferred the files. The progress bar moved quickly — biological signals compressed well. Eleven seconds. He disconnected the drive, dropped it into his trouser pocket, and closed the second window.

The workstation's display showed only the Kirchberg reporting interface. The official logs. The filed reports. The clean and complete records of a thorough engineer's working day.

He turned off the monitor and left.

The connecting corridor had glass walls on the mountain side, and at this hour, in late January, the mountains were a dark mass against a slightly less dark sky. No moon. The exterior temperature display by the corridor junction read minus twelve. Inside, the facility's heating system held a steady twenty-one degrees, and the transition from the calibration bay's seventeen to the corridor's warmth was the only temperature change Yusuf experienced most days. He didn't go outside often. The cold didn't bother him but the mountains did, in a way he'd never examined — too large, too indifferent, a landscape that was the opposite of Birmingham in every dimension and reminded him of it by sheer contrast.

His quarters were on the third floor of the residential wing. A single room, larger than the athletes' rooms — a staff privilege he'd never requested and didn't refuse. Bed, desk, bathroom. The wall-mounted diagnostic display that glowed faintly with facility status data he'd stopped reading months ago. A kettle, a jar of loose-leaf Yorkshire Tea his mother posted monthly, and a stack of books on the desk that he was getting through at roughly a chapter a week, which meant he was three months behind on a history of the Birmingham canal network that Farah had given him for his birthday.

He made tea. Dropped the data drive into the desk drawer next to its twin — the backup, loaded weekly, same encryption, same matte black casing. Then he sat down and opened his laptop and dialed Farah.

She picked up on the third ring. Her face filled the screen — dark hair pulled back, the living room of the flat in Moseley behind her, the corner of the bookshelf he'd helped her assemble two Christmases ago visible over her left shoulder. She was smiling.

"Yu! Hang on, let me —" She adjusted something off-screen. The camera settled. "There. Hi."

"Hey. How's your day been?"

"Fine. Normal. Went to Asda, made a lasagne. It was terrible but I ate all of it, so." She shrugged. Her face was animated, present. She looked good. She always looked good on the screen — the compression hid the small things.

"Did you use Mum's recipe or the one from that website?"

"The website. Mum's has too many steps. I can't be arsed with bechamel from scratch when there's a perfectly good jar."

"She'd disown you."

"She'd disown me and then bring round a proper one. Win-win." Farah grinned. There was a pause — half a beat longer than the grin warranted — before she continued. "So what are you up to? Still in the lab at —" She checked something, probably the time. "Seven at night?"

"Just finished up. Long day."

"They're all long days with you. You need to get out more. Go look at a mountain or something. You're in the Alps and you spend all your time in a room with no windows."

"There's a window in the corridor."

"Yu."

"I'm fine. I like the work." He took a sip of tea. The screen showed Farah's expression — fond irritation, a familiar arrangement. He watched for what he always watched for: the speed of her responses, the fluidity of her transitions, the gap between thought and word. She was good tonight. Animated. But there was something in the rhythm. A fractional delay, not in the connection — he'd long ago learned the difference between network lag and the other kind — but in her. The sentences arrived at the same brightness, the same pace, but each one took slightly longer to begin. Like a stride that looked the same to a spectator but whose foot hit the ground later.

"How's the physio going?" he asked.

"Oh, fine. Same. She's dead enthusiastic. Lots of squeezing things." Farah held up her hands and mimed squeezing something invisible. "I told her I don't really need more grip strength, I need to be able to open the —" She stopped. Her expression didn't change — the smile held — but there was a gap where a word should have been. "Jars," she said, and the smile widened slightly, performing the recovery. "I need to be able to open jars. The pasta sauce specifically. The Tesco one. Whoever decided to make those lids that tight is my actual enemy."

"I'll send you one of those rubber grip things."

"I've got three. They don't work on the Tesco ones. I've tried. I think they're sealed at a molecular level."

She was past it, the lost word already behind her. But Yusuf had heard the gap. A fraction of a second where "jars" — a word she'd used ten thousand times, a word with no complexity, no retrieval difficulty — had not been there when she reached for it.

Voss-Kessler worked like that. Not the dramatic losses first — not memory, not cognition, not the things that would be immediately alarming. The deterioration began with the small retrieval tasks, the frictionless ones that a healthy brain performed without conscious effort. Word-finding in casual speech. The timing of a sentence. Fine motor adjustments that fell below the threshold of awareness. By the time the losses were visible to anyone who wasn't specifically looking, the progression had been at work for months.

"Mum called today," Farah said. "She's worried about you. Says you're not eating properly."

"How would she know?"

"She has sources. I think she's paying one of the cafeteria staff."

"She's not paying anyone. She doesn't even know where the cafeteria is."

"Well, she's worried. Eat a vegetable, Yu. One vegetable. For her peace of mind."

"I had a sandwich today."

"What was in it?"

"Cheese."

"That's not a vegetable."

"There was lettuce."

"Iceberg doesn't count. It's basically water in leaf form."

He laughed. She laughed. The sound was the same as it had always been — bright, a little too loud for the flat's thin walls. But underneath it, or alongside it, in a register that only someone calibrated to hear it would catch, there was the delay. The half-second gap before each response that hadn't been there six months ago. The word that vanished mid-sentence and was recovered so smoothly that anyone else would have marked it as a normal fumble. The slightly slower execution of the joke's delivery — not the wit, which was intact, possibly sharpening in compensation, but the motor production of the words themselves.

He could track it. He could not fix it.

"You still there?" Farah asked.

"Yeah. Sorry. Thinking."

"About work?"

"About what I'm having for dinner."

"Cheese sandwich?"

"Probably."

"Hopeless." She yawned, and the yawn lasted longer than it should have — her jaw moving with a looseness that would have been invisible on a lower-resolution call but was legible on his screen, to his eyes. "I should go. I've got that thing with Priya tomorrow, early."

"What thing?"

"We're going to the —" She paused. Not the brief pause of thinking. The pause of reaching for something that should have been within arm's length and finding the shelf empty. "Cadbury World." She found it. The path to finding it had been visible. "She's never been. I've been four times and it's still good. They give you free chocolate at the end."

"Tell Priya I say hi."

"Will do. Eat a vegetable."

"Night, Farah."

"Night, Yu."

The screen went dark. Yusuf sat at his desk with the tea cooling in his hands. The wall display cast its faint blue glow across the room. The heating system droned through the walls, the building breathing.

Farah was eight months past diagnosis. Her neurologist in Birmingham, Dr. Adeyemi, had described the typical progression: speech processing and fine motor first, then gross motor, then cognitive. Farah had stopped her there. She didn't want the full timeline. She wanted to know what she could do now, what she'd need help with, and when. Practical questions. She had always been practical.

Voss-Kessler syndrome had no treatment. The condition was rare — perhaps six hundred people worldwide — which meant it attracted neither pharmaceutical investment nor the large-scale clinical attention that produced breakthroughs. The most promising research, published fourteen months ago in a neurology journal Yusuf now read cover to cover, suggested that high-resolution neural mapping data from enhanced-human systems could illuminate the degenerative pathway well enough to design targeted interventions. The authors had proposed a collaborative data-sharing initiative. Meridian BioSystems, which controlled the vast majority of enhancement-generated biological data worldwide, had not responded.

The data existed. It was generated every day in the room Yusuf worked in, by every athlete who lay in his calibration rig and trusted him with their systems. Neural mapping at a resolution no hospital scanner could match, because the enhancement hardware was already inside the body, already reading the signals at the source. Months of it. An archive growing daily in the drawer of his desk, in a resolution that Farah's neurologist could only dream of accessing.

It was not available through any channel he'd tried. He had written to Meridian's research liaison office — a careful, professional inquiry about data-sharing protocols for academic research partnerships. The response, eleven weeks later, had been a two-paragraph form letter directing him to Meridian's commercial licensing division, where the minimum data access agreement started at figures that assumed you were a pharmaceutical company, not an engineer with a sister.

So Yusuf took it.

He set the mug down. Opened the laptop and navigated to his analysis folder — not the raw biometric archive on the drives, but the work he did with it. The cross-referenced correlation from today sat at the top: four athletes, simultaneous autonomous drift, no functional explanation. He could report it. Flag the pattern, submit it to Kirchberg's data review board, let the institutional process evaluate it. That was the correct procedure. That was what his contract, his professional obligations, and his training all required as the appropriate response to an unexplained pattern in safety-critical biological systems.

He would need to explain the cross-referencing. He would need to explain why he'd retained raw data outside his reporting scope. He would need to explain the second window and the eleven months of files in his desk drawer and the encrypted drives that held the biological lives of eighty-three athletes in more detail than those athletes knew about themselves.

He closed the laptop. The blue glow from the wall display settled on the ceiling. The building hummed around him, and outside the window the mountains were nothing, just a darkness where the sky stopped, and he sat there for a long time before he turned off the light.


# Chapter 4

The coaching suite smelled like adhesive tape and the faint metallic discharge of processing hardware running warm. Margot arrived fifteen minutes before Tomás's session, which was ten minutes early. She told herself this was professionalism — checking the monitoring displays, running a diagnostic on the feed interface, reviewing his training targets for the day's hurdle and four-hundred-meter blocks. All of that was true. She did do those things.

But she also sat in the coaching rig with the wrist mount already docked, waiting for the signal handshake that wouldn't begin until Tomás arrived and connected on his end, and she watched the green ready-light blink its slow patient rhythm, and her mouth was dry.

The suite was a small room adjacent to the indoor track, separated from the training floor by a glass observation window. Three monitors on the wall showed biometric grids waiting for live data. The chair was padded, adjustable, designed for sessions that could last two hours. It was the most comfortable chair in the Kirchberg Institute, which was a detail nobody except Margot would ever notice or care about.

Tomás came through the door at 9:02, already in his training kit, carrying his hurdle spikes in one hand and a water bottle in the other. The spikes were custom-fitted, Meridian-issued, and he carried them the way Margot had seen other first-Games athletes carry expensive equipment — careful, slightly amazed, like a kid with a birthday present he wasn't sure he deserved. He was smiling. Tomás was always smiling.

"Morning, Coach."

"Morning. You eat?"

"Yeah, eggs and that quinoa stuff from the cafeteria. It's not — how do you say — it's not bad, actually. My mom makes better, but." He shrugged. "She makes better than everything, so."

"Sit down, let me check your calibration readings before we start."

He sat in the athlete's station across from her and she plugged the auxiliary cable from her interface into the port on the back of his training vest. The handshake began: his system and her feed device negotiating data access, confirming neural channel permissions, establishing the one-way sensory pipeline that would let her feel what he felt. The monitors populated with his resting biometrics. Heart rate sixty-two. Core temperature thirty-seven point one. Enhancement subsystem status: all green.

"How's the shoulder?" she asked. He'd bruised his left deltoid two days ago in a shot-put session — a training weight that had shifted in his grip at release, the kind of minor thing that happened when you were learning to integrate three-year-old synthetic grafts with a throwing motion the grafts hadn't been optimized for.

"Good. Little sore when I raise it past here." He demonstrated, lifting his arm to about a hundred thirty degrees. "But it doesn't catch. Just, you know. It aches."

"Don't baby it on the hurdles. Your trail arm needs full extension or your hip rotates and you clear the bar like you're climbing over a fence."

He laughed. "Got it."

"I'm serious. I watched your last session tape. Your trail leg is fine but that arm drops three centimeters on the left side."

"Three centimeters? That's nothing."

"It's three centimeters that turns into a tenth of a second that turns into sixth place. You didn't come here for sixth place."

"No." The smile changed — still there but sharper underneath. Tomás was twenty-one and eager and came from a family in Quito that needed him to be good, and sometimes the need showed through the good nature like bone through skin. "I didn't."

"Ready?"

"Ready."

She initiated the feed.

The world doubled. Her own body stayed in the chair — she could feel the padding against her lower back, her feet on the floor, the suite's controlled twenty-one degrees against her forearms — but layered over it, arriving through the neural feed like a second skin pulled on while the first was still wet, was Tomás's body. His hamstrings, loose and warm from the pre-session stretching. His right quadriceps, where the synthetic muscle graft met natural tissue and the integration seam produced a faint thermal gradient she could feel as a line of cooler pressure running from mid-thigh to the lateral side of the knee. His breathing, deep and regular, the rib cage expanding in a rhythm that pushed against her own breathing like a hand on her sternum. His heart, at sixty-two, the contraction arriving in her chest as a ghost beat that didn't match her own.

He stood. She felt the standing — the load shift through his feet, the engagement of his calves, his core stabilizing around the spinal column and the grafted musculature pulling everything into alignment the way a well-tuned suspension settles under weight. The sensation of his body was not the same as feeling her body. It was translated, slightly delayed, a borrowed proprioception that arrived through the feed's sensory mapping rather than through her own nerves. But it was enough. It was more than enough.

He walked to the indoor track and she watched him through the observation window and felt him through the feed at the same time, the two channels of information overlapping like a conversation where both people talked simultaneously and you somehow followed both. He set up the hurdles. Ten hurdles, standard spacing for the 110m, regulation height because Tomás always trained at competition spec. He bent to adjust the third one and she felt the stretch through his lower back, the way his hamstrings resisted for a half-second before the graft-enhanced tissue released, elastic and massive.

"Pacing targets loaded," she said into the headset mic. "First set, seventy percent effort. I want clean technique, don't worry about the clock."

"Got it."

He positioned himself in the blocks. His heart rate climbed to seventy-eight — anticipatory, normal. Through the feed she felt the balls of his feet pressing into the block pads, the coiled readiness in his quads, the way his enhanced visual system sharpened focus on the first hurdle forty-five meters away. The rubber track surface resolved in his vision into individual granules of texture, and she experienced the resolution as a brightening, a crispness in her own visual field that wasn't real but felt real, the feed translating his sensory enhancement into a version her unenhanced brain could parse.

He pushed out of the blocks.

The acceleration was a compression behind her sternum — not her body's, his, but the feed mapped it onto hers, and for the first three strides she was in two places at once, sitting in a chair and driving forward on synthetic-enhanced legs, the track surface hammering up through feet that were not her feet. His hamstrings loaded with each stride, elastic and enormous, and the first hurdle came up fast. His lead leg drove forward — she felt the quadriceps fire, the hip flexor engage, the brief extraordinary moment where both feet left the ground and his body traveled over the bar in a tight controlled arc, the air rushing past his forearms and the hurdle dropping away beneath him. Trail leg pulled through. Landing.
