The impact absorbed through his knee and ankle and the immediate reacceleration, three strides to the next hurdle, and his heart rate climbed to a hundred thirty-eight and the feed was beautiful.

That was the word she didn't use with anyone. Beautiful. The feed at full intensity — a young athlete's body working near the edge of its engineering — was the most beautiful thing Margot had experienced in years. More beautiful than her own swimming had ever been, and she'd had good days in the pool, days when the stroke was right and the water felt like it was cooperating. But even her best days had been limited by her own body, by the specific disappointments of Margot Voss's musculature and cardiovascular ceiling and the gap between what she wanted and what she could produce. Through the feed, there was no gap. Through the feed, she was a body that could do the thing.

Second hurdle. Third. Fourth. His form was excellent — Tomás had natural talent, three years of enhancement optimization, and the disciplined eagerness of someone who couldn't afford to waste this. His heart rate at a hundred forty-six. The pacing target she'd loaded was set for a hundred fifty peak.

She touched the adjustment on her console. Moved the target up four beats. A hundred fifty-four.

It was such a small number. She'd done this before — not every session, but enough that the pattern lived in the telemetry data if anyone bothered to look for it. A few beats above the prescribed margin during Hana Petrov's sprint conditioning last week. Same adjustment for Marcus Adler's gymnastics session the Tuesday before that. The coaching interface logged pacing changes in a sub-table that the standard medical summary reports didn't pull from. You had to query the raw session data directly. You had to go looking.

Nobody went looking. Margot knew this because she'd checked.

Fifth hurdle. His visual field sharpened as the bar approached, the enhanced optics pre-calculating clearance trajectory, and through the feed she experienced the calculation as a spatial compression, as though the air between his chest and the hurdle thickened and thinned in a rhythmic pulse timed to his stride. His heart rate hit a hundred fifty-one. Climbing. She felt the cardiovascular load through the feed as a warmth spreading from the center of her chest outward — not her warmth, his, but the feed didn't distinguish, it just delivered the sensation and let her nervous system mistake it for her own. The warmth was effort. Real effort. The zone past the enhancement system's optimization routines where the athlete's biology was working harder than the engineering had budgeted for, where the body had to produce something the system couldn't supply, and that raw surplus — that was the high. Not the efficiency. The inefficiency. The part that was Tomás, not Meridian's design.

Sixth hurdle. Clean. His breathing came harder and she felt it in her own diaphragm, the secondary respiration riding alongside her own like a harmony she couldn't stop hearing. A hundred fifty-four now. His body meeting the target she'd set, his cardiovascular system pushing past the optimization ceiling, his synthetic quads demanding more oxygen than the efficiency models allocated, and the deficit showed up as a beautiful, stupid, human strain.

She could have pulled the targets back.

Seventh hurdle. His heart rate at a hundred fifty-eight. His lead leg drove forward but the timing was off by a fraction she felt before she saw — the cardiovascular load pulling resources from his motor coordination, the fine-grained muscle timing that the enhancement system managed degrading under the strain she had built. Through the feed, it arrived as a wrongness in the spatial rhythm, the hurdle bar approaching at a slightly different angle than his body expected, and then through the observation window she watched it happen: his trail leg catching the bar at shin height. Not a glancing touch. The bar was at regulation competition height — Tomás liked full spec — and his shin struck it at an angle that torqued his knee as his momentum carried him forward and down.

He went down. Hands first, palms slapping the rubber. Then his knees. Then his right shoulder and the side of his face sliding on the track surface, the friction-burn of rubber against skin arriving through the feed as a hot scraping pressure across Margot's own cheek and shoulder. His heart rate spiked to a hundred sixty-one. His body, horizontal and not understanding why, sent a cascade of proprioceptive signals through the feed — where is the ground, where is up, why has forward stopped — and Margot sat in the coaching rig and felt all of it, the confusion of a borrowed body that she had pushed past its margins and then watched fall.

She disconnected. Her fingers found the release on the wrist mount and the doubled world collapsed to one — her body, in the chair, her own heart rate at ninety-one from the sympathetic feed response, her own palms sweating against the armrests, the observation window showing Tomás getting up off the track and examining his knee.

"Tomás. You good?"

"Yeah." His voice in the headset, breathless. "Yeah, I'm all right. Knee's a little — I think I tweaked it on the way down, but it's not bad. What happened?"

She had the answer ready. "Your pacing fell off on the last three hurdles. Cardiovascular response lagged — that's altitude. You've been here, what, twelve days? The thin air catches up once you push past seventy percent. I should've started you at sixty-five. My fault."

A small, specific, plausible lie. Altitude adjustment was the all-purpose explanation at twenty-one hundred meters, and she deployed it with the fluency of someone who'd used it before.

"Oh." He tested the knee, standing with his weight on it, shifting. Winced, but stayed upright. "So we skip the four hundred today?"

"Yeah, let's call it. I'll have the training team look at that knee. We can reschedule the four hundred for Thursday if it checks out."

"Okay." He sat on the track apron, drank from his water bottle, and pulled off his spikes with the same care he'd carried them in with. "Coach? It's weird, I felt fine until the sixth hurdle and then it was like my legs got heavy. Is that the altitude too?"

"Yeah. That's what it does. Your system's optimizing for sea-level oxygen saturation and the air up here doesn't cooperate. Takes about three weeks to fully adjust."

He nodded. "Thanks for catching it."

Margot looked at him. Twenty-one, sitting on the track apron with a tweaked knee and a rubber burn on his right cheek, thanking her for catching the thing she'd caused. His trust in her was total and unexamined and rested on the simple fact that she was his coach and coaches kept you safe. He had no reason to think otherwise. He wouldn't have a reason for months.

"Go get that looked at," she said. "And eat something. You're burning through calories at this altitude."

"Yeah, I will. See you Thursday?"

"Thursday."

He gathered his spikes and left. The facility trainer came in ten minutes later, reviewed the session summary on the monitor — the one that showed the heart-rate spike but not the manual target adjustment — and noted the altitude explanation in the incident log without comment. Minor knee sprain. Two days off hurdle work. Light conditioning permitted.

Margot sat in the rig after everyone left. The coaching suite was quiet. The monitors had dropped to standby. The green ready-light on her docked interface blinked its slow rhythm again, waiting for the next session, patient and indifferent. Her wrist, where the mount pressed against skin, was warm from the connection. The device held a residual charge for about thirty minutes after disconnection, and she could feel it — a faint pulse against the tendons of her inner wrist, like a second heartbeat winding down.

She filed her session report. Noted the heart-rate anomaly. Attributed it to altitude acclimatization, incomplete cardiovascular adjustment, and recommended a reduced intensity threshold for his next two sessions. The report was thorough, professional, and accurate except for the part that mattered.

---

Her quarters were on the third floor of the residential wing, facing the mountain. At five in the afternoon in late January the view through the window was a black ridge against a sky not much lighter, and the glass radiated cold.

She locked the door. Took off her shoes, her coaching jacket, her track pants. Stood in a t-shirt and underwear in the middle of the room. Her body felt like a house she'd come back to after staying somewhere better.

She stretched. Hamstrings, quads, hip flexors — the old competitive routine, which she'd never stopped doing even though the competition was eight years gone. The body kept its habits after the reason for them left. Her hamstrings were tight in the way they'd been tight for years now, the specific tightness of tissue that remembered what it used to be asked to do and resented the demotion. When she'd been swimming six hours a day they'd been responsive and warm, and stretching had been a conversation between her and the muscles, a negotiation before the real work. Now it was maintenance. She was maintaining a body for no particular purpose.

Thirty minutes ago she'd felt Tomás's hamstrings through the feed. Elastic, dense, engineered for the kind of explosive power her body had never been built for even at its best. Through the feed, his body had been a machine she was permitted to ride. Hers was the parking lot she came back to afterward.

She went into the bathroom. The mirror was full-length — institutional bathrooms at training facilities always had full-length mirrors, because the athletes needed to inspect their bodies the way mechanics inspected engines, and nobody had swapped the mirror for a smaller one in the coaching staff quarters. She looked at herself. Five-eight, a hundred fifty-two pounds, which was fourteen more than her competitive weight. The shoulders were still broad from the swimming years but the definition had softened into a mass that moved differently when she raised her arms. Her stomach was flat enough if she stood straight. Her thighs were thick in a way that had been functional at twenty-seven and was just mass at thirty-five.

She was being cruel to herself and she knew it. There was nothing wrong with her body. It was healthy, it worked, it carried her from room to room and held her upright and processed food and sleep and oxygen without complaint. But she worked in a building full of engineered bodies — Tomás's twenty-one-year-old decathlon build, Kai Brennan's sprinter architecture with its inhuman bilateral symmetry, the swimmers with their optimized shoulder girdles and lung capacity, all of them projects, all of them works in progress toward something their bodies were designed to become — and she was the only person in the building whose body wasn't becoming anything. Hers had arrived at its destination and the destination was this.

She sat on the edge of the bathtub. The porcelain was cold through the cotton of her underwear, and she registered the cold the way she registered everything in her own body now — as fact, without texture. Through the feed, cold had been information. Temperature differentials mapped to nerve response, the enhancement system cataloguing sensation for future optimization. Through her own skin, cold was just cold.

She pressed her thumb against the feed mount on her left wrist. The residual charge had faded to almost nothing, the faint pulse barely detectable, and the skin beneath the mount was slightly indented from the docking pressure. She pressed until the indentation whitened and began to ache.

The comedown was always the same. Not dramatic. No shaking, no sweating, nothing a medical scan would flag. Just a flatness. A subtraction. Through the feed she had been a body at full intensity — a cardiovascular system at its working limit, a visual field sharpened by optics she didn't have, hamstrings that could launch a hundred-eighty-pound body over a meter-high bar at twenty-eight kilometers per hour. And now she was back in her own nervous system, and her own nervous system offered her the cold of porcelain, the tightness of her hamstrings, and the reflection in the mirror that she could see from the bathtub's edge, half her body visible in the glass, looking like exactly what it was.

She wanted to plug in again. Not the next scheduled session — now. Walk down to the track, find whoever was still training, jack into their feed and feel anything other than this. She wanted it the way she'd wanted the wall at the end of the last lap in her swimming years, the specific desire for the race to end so the pain would stop but also for the pain to never stop because the pain was the only thing that confirmed she was in it, she was competing, she was real.

She didn't call it addiction. She called it immersive coaching methodology. She called it the approach that produced measurable results in athlete performance metrics, which it did — the athletes she worked with improved their times, their form, their competitive readiness, and if she nudged their heart rates past the prescribed margins during sessions, that was a training judgment about optimal intensity, and training judgments were her job. She was good at her job. She had the data to prove it.

The diagnostic display on the wall of her quarters showed her biometrics — not enhanced, but all Kirchberg staff wore basic monitoring patches as facility protocol. Heart rate sixty-six. Core temperature normal. Blood pressure normal. Everything normal. Thoroughly, completely, miserably normal.

She showered. The hot water was hot water. Through the feed, the hot water on Tomás's skin would have arrived as a registered thermal event — temperature differential logged, nerve response catalogued, the sensation annotated by the enhancement system for future calibration. Through her own skin, it was just warm and then it was done and she turned the tap off and stood in the steam looking at the tiles.

She got dressed. Sat at her desk. Opened her schedule for the week. Hana Petrov at seven a.m. tomorrow — sprint conditioning. Two afternoon sessions Thursday with the gymnasts. Friday, Kai Brennan had requested a neural coaching evaluation for his proprioception issues, which she'd agreed to because Kai's system was complex and the feed experience during a sprinter's acceleration was the closest thing to what she'd felt during Tomás's hurdles — that same explosive, compressive, total engagement of a body built for speed.

She closed the schedule. She wrote up the incident report for Tomás's fall. Minor sprain, right knee. Contributing factor: altitude adjustment, incomplete cardiovascular acclimatization. Recommendation: two days off hurdle work, light conditioning permitted. She filed it. It joined the other session reports in the coaching database — Hana's sprint sessions, Marcus's conditioning blocks, all of them with their pacing adjustments logged in the sub-table that the summary view didn't surface. The data was there. It sat in the system, timestamped, tagged with her coaching ID, linked to the target changes she'd entered on her console. A clear, legible pattern for anyone who queried the raw feed logs rather than the medical summaries.

Nobody queried the raw feed logs. That was the thing about institutional data — there was always more of it than anyone had time to review, and the systems were designed to surface the important numbers and bury the rest. The important numbers were the athlete outcomes: times, performance metrics, injury rates. The coaching adjustments were infrastructure. Background noise. There unless you knew to look.

Margot knew where the data lived and she knew nobody was looking and she filed her report and closed her laptop.

Through the window, the last light had gone from the ridge. The mountains were a black absence against a sky nearly as dark, and inside the facility the artificial lighting maintained its constant warmth — the same temperature at five a.m. and five p.m. and midnight. She could still feel the seventh hurdle in her forearms. Not the real impact — the feed's translation of it. Tomás's palms hitting the rubber, the jolt traveling up through his wrists to his elbows, the hot scrape of track surface against skin, all of it mapped onto her motor memory and stored as a sensation that didn't belong to her but that her body kept replaying anyway, the way a song stuck in your head wasn't your song but your brain wouldn't let go of it.

In two days the ghost sensation would fade. In two days she'd be plugged into Hana or one of the gymnasts, and a newer borrowed experience would overwrite this one, and the cycle would continue because the cycle was the job, the job was the cycle, and Margot Voss did not have a problem. She had a methodology.

She checked her email. Nothing urgent. She looked at her reflection in the dark window — just an outline, a shape without detail, which was better than the bathroom mirror. She turned off the desk lamp and sat in the near-dark, the only light the faint glow of the biometric display on the wall and the green standby indicators on the monitoring equipment down the hall, visible through the gap beneath her door.

Her wrist mount sat on the desk where she'd placed it after the shower. She picked it up. Turned it over. The docking contacts were still slightly warm. She pressed the mount against her left wrist, not connected to anything, just the familiar pressure of the device against her skin, and she held it there until the warmth transferred from the metal to her pulse point, and then she put it down and went to dinner.


# Chapter 5

Kai's left ankle clicked when he crouched into the starting blocks — the natural kind, a tendon snapping over bone. His right ankle adjusted a fraction of a second later, the synthetic muscle recalibrating the joint angle before he'd finished settling into the pedals. He'd mostly stopped noticing the difference. Today it registered, faintly, like seeing a word misspelled on a sign you drove past every morning.

Lane three. The indoor track had its own atmosphere: the rubbery chemical smell of the surface, the dry altitude-compensated air that sat in the back of his throat, the overhead lights throwing a flat, temperatureless brightness across the oval. High ceilings with acoustic dampening that turned footfalls into soft, muted thuds — nothing like the outdoor crack of spikes on a real track. Two weeks at Kirchberg and the thin air had stopped being a problem, but each morning's first deep breath still carried a small sharpness at the bottom of the inhale, a reminder that the oxygen up here was rationed by geography.

Six other sprinters were warming up. Novak on the far lane, doing strides with a gait so bilaterally perfect it looked rendered. Priya Chandrasekaran shaking out her hamstrings near the curve, her right leg extending and retracting with a mechanical evenness that said her grafts were freshly calibrated. A couple of athletes Kai knew by face but not by name jogging easy laps. Through the glass partition at the 100m mark, two coaches sat with tablets on their knees, watching telemetry feeds.

The start tone sounded. He drove out of the blocks.

The first fifty meters were the part that still felt like running. His feet hit the rubber, knees driving forward, the initial acceleration building in his quads with a burn that was unmistakably organic — lactic acid, effort, the specific heat of muscle doing work it was being asked to do. His arms pumped. His breathing locked into the sprint cadence, short exhales timed to footstrikes. The synthetic grafts operated on a latency gradient, ramping up output as his body exceeded its natural threshold. For those first seconds, the effort belonged to him.

Then the system engaged, and the effort smoothed. There was a seam — somewhere around the sixty-meter mark, impossible to pin down — where his own power generation handed off to the assisted version. His stride lengthened by a centimeter he hadn't chosen. His arm swing adjusted, elbows tightening, hands loosening. The burn in his quads didn't vanish but it changed character, became a managed thing, the system distributing load across natural and synthetic fibers in a pattern that prioritized efficiency. He was still running. The running had stopped needing him.

Eighty meters. Ninety. The visual processing enhancement sharpened — lane markings gaining a crystalline clarity, the far wall of the track becoming almost hyperreal while the periphery softened to blur. Enhancement tunnel vision. Everything not directly ahead dimmed to irrelevance.

A hundred meters. The curve approaching. His body leaned into it at the angle the system calculated, banking against the centrifugal force with a precision his own balance had never achieved. The wall on his left — padded concrete, grey foam over grey block — slid past, close enough to touch if he'd reached out. The padding was standard: rated for someone decelerating, someone who'd already started pulling up. Not for someone at full speed.

A hundred and twenty. A hundred and thirty. His time was good. He could feel that without checking the clock, the same way a driver reads speed from the pitch of the engine.

A hundred and forty.

His left foot struck the track and the track was gone.

Not gone. He was gone. Every channel that connected his mind to his body shut off simultaneously — not one after the other, not a sequence, but a single clean severance. Vision didn't go dark. Darkness would have been something he was seeing. This was the removal of the ability to see. Sound didn't go silent. Silence is an auditory experience. This was the end of hearing as a faculty. Proprioception — the sense of his own body's position, the knowledge of where his limbs were — didn't go numb. It ceased to be a category his mind could access.

He was thinking. He knew that. The thoughts arrived clearly, one after another, in a mind that had been untethered from everything it was supposed to be housed in.

*I was running.*

*I can't feel my legs.*

*I can't feel anything.*

The thoughts were precise and useless. They had nowhere to connect. He was aware in the way that a person dreaming is aware — present, conscious, processing — but without a single piece of sensory data to process. No body. No ground. No air in the lungs that he could feel being his lungs. He tried to open his hands and there were no hands. He tried to stop running and there was no running, not from where he sat inside his own skull. Just the knowledge, arriving from nowhere, that somewhere below the thinking, the machine was still going.

His body was still sprinting.

He knew this without evidence. The synthetic grafts, the spinal neural bridge — they were receiving system commands through channels that didn't pass through his conscious mind. His legs were driving through their stride cycle at thirty-seven kilometers per hour. His arms were pumping. His lungs were cycling air. The body was executing the sprint it had been optimized to execute, and the person whose name was on the entry form had been disconnected like an unplugged peripheral.

He tried to stop. He tried with everything that trying meant in a place with no muscles to engage. The command formed in his mind — *stop, slow down, stop* — and went nowhere. There was nothing between the thought and the legs. The channel was cut.

Time passed, or didn't. He had no way to measure it. No heartbeat he could feel, no breathing rhythm, no external reference. The thoughts kept coming: *The curve. The wall is on the curve. The wall is padded. The padding isn't for this.* He was doing math he didn't want to do. Thirty-seven kilometers per hour. The curve was coming. Or had passed. Or was now. He didn't know.

His consciousness sat in the void and waited for something to happen to a body it could not reach.

He thought about bracing. The thought was detailed and specific — tuck the chin, drop the shoulder, turn sideways to distribute the impact — and it went to exactly the same place as the command to stop. Nowhere. He could choreograph the perfect crash response in his mind and his body would not receive it. He was composing instructions for a machine that had changed the locks. The wall was coming at the speed his legs were carrying it, and he could plan and strategize and problem-solve inside his skull for as long as the thirty-seven kilometers per hour allowed, and none of it would reach his arms, his legs, his neck, the muscles that would need to absorb the impact.

The crash brought his senses back.

Sound came first — a deep, concussive thud that he felt through his skull before he heard it, the vibration traveling through bone. His right shoulder hitting the padded concrete at sprint speed, the foam compressing to nothing and the block wall behind it absorbing the remainder with a force that shoved the humeral head out of its socket. The joint separated with a wet, structural wrongness, like a hinge coming off a door — not a clean pop but a grinding dislocation, the ball of the shoulder sliding forward and down to a place where it didn't belong.

Then the ground. His body spun off the wall and his face met the rubber track surface, his left cheek dragging across it with a friction that peeled skin. The rubber was textured — fine grit, designed for traction — and it abraded his cheek and the skin above his left eye with the efficiency of sandpaper. He felt the track surface embedding in the wounds, tiny particles of rubber compound grinding into the raw tissue. Blood came immediately, warm and then cold in the facility's dry air.

His left hand went out. Reflex or system command, he'd never know. The wrist took his weight wrong and something inside it broke — a sharp, specific failure, the radius snapping at the distal end. He felt the bone give, felt the architecture of the wrist lose its structural logic. The pain arrived in a bright, narrowing flare that traveled up his forearm and stopped behind his elbow.

He was on the track. On his side, then on his front, then something between the two. The overhead lights glared. Blood ran from his cheek across his left eye, turning the fluorescent panels into smeared halos. His right shoulder was hot, displaced, the joint sitting inches from where it was supposed to be, every small movement sending a sick, grinding pulse through his torso. His left wrist was broken and he could feel the bones misaligned inside the brace of his own skin, a wrongness he could map with his mind's eye: the clean bone, the break, the offset.

His legs were still twitching.

Not running. Not anymore. But firing — quadriceps clenching and releasing in a stuttering half-rhythm, calves contracting, the synthetic grafts executing fragments of the sprint stride that had nowhere left to go. Short, jerky kicks against the track surface, his spikes scraping rubber. Residual system commands feeding through the neural bridge, the body's optimization software still trying to run a program the body had already crashed out of.

He watched his own legs do it. Lying on the track in blood, his shoulder wrecked, his wrist broken, he watched the muscles in his thighs fire without his permission, watched his feet scrabble at the rubber like something pinned and struggling, and he could not make them stop. The commands were not his. The legs were his. The commands were not.

People were already running toward him. Novak arrived first — Kai saw his shoes, blue and white, stopping two feet from his face. Someone shouted for medical. The coaches behind the glass partition had stood up, their tablets forgotten. Through the blood in his left eye, Kai could see Priya standing at the edge of the far lane with her hands hanging at her sides, not coming closer, watching with the particular stillness of a person looking at something happening to a body and thinking about her own.

"Don't move him." A voice from somewhere above and behind. "Wait — don't move him."

He wasn't trying to move. He was lying on a track he'd run a hundred times in two weeks and watching his left leg kick once more, the spike leaving a grey scuff mark on the rubber, and then stop.

The medical team arrived. Two technicians and a woman from Petra Engel's staff — Erika, he thought, or maybe Ingrid, a broad-shouldered German woman with cold hands and the steady efficiency of someone who'd trained for exactly this and never expected to use it on a morning sprint session. They had a backboard and a cervical collar and a calmness that was either professional or pharmaceutical.

"Can you hear me?" She was stabilizing his cervical spine, her fingers cold against the sides of his neck.

"Yeah."

"Tell me what hurts."

"Shoulder's out. Right shoulder. Wrist is broken, the left one." He heard his own voice — flat, emptied of the humor he'd normally use to pad a situation like this. The humor was gone. Not suppressed, not held back. Gone.

"Can you feel your feet?"

He could. The twitching had stopped and what remained was his own body, reporting in: toes, ankles, the deep ache of muscles that had been doing work he hadn't authorized. "Yeah. I feel them."

She didn't look relieved. She looked like she was filing the answer.

They rolled him onto the backboard. The shoulder shifted and he grunted — a closed-mouth sound that he chose over the scream that was the honest alternative. Five other athletes were standing in a loose semicircle, watching. Novak had his arms crossed. Priya hadn't moved from the far lane. The indoor track was very quiet, the acoustic dampening doing what it was designed to do — absorbing the small sounds of the medical team working, turning everything into a muted, padded version of itself. The same dampening that had swallowed the sound of his crash, reduced it to something the athletes at the far end of the track probably heard as a thump and nothing more.

Blood dripped from his face onto the white surface of the backboard. Small red drops, pooling. His cheek was raw — he could feel the rubber burns, the gritty texture of abraded skin. The split above his left eye was bleeding freely, the blood running into his eye and making the world pulse red at the edges.

They carried him down the corridor between the track and the medical wing. Three minutes of fluorescent light and ventilation hum, the ceiling panels sliding past at the speed of other people's walking. Kai stared at the ceiling and tried to locate the boundary between the moment he'd lost his senses and the moment the wall had given them back. The boundary was clean. No fade. No static. No transition. One stride he was running, the next he was nowhere, and then his shoulder hit a wall and the world poured back in all at once, too loud, too bright, too much.

The thirty seconds in between were not dark. They were not anything. He kept coming back to that. Darkness was something. This had been nothing.

And the legs. His legs had kept running through the nothing as if the nothing were normal. As if the legs didn't need him. As if he were the peripheral, and the legs were the system.

---

The medical bay smelled like isopropyl alcohol and the cold metal of instrument trays. They reduced his shoulder first — two techs bracing him while a third manipulated the joint, working the humeral head back into the socket with a practiced, mechanical patience. The moment it slid home was a nauseating pop, the joint finding its seat with a wrongness that was somehow worse than the dislocation, as if the shoulder remembered where it used to be and was angry about the detour.

"Shit," Kai said, and that was all he said about it.

They braced the wrist. The bones were misaligned, and when the technician palpated the break site, the pain was specific and informative — it told him exactly where the radius had failed and in which direction. They immobilized it in a rigid splint that held his hand in a position of mild extension, the fingers free, the wrist locked. It would need imaging. The grafts would accelerate the bone repair, which was its own kind of problem — the bones would knit faster than they should, and whether they'd knit correctly was a question the accelerated healing didn't ask.

The facial work was done by a doctor Kai hadn't met — a quiet man with a Swiss accent and the practiced steadiness of someone who'd sutured hundreds of faces. He cleaned the abrasions first, flushing the rubber grit from the raw tissue on Kai's cheek with saline that stung in a clean, medical way. Then the sutures above the left eye — five of them, the needle and thread moving with a rhythm Kai tracked because there was nothing else to look at except the ceiling.

"You'll have a small scar here," the doctor said, tying off the last stitch.

"Sure."

He lay on the exam table and let them finish. The fluorescent panels overhead were the same as in the corridor, the same as in every room in this facility — flat, revealing, the kind of light that showed you everything and warmed you with nothing. His shoulder throbbed in slow, deep pulses. His wrist was a constant low-grade pressure punctuated by sharp spikes whenever his fingers moved. The abrasions on his cheek burned when the air touched them.

The door opened and Yusuf came in carrying his tablet and a data cable. His face was set in the expression Kai had learned to read over two weeks of calibration sessions — concentration pulled tight, his eyes slightly narrowed behind his glasses, an engineer looking at data he didn't trust.

"How bad?" Yusuf asked, pulling a chair to the bedside.

"Shoulder's back in. Wrist is broken. Face is—" Kai gestured vaguely with his unbraced hand.

"Yeah, I can see the face. Can I plug in?"

Calibration shorthand. Yusuf wanted access to Kai's system logs — the neural bridge data, the graft telemetry, the visual processing records. Everything the system had recorded while Kai had been absent from his own body. The data that said what the body had been doing when the person inside it wasn't there.

"Go ahead."

Yusuf leaned over him and tilted his head gently, finding the port at the base of Kai's skull. His hands were steady, the way they always were — an engineer's hands, precise and unhesitating. The cable clicked into the port with a small, definitive sound. Yusuf sat back and started scrolling.

Kai watched him read. The tablet's screen was angled away, but the glow reflected in Yusuf's glasses — data streaming past, timestamps and system metrics. Yusuf's expression shifted. A tightening around the eyes first. Then a stillness that was worse.

"What happened at one-forty," Kai said.

"That's what I'm looking at." Professional voice — clean, measured, the Birmingham scraped away. He scrolled. Stopped. Scrolled back. "Your visual processing dropped to zero at the one-forty mark. Full shutdown, not a degradation. Simultaneous."

"It wasn't just visual."

"No, I can see that. Proprioception at the same timestamp. Auditory input—" He checked the screen. "Forty-two milliseconds later. Total sensory dropout." He paused on something. "The spinal bridge maintained motor output throughout."

"My legs didn't stop."

"No." Yusuf still hadn't looked up from the tablet. "The grafts were receiving movement commands from the bridge's autonomous subsystem. Stored sprint pattern. It didn't need your conscious input."

The medical bay's equipment hummed. The same hum as the calibration bay, the same hum as every room in the building — infrastructure processing, running its own patterns, doing things without being asked.

"How long was I out?"

"Thirty-one seconds." Yusuf pulled something up on the screen. "Sensory channels came back at the one-seventy mark. Wall's at one-seventy-three." He said this without emphasis, as a measurement, but his jaw was tight.

"I know where the wall is."

"I know you do." Something cracked in his professional voice — a small fissure. He tapped through several more screens, opened something Kai couldn't see. Then his finger stopped moving.

"What?"

"Hold on." Yusuf was scrolling fast now, his fingertip dragging through the data with the urgency of someone chasing a pattern through noise. He stood up, still tethered to Kai by the cable, and moved to the wall-mounted terminal. The larger screen showed what the tablet had been showing — dense columns of system code, error logs, timestamps flagged in amber and red.

"Your calibration firmware's corrupted," Yusuf said. He was talking to the screen as much as to Kai — the monologue mode, the engineer thinking aloud. "Not a mechanical fault. Corruption. Errors propagating through the sensory processing layer into the motor architecture." He pulled up another data set. "And it's not—" He stopped. Pulled up another. Stood very still.

"Yusuf."

"Three other athletes," Yusuf said. "Novak. Hoffman. Priya. Past forty-eight hours. Same calibration subsystems, same error signatures. Low-level anomalies — micro-disruptions in sensory timing, offset in motor coordination. Nothing that'd cause a full blackout. Yet." He turned from the screen. His glasses still reflected the error logs, amber and red scrolling across the lenses. "But the pattern's the same. The errors are replicating through the shared calibration network."

"Replicating." Kai tried to sit up. His shoulder objected with a grinding flare, and he settled back against the exam table.

"Every athlete who's been calibrated on this network in the past month is connected to it," Yusuf said. His professional register was thinning, the careful clinical vocabulary giving way to something faster, less controlled. "The corruption spreads through the calibration handshake. One system syncs with the network, the errors copy across."

"That's a virus."

Yusuf didn't answer immediately. He stood at the wall terminal with his hands at his sides. His glasses had slid down his nose and he hadn't pushed them up, which was, in Kai's two weeks of watching Yusuf work, something that didn't happen. Yusuf always pushed up his glasses. It was the first thing his hands did when they weren't holding instruments.

"It behaves like one," Yusuf said. "Replication mechanism, propagation vector, cascading error structure. It's consistent with a self-replicating firmware exploit." He paused. "Everyone on this network is carrying it. Every enhanced athlete in this facility."

The hum of the equipment filled the silence. Kai lay on the exam table with his shoulder put back and his wrist braced and blood drying on his face in stiff, crackling patches, and he thought about Novak's perfect stride, and Priya shaking out her hamstrings at the curve, and every other athlete in this building whose body was connected to the same network that had just switched him off and kept running.

"Can you fix it?"

"I don't know yet." Professional voice gone entirely. Just Yusuf — a man from Birmingham who was looking at something in the infrastructure that he hadn't expected to find. "I need to quarantine the network. Full diagnostic. That means grounding every enhanced athlete from training until I can map the scope."

"Every athlete."

"Everyone who's been calibrated here. Which is everyone." He reached up and pushed his glasses into place. The gesture was automatic, but Kai saw his fingers weren't quite steady. "I'll talk to Petra and the facility director. We need to shut down all calibration access until I can isolate the propagation mechanism."

Kai closed his eyes. The darkness behind his eyelids was familiar, ordinary — the comfortable dark of a person choosing not to see. He could feel his eyelids. He could feel the pull of the stitches above his left eye, the crust of drying blood on his lashes, the faint pressure of the exam table against the back of his skull. He could feel all of it, and the feeling was so ordinary, so unremarkable, that it made him want to keep his eyes closed for a very long time.

The thirty-one seconds hadn't been dark. They hadn't been anything. And through all thirty-one of them, his legs had been running at thirty-seven kilometers per hour toward a wall, and the legs had not needed him to be there.

Yusuf unplugged the cable. The click of it disconnecting was small and precise, a sound Kai had heard at the end of every calibration session for two weeks. Yusuf stood, collected his tablet, and paused at the door.

"Get some rest," he said, and the words had the shape of professional advice but the weight of something else — an engineer who had just learned that every system he maintained was carrying something he didn't put there, talking to a body that had just demonstrated what that something could do.

The door closed. Kai lay in the antiseptic light and listened to his own breathing and felt his legs resting on the exam table — still, quiet, entirely under his control. He flexed his right foot. The synthetic muscle responded instantly, the motion smooth, obedient. He flexed the left. Same. His legs did what he told them to do, and the ordinariness of it was the most frightening thing that had happened to him all morning, because thirty minutes ago his legs had done exactly what they wanted, and they had done it well, and he had not been there for any of it.


# Chapter 6

Yusuf hadn't left the calibration bay since the crash. Fourteen hours now, maybe fifteen — he'd stopped checking the clock around midnight when the log analysis consumed whatever part of his attention had been tracking time. The mug of coffee on the workstation ledge had gone cold hours ago. He moved it aside every time he reached for the secondary display and moved it back when he was done, a small mechanical rhythm that kept his hands occupied while the data on the screen got steadily worse.

The bay's constant sixty-hertz hum — the processing equipment, the cooling systems, the background drone of a building full of machinery keeping bodies tuned — had shifted in his perception sometime around four in the morning. It no longer sounded like infrastructure. It sounded like a pulse belonging to something sick.

He scrolled through another block of propagation logs. Outside the bay's single window, snow fell in the predawn dark. The facility was under restricted-operations protocol: no training sessions, no calibrations, no scheduled maintenance. Everything offline until his team could map the extent of the corruption. Forty-three athletes were in their quarters or the common areas or the cafeteria, waiting for someone to tell them whether their bodies were safe. Yusuf was supposed to be that someone.

The pattern was clear. He'd been hoping it wouldn't be.

Every athlete who had connected to the calibration network in the past thirty-two days showed micro-level firmware irregularities. Not all were catastrophic — Kai's blackout had been the worst case, a full cascade that wiped sensory processing in under 0.8 seconds. But the corruption was present in varying degrees across every system that had handshaked with the shared network since early January. It was dormant in some systems, active in others. There was no way to predict which would cascade next, or when.

"Right," he said to the empty bay. "So that's everyone, then."

His phone showed seven missed calls from the facility director. He hadn't answered because he'd wanted to be certain before he spoke. Now he was certain, and he still didn't pick up the phone.

The bay door opened. Petra Engel came in holding a tablet and looking like she'd slept about as long as he had — which was to say she hadn't. The grey streaks in her hair seemed more pronounced under the fluorescent lights, and the clinical precision of her posture had a new rigidity to it, as if holding herself upright required active effort.

"Two more," she said.

She set the tablet on his workstation. Two medical intake forms. He read them while she stood next to him, not sitting, not leaning, just present.

The first: Marcus Ndlovu, twenty-six, South African, sprint relay. He'd lost all hearing for approximately three seconds during a start drill the previous morning — the same session Kai had been running on the indoor track, on the other side of the facility. Three seconds of total silence while his body stayed locked in the starting blocks. The hearing had returned on its own. Marcus had finished the drill, told no one, and come to the medical bay at ten o'clock that night because he couldn't sleep and his left ear was still ringing. The intake nurse noted that he'd tried to laugh it off when he arrived. His hands had been shaking.

The second: Sonja Petrić, twenty-three, Croatian, artistic gymnastics. Her left hand had gone numb on the uneven bars during a release skill. Complete loss of sensation from the wrist down, lasting approximately four seconds. The synthetic grafts in her forearms had maintained her grip on instinct — engineered muscle holding a bar her nerves couldn't feel. She'd dismounted cleanly and gone straight to medical. The intake notes recorded that she'd spent the entire wait opening and closing her left fist, over and over, checking that the fingers still answered.

Yusuf took off his glasses and rubbed the bridge of his nose. Put them back on.

"Have you told them what it is?" he asked.

"I've told them the anomalies are under investigation. I haven't given them a mechanism." Petra's voice was measured, each word placed. The register she used when she was building a wall between herself and what she was about to learn. "I need to understand the scope before I say anything definitive."

"The scope is everyone."

A silence. Petra pulled a stool from under the secondary workstation and sat down. She set the tablet on her knees and folded her hands over it.

"Define everyone."

"Every enhanced athlete who's connected to the shared calibration network since the first of January." He pulled up the session log and turned the monitor so she could see. "Forty-three athletes. That's everyone at the facility except the two traditional-category trainers who don't interface with our systems."

"You're certain of the propagation mechanism?"

"I can show you the path." He brought up the propagation map — nodes and connections, each node an athlete, each connection a calibration session where the handshake protocol had carried corrupted firmware. The map looked like a disease network, which was exactly what it was. "The virus — and it is a virus, I'm not being loose with that word — distributes through the calibration handshake. When an athlete's system connects for a tuning session, the network pushes firmware updates as standard procedure. The virus embedded itself in the update payload. Every system that accepted a firmware push in the past month has some degree of corruption."

Petra studied the map. Forty-three nodes, all radiating from the central hub of his calibration bay.

"The severity varies," Yusuf continued. The professional polish in his voice was cracking, more Birmingham coming through. He was talking faster now. "Some systems got a partial load. Enough to cause intermittent disruption — what Marcus and Sonja experienced. Others caught the full payload. Kai got the full payload."

"How do we determine who has partial and who has full?"

"Individual diagnostics on every system. Which means bringing athletes back into the calibration bay and connecting them to the same network that infected them in the first place." He caught himself, exhaled. "I'm working on an isolated diagnostic protocol — air-gapped from the main network, standalone firmware analysis. But it's going to take time."

"How much time?"

"Days. Three at least. Maybe five."

Petra stood. She touched the edge of her tablet, aligning it precisely with the workstation's edge — a small, careful gesture that looked like clinical practice and was probably just the need to impose order on something.

"I'll inform the director," she said. "And then the athletes. All of them. They deserve to know."

"Yeah. They do."

She left. The bay door closed behind her with its usual pneumatic sigh.

Yusuf sat alone with the propagation map. Forty-three nodes, all connected through his calibration bay, his protocols, his firmware update schedule. He had built this network. He had managed every handshake, approved every firmware push, maintained the infrastructure that the virus had used to move from body to body. And he hadn't seen it until a man ran face-first into a concrete wall at sprint speed.

He took his glasses off again and pressed his thumb and forefinger against his closed eyes until he saw phosphene static. When he opened them, the propagation map was still there. His hands were steady. He noticed this and couldn't tell whether it was competence or whether something in him had just gone numb.

***

Lena went to the medical bay at half past three in the afternoon because she had run out of reasons not to.

The restricted-operations protocol had emptied the facility's usual schedule. No pool time, no dryland training, no calibration sessions. The pool was locked. The indoor track where Kai had crashed was sealed behind yellow maintenance barriers taped across the corridor entrance. She'd eaten lunch in the cafeteria with Dara and two other swimmers, the four of them working through high-protein trays without talking about the one thing they were all thinking about. Dara had asked if Lena wanted to watch something in the common room afterward. Lena had said maybe later, and then gone back to her quarters and sat on the edge of her bed for forty minutes doing nothing.

She'd heard from another swimmer that Kai was conscious. She didn't know him — they'd nodded at each other in hallways, shared a cafeteria table once with five or six other people, exchanged maybe twenty words total since she'd arrived at Kirchberg. He was the sprinter who won everything and never looked like winning meant what it was supposed to. She was the swimmer whose neural link had started making decisions without asking. They'd had no particular reason to speak before yesterday.

The medical bay smelled of antiseptic and the faint mineral trace of surgical-grade synthetic polymer — the smell of graft work, maintenance fluid, the slightly sweet chemical note that was supposed to be odorless and never quite was. She knew the smell from childhood. The Meridian developmental facility in Zug had smelled like this during every calibration appointment from age four onward. It was one of her earliest sense memories, and she did not find it comforting.

Kai was in the second recovery room, propped up at a low angle on the adjustable bed. His left wrist was encased in a rigid brace — white polymer extending from his knuckles to mid-forearm. His right shoulder sat in an immobilization sling. The left side of his face showed six butterfly closures tracking the lacerations along his cheekbone and brow, and the surrounding skin had swollen into a mottled yellow-purple that looked worse than yesterday, the bruising still settling into the tissue. He was watching something on the wall-mounted display and turned it off when she appeared in the doorway.

"Hey," he said.

"Hello." She stood in the doorframe, uncertain how to enter a room she had no clear reason to be in. "I don't have to stay. If you'd rather—"

"No, come in. It's fine." He waved with his braced hand, a stiff half-gesture that visibly cost him something. "I've been not doing anything for about sixteen hours straight. I'm genuinely talented at it now."

She came in and sat in the hard plastic visitor's chair. It was the kind of chair designed to discourage long stays — the seat angled slightly forward, no armrests, a surface that made your back stiff within ten minutes.

"How is the wrist?" she asked.

"Weird." He held it up, the brace catching the fluorescent light. "The grafts are knitting the bone faster than it should go. I can feel it happening in there. Like — you know when your foot falls asleep and the blood starts coming back? That pins-and-needles feeling? It's like that, but inside the bone. Constant. This low buzzing, like the bone itself is humming."

She nodded. She did not know the specific sensation but she understood the category: a body performing a repair function that should have been invisible, the repair itself becoming a new wrongness layered over the injury it was trying to fix.

"The shoulder?"

"Back in the socket. Sling for a week. Petra says full range of motion in ten days, which—" He shifted in the bed, adjusting the sling strap with a grimace. "That's insane. Should be six weeks, minimum. But the system doesn't care about should."

Through the wall that separated the medical bay from the calibration bay, the equipment hum was faintly audible — a low vibration at the threshold of hearing. Lena had been aware of it since she'd entered the corridor. She was always aware of it.

"I heard that two other athletes had problems," she said. "Marcus Ndlovu and Sonja Petrić. During training."

"Yeah. Yusuf was here about an hour ago." Kai looked at the ceiling. "He says it's in the network. Everyone who's been calibrated in the past month. So, everyone."

A quiet. The word sat between them. The weight of it — not just one person's crash but forty-three people carrying corrupted firmware in their bodies, all of them walking around the facility waiting to find out if they were next.

Lena touched the back of her neck. The neural link port — two centimeters of surgical polymer, flush with the skin, usually hidden beneath her hair — was warm under her fingertips. She touched it without deciding to, the way you confirm a thing is still where you left it.

"Mine has been doing things," she said.

She hadn't planned to say it. She wasn't sure why she was saying it now, to this person she barely knew, in this fluorescent room with the smell of graft polymer in the air. But he was looking at her with an expression that wasn't curiosity or pity or professional interest. It was recognition.

"During training?"

"During everything. Training, reading, meals." She paused, choosing her words with the care of someone constructing a sentence in a language that was fluent but not first. "I will be present, and then I'm not, and then I come back and I don't know how long I was gone. Yesterday I was reading in my quarters. Twenty minutes passed. I didn't fall asleep. I just wasn't there."

Kai was quiet. The humor she'd seen him deploy in public — the self-deprecating deflection, the media-trained warmth that kept everything at a comfortable distance — wasn't there. Just attention, and a face too swollen and bruised to perform much of anything.

"Your body keeps going when you're out?"

"Yes. In the pool, my stroke continues. The neural link maintains the pattern it was running. From the outside it looks — I'm told it looks normal." She stopped. "From the inside it's not."

"No," he said. "It's not."

Another silence. Outside the medical bay's window, the afternoon light was already failing — winter in the Bernese Alps, the sun dropping below the western ridgeline before four o'clock. Snow had been falling most of the day. A precise line of accumulation sat along the outer window ledge, growing by fractions.

"When the blackout happened," Kai said, and then didn't continue for several seconds. He was looking at his braced wrist, not at her. "I couldn't see or hear or feel anything. Total nothing. But my legs kept going. The grafts were still getting system commands and they were running me at full speed. Perfect form, probably. And I was just... gone." He exhaled through his nose, a flat sound with no humor in it. "It didn't need me there to run. My body did the job fine without anyone home."

Lena sat with what he'd said. She gave it the space it required rather than filling the silence with something inadequate.

"That's what's frightening," she said. "Not the malfunction. The competence."

He looked at her then. Something in his face shifted — not toward warmth, exactly, but toward a kind of openness, as if she had found a word he'd been circling without reaching.

"Yeah," he said. "Exactly that."

They sat for a while without speaking. The medical equipment produced its small mechanical sounds — the IV pump's intermittent click, the diagnostic monitor's low tonal beep every thirty seconds. Snow accumulated on the window ledge. Kai's wrist buzzed its constant pins-and-needles signal from inside the bone, the synthetic grafts knitting fracture faster than any body should heal, and Lena sat with her hand resting against the back of her neck, fingertips on the neural link port that had been part of her since she was four years old and that she had never once considered foreign until this month.

A nurse came in to check Kai's vitals — blood pressure, graft integration readings, the standard post-trauma sequence. They made small talk while the nurse worked. What the cafeteria was serving for dinner. Whether anyone had fixed the projector in the common room. The kind of flat, functional conversation people produce when a third person is present and the real conversation has to wait. Kai asked the nurse about the painkillers wearing off and the nurse said she'd check with Dr. Engel. Lena asked if there was decaf in the hallway machine and the nurse said it had been broken since Tuesday. The nurse left.

The quiet that returned was different from the quiet before the nurse came in. Something had been established between them that had no name yet. Not friendship, not romance, not solidarity in any organized sense. Just two people whose bodies were doing things without permission, and the particular relief of not having to explain why that was terrifying to someone who already knew.

"I should go," Lena said, after a while.

"Yeah. OK."

She stood. At the doorway she turned back.

"The pool will still be closed tomorrow?"

"Yusuf said restricted ops for at least three more days. Maybe five."

"Then I'll probably come back. If that's all right." She started a sentence, stopped it, started a different one. "There isn't much to do."

"Door's open." A trace of humor, deflated at the end, making the invitation deliberately smaller than it was. "Not like I'm going anywhere." He tapped the sling with his braced fingers and the tap made him wince.

She nodded and walked out. In the corridor, the fluorescent lights buzzed at their constant pitch. The calibration bay hum was louder here, the shared wall between medical and engineering transmitting it into the hallway like a low-grade headache. She walked back toward the residential wing with her hand pressed to the back of her neck, fingertips resting on the port, and she did not take her hand away until she was inside her room with the door shut behind her.

***

Margot sat on the edge of her bed in the dark and thought about vectors — not the mathematical kind, the epidemiological kind. She'd done a semester of public health coursework during her coaching certification, enough to know the basics: transmission required a pathway, a medium, and a susceptible host.

Yusuf had confirmed the mechanism by mid-afternoon. Word had traveled through the facility's informal networks faster than any official announcement — the athletes texting each other, coaching staff comparing notes in the corridor, everyone assembling the same picture from different fragments. The virus propagated through the shared calibration protocols. Firmware updates carrying corrupted code. Forty-three athletes on the same infected infrastructure.

But the calibration network wasn't the only way into an athlete's system.

She hadn't turned on the overhead light. Her room was filling with the particular grey of a winter afternoon dying in the mountains, the snow-reflected light from outside fading degree by degree. The wall-mounted monitoring display — the stripped-down coaching-staff version, not the full diagnostic suite the athletes had — glowed faintly with her baseline metrics. Heart rate, neural baseline, integration status, all showing green. The display didn't know what she was thinking about.

The neural coaching feed. Her wrist-mounted interface, the device she'd carried since her first coaching season, personal equipment she'd bought and maintained herself. When she plugged into an athlete, the device established a bidirectional data connection between her system and the athlete's enhancement architecture. She received their sensory input — that was the job, that was what the technology was designed for. A coach experiencing what the athlete experienced, reading the body's signals in real time to guide pacing and technique.

But the connection protocol opened both lanes. It had to. Data flowed from athlete to coach and from coach to athlete — the return pathway carried the pacing adjustments, the heart-rate targets, the micro-corrections that justified neural coaching as a profession rather than passive observation. The feed was a two-way street running directly into the deepest layers of an athlete's neural system.

If the virus could move through the calibration network's handshake protocol — a relatively constrained data exchange compared to a full feed session — then the feed's deep-access connection was a wider road, a more direct line into the exact firmware the virus was targeting.

She ran the list in her head. In the past thirty-two days she had plugged into Tomas Reyes — three sessions, then two more after the hurdle fall. Lena Morozova, two sessions. A distance runner named Amara whose 10K pace needed adjustment, one session. Hendrik, a pole vaulter, one session. Nine feed sessions in a month, four different athletes, and in every single one the bidirectional pathway had been wide open, her interface handshaking with their enhancement systems at a depth that made a calibration tune-up look like a handshake through a closed door.

She stood up and went to the bathroom. The fluorescent tube above the mirror flickered once before catching, and her face in its light looked washed out — bags under her eyes, the softness along her jaw that bothered her some days and didn't bother her other days and right now didn't register at all. She turned the tap on and off without washing anything. Stood there with her hands on the edge of the sink. Turned the light off and went back to the bed.

She should tell Yusuf. The action was simple. Yusuf was in the calibration bay mapping the virus's propagation paths, and his map was incomplete — the feed sessions didn't route through his network, they ran on her personal equipment, a separate data channel that his system analysis couldn't see. If the feed was a transmission vector, he was working with a partial picture. He needed this information. Marcus Ndlovu and Sonja Petrić and the rest of them needed this information.

She picked up her phone. The screen lit her face in the dark room. She opened her contacts, scrolled to Yusuf's name.

She put the phone down on the mattress.

Because telling Yusuf about the vector meant telling him about the sessions. Not just the count — the depth. How far into the athletes' systems she went. How long she stayed connected. The pacing adjustments she made that pushed past coaching protocol boundaries. The heart-rate elevations that any sports medicine doctor would flag as reckless if they ever saw the numbers. The session with Tomas three weeks ago where she'd pushed him past the safety margin and he'd fallen on the track, and she'd stood in the debrief and blamed altitude fatigue. The session with Lena where she'd watched the desync data spike and called it acceptable drift because calling it anything else would mean someone restricting her access to the feed.

Telling Yusuf meant opening the logs. Her device recorded everything — connection duration, data throughput, which system layers she accessed, every parameter she adjusted during a session. The records would show that Margot Voss, neural performance coach, spent twice as long in the feed as her coaching duties required, accessed architecture deeper than any coaching protocol authorized, and pushed athletes' cardiovascular parameters to levels that would end her career the moment a medical director saw them.

She picked the phone back up. She could call Petra instead. Petra was medical, not engineering — she might understand the clinical urgency without immediately digging into the session details. But Petra was thorough, and Petra was already suspicious of everyone right now, and Petra would want the logs. Petra would absolutely want the logs.

She thought about Kai in the medical bay with butterfly closures holding his face together. About Marcus Ndlovu trying to laugh and his hands shaking. About Sonja Petrić's fingers opening and closing around nothing.

She thought about Tomas's hamstrings loading before a hurdle, felt from the inside. The sharpness of Lena's stroke, the chain of muscle from fingertip to hip engaging in sequence, felt from the inside. The way it was to be inside a body that performed — not her body, not her performance, but the rush real and total and hers for as long as the connection held.

Margot put the phone in the bedside drawer and closed it.

The diagnostic display on the wall pulsed its quiet green. Outside, the snow had stopped, and the particular silence that follows heavy snowfall in the mountains filled the room — a dense, padded quiet, the kind that made even your own breathing sound conspicuous. The mountains absorbed sound. They absorbed everything, if you let them.

She lay back on the bed, still in her clothes, and stared at the ceiling she couldn't see and did not call anyone.


# Chapter 7

The brace was off. Petra had cleared him to remove it for short periods, and the bone had knitted -- faster than any break had a right to mend. Not healed, exactly. Knitting implied the body's own patient labor, tissue finding tissue on its own schedule. This was different. The synthetic grafts had driven the bone repair forward on a timeline that had nothing to do with his pain tolerance or his patience, and the wrist had closed itself up six weeks ahead of the curve. The itch was deep inside, below the skin where the brace had rubbed raw, down in the bone itself where new tissue met old -- a wrongness that wasn't pain but speed, his body rebuilding on a schedule he didn't set.

Kai sat on the edge of his bed and put his left hand flat on the mattress.

The experiments had started by accident three days ago. He'd been reaching for a glass of water and caught the movement early -- his hand arriving at the glass a fraction ahead of his decision to reach. Normally he let these moments pass, the way you let your hand find a light switch in a dark room without thinking about how it got there. But this time he'd frozen with the glass in his fingers and tried to feel inside the gap. It was there -- not visible, not painful, just a tiny anticipatory firing in the rebuilt forearm muscle, the synthetic tissue queuing the motion before his conscious intent arrived. The system reading his mind and jumping the queue.

He'd started testing it. Index finger. He told himself to lift it, then waited. Felt for the flutter -- there, a gathering in the synthetic muscle, below the threshold of anything anyone watching would notice. He pressed the finger flat against the mattress. The system pushed. He held. One beat. Two beats. The gathering stalled against his resistance.

The finger lifted when he let it.

It was nothing. A fraction of a second of conscious override, invisible to anyone else, barely perceptible to him. But he'd been doing it every evening since, small repetitions with the same hand, and the guesses were getting less like guesses. He was starting to feel where the system's timing lived in the rebuilt muscle -- not as data, not as numbers on a calibration screen, but as sensation. A shape he could press against.

His shoulder clicked as he reached for the wrist brace. The joint had been tracking smoother since the dislocation healed, clicking into place with a precision that felt engineered rather than organic. His body was repairing itself into something more efficient than the version that had been injured. The scrapes on his cheek and brow had closed into thin pink lines, fading faster than they should have, the skin knitting itself shut with the same accelerated urgency that had mended the bone. He wasn't healing. He was being optimized around the damage.

He put the brace back on and went to dinner.

---

The cafeteria was half-empty at seven-thirty. Most of the athletes ate at six, their days partitioned into training blocks and recovery windows that left no room for eating late. The stragglers were support staff finishing shifts, a pair of physiotherapists arguing about ice bath protocols at a corner table, and Kai.

He took a tray through the line. Chicken breast, rice, steamed broccoli that had been in the warmer long enough to go soft. A cup of coffee that tasted like it had been brewed hours ago and kept alive through institutional persistence. The windows along the far wall showed the mountains as dark cutouts against a sky that was holding its last blue -- that deep alpine color February evenings produced when the days were short enough that dusk seemed to start at three.

Lena was sitting alone at one of the long tables near the windows. She had a plate of pasta half-finished and a book open beside her tray, though she wasn't reading it. She was looking at the dark shapes of the mountains through the glass.

Kai set his tray down across from her. "Hey."

"Hey." She shifted the book closer to her plate, making room. She didn't close it.

"What's that?"

She glanced at the cover as though she'd forgotten what she'd brought. "A novel. In German. I keep starting it."

"Good?"

"I don't know yet. I keep reading the same pages."

He didn't ask why. He could guess -- the same thing that had been eating her time since before his crash, the cognitive blankness that swallowed minutes without leaving any trace of its passage. She wouldn't want him to name it here, under the cafeteria's fluorescent lights. He unwrapped his fork and started on the chicken.

They ate without talking for a while. The cafeteria's background noise was enough to fill the silence comfortably -- trays being cleared somewhere in the kitchen, the physiotherapists still going about ice baths, the steady thrum of the heating system working against the alpine cold on the other side of the glass. Kai's shoulder clicked when he raised the fork. He'd mostly stopped noticing it except when the room was quiet enough for the sound to travel.

"How's the wrist?" Lena asked.

"Getting there." He held it up, the brace bulky and medical-looking under the lights. "Petra says I can start grip exercises next week. Which is her way of telling me to stop doing them now."

"You were already doing them."

"I was doing something. I don't know if exercises is the right word." He set the hand back on the table. "It's weird. The bone's basically done. Structurally sound. But it doesn't feel like my wrist healed. It feels like someone fast-forwarded through the healing and skipped the part where your body catches up to the repair."

Lena nodded. She pushed a piece of penne around her plate without eating it. "My mother broke her arm when I was twelve. Skiing. She wore the cast for eight weeks and complained about it every day. I remember thinking it seemed like such a long time for something to mend."

"It is a long time. That's sort of the point."

"I know. I didn't understand that then."

Kai drank his coffee. It was worse than he'd expected, which was saying something given that his expectations for institutional coffee were already on the floor. "Where was that? The skiing."

"Engelberg. We went every February when I was young." She paused, and then corrected herself. "My parents went. I went to training camp."

"Every February?"

"Every school holiday. The program had training blocks through the year. Summer was Tenero, for the outdoor pool. Winter was altitude work here or in Austria. Spring was a facility outside Munich for strength testing." She listed these the way someone describes a transit route -- factual, organized, without complaint or nostalgia. "I was home for ten days at Christmas and two weeks in August."

"Home being where?"

"Zug. My parents' apartment. But I'd lived at training facilities since I was eight, so." She made a small motion with her hand that seemed to close a door on the rest of the sentence. "It's just how it was."

"Sure." He looked at her across the institutional table. The fluorescent lighting did what fluorescent lighting always did -- washed the color out of everything, made the trays and the food and the faces all look like they existed on the same flat plane. He could see the neural link port at the base of her skull where her hair was pulled up. A small disc, almost but not quite the color of her skin. "Did you like it? The program?"

She considered this. He'd noticed she did that -- took a question and turned it before answering, not from evasion but from a genuine need to get the answer right.

"I liked swimming," she said. "The program was how I swam. So, yes. But the question doesn't quite fit, because you're asking as if there was an alternative."

"There wasn't?"

"My parents signed the developmental contract before I was born. Embryo-stage selection, then the link at four, then calibration every six months. By the time I was old enough to have opinions about it, the program was the structure of my life. My body was the program's body." She said this evenly. It wasn't a confession and it wasn't a complaint. It was a description of how things had worked. "I don't know what I'd be without it, because there isn't a version of me that exists without it."

"That's different from what I had," Kai said.

"I know."

"I had twenty-seven years of knowing what my body felt like before they rebuilt it. I've got a whole baseline sitting there. I know what running felt like when it was just me, because I spent a decade doing it. I can feel the gap." He turned his coffee cup on the table a quarter rotation, no reason. "You don't have that."

"No."

"So when your system does something you didn't choose -- when the link makes a decision before you make it -- you can't even tell if that's new, or if it's been happening your whole life and you're only noticing now."

Lena looked at him. "That's exactly it."

"Sorry. I'm not trying to --"

"No, it's fine. You're right. I don't know what my baseline is. Maybe I've never had one." She set her fork down. "When I was small, five or six, I had calibration sessions twice a week. I'd lie in the rig and the technician would say, can you feel this, can you feel that. And I always said yes. But I don't know now if what I was feeling was the calibration or my own nervous system reacting to the calibration. I can't separate them. There isn't a way to untangle it."

"Jesus."

"It's just how it is." She said it again, that phrase. Kai heard the work in it -- the practiced acceptance that wasn't quite acceptance but was the nearest thing she'd found.

He didn't try to reframe it or find the usable lesson. He ate his broccoli. It was overcooked the way institutional broccoli always was, the stems collapsing under his teeth into a flat green mush.

"Where's your Lafayette?" he asked after a minute. Then, seeing her confusion: "Sorry. I mean -- I'm from Lafayette, Indiana. When I think about home, I've got this specific picture. The fields behind my parents' house. Flat soybean fields that go to the horizon with nothing in between. Summer storms you could watch building for hours. These big thunderheads stacking up out of nothing, and you'd see the wall of rain coming from fifty miles away, this dark curtain dragging across the fields. And the high school track where I first ran. Nothing special. Four hundred meters of cracked rubber behind a school that smelled like floor wax and had water stains on the ceiling tiles. That's my home. That's what I miss when I miss something."

"I don't think I have a Lafayette," Lena said.

"Nothing?"

She thought about it. "The pool in Zug. The old facility, before they renovated. It had windows on the east wall, and in the mornings the light came through the water and made patterns on the tiles at the bottom. I liked that." She paused. "But that isn't homesickness. That's just remembering a pool."

"Maybe that's the same thing."

"It's not. You miss the fields and the storms and the specific smell of your school. You miss a place that existed before you. I miss a room in a facility that was built for my training program." She turned her water glass on the table. "Those aren't the same."

She was right, and Kai didn't have a way to argue her out of it without minimizing what she was telling him. So he didn't. He finished his rice. The cafeteria had emptied further while they'd been talking, the physiotherapists gone, a janitor beginning to mop the far end of the floor.

"My mom still watches my old race footage," he said. "The pre-enhancement stuff. She records herself reacting to it and sends me the videos. It's a lot."

"Does she like the old races better?"

"She says she can tell the difference. She says I used to lean differently out of the blocks. Which is true -- the grafts changed my push-off angle by about three degrees. But I don't think she's analyzing biomechanics. I think she's looking at her son versus whatever she thinks I am now."

"What does she think you are?"

He shrugged. The shoulder clicked. "Fixed. She uses that word. 'After they fixed you.' Like I was broken and then someone repaired me and now I'm finished. Which -- I was broken. Spine compressed, both knees gone. I couldn't make it to the mailbox without my legs going out. But 'fixed' implies there's an end point, and there isn't. The system keeps adjusting. I'm still being fixed."

Lena picked up her coffee and drank it without expression. It was the same bad institutional stuff from the same machine, and she consumed it the way she consumed everything the facility provided -- without complaint, without pleasure, as a fact of the environment.

"My parents use the word 'complete,'" she said. "The program made me complete. That's worse, I think."

"Yeah," Kai said. "That's worse."

Someone at a nearby table was having a loud conversation about lactate thresholds, the numbers floating across the room without landing on anything. Kai finished his chicken. Lena closed her book and stacked it on her tray.

"Do you want to walk?" she said.

"Outside? It's freezing."

"I know."

"Yeah. OK."

---

The cold hit them like a wall. They'd grabbed jackets from the row of hooks by the cafeteria's side door -- the heavy insulated ones the facility kept for anyone moving between buildings in winter -- and even through the padding the air found every gap. Minus twelve, maybe colder. Lena felt it in her sinuses first, a sharp chemical cleanliness that the building's recirculated air never achieved.

The path from the cafeteria to the residential wing was cleared and salted, but beyond the buildings the snow was deep, banked into drifts that the maintenance crew had pushed to the margins with their plows. The sky was black and enormous. The stars came through in a density that the facility's light pollution couldn't entirely wash out, though the sodium glow from the training halls yellowed the nearest snow and faded to nothing within a hundred meters.

They walked past the residential wing and onto the access road that led down toward the valley.
