The road was plowed but icy in stretches, and Lena watched her footing with the automatic caution of someone who'd been trained since childhood to protect her body from unnecessary injury. The mountains were shapes at this hour rather than scenery -- massive dark presences that blocked the stars where they met the horizon, their ridgelines rough and irregular against the sky.

Kai walked with his hands shoved into his jacket pockets, the wrist brace making his left side bulge. His breath made small clouds that the cold tore apart before they traveled a meter.

"I always forget how many stars there are up here," he said.

"You can see the Milky Way in summer. When it's clear."

"I haven't been here in summer."

"It's different. The fields around the facility go green and there are wildflowers on the lower slopes. The snow goes except on the peaks." She heard herself describing the landscape the way she'd described the pool in Zug -- as a feature of the institute's environment, framed by what training season it accompanied. The mountains had been here before the facility. Before the access road, before anyone decided this altitude was useful for calibrating enhanced athletes. They would be here after.

They reached a point where the road curved and the view opened -- the valley below, dark peaks across the gap, the town of Meiringen visible as a scatter of warm lights against the black valley floor. The air smelled like snow and pine resin and cold stone.

"Can I ask you something?" Kai said.

"Yes."

"When did you first notice the desync?"

She'd answered this before -- for Margot, for the diagnostics, for herself in the quiet inventory she ran each night in bed. But Kai wasn't asking for data.

"In October. Maybe November. I was in the pool and I finished a length and I couldn't remember the turn. Not like forgetting -- it was more like the turn hadn't happened for me. The movement was in the telemetry, in my muscle memory, but it wasn't in my experience."

"Was that scary?"

"I don't know what it was. It was like asking whether it's scary to blink and miss a second. You don't miss it. You arrive at the next moment without the transition."

"But you know it happened."

"After. From the data. There's a gap in what I experienced and the telemetry fills it in." She folded her arms tight against her body. The cold was getting into her hands. "The part that frightens me is that the gaps swim better than I do. The desync sections in my data -- my stroke rate is more consistent, my timing more efficient. When I'm not there, the system performs better."

Kai was quiet. The cold pressed against both of them, and she could hear his breathing -- the slight shallowness the altitude imposed on everyone, even after weeks of acclimatization.

"That's rough," he said.

"Yes."

"I don't have anything useful to say about that. I just -- yeah."

She appreciated that he didn't try to reframe it. Didn't tell her the data might be wrong, or that efficiency wasn't the same as better, or any of the reassurances that would have required her to perform gratitude. He let the fact sit in the frozen air between them, ugly and specific and hers. A car was moving on the valley road below, its headlights tracing the curves along the river.

"My mother sent me a care package last week," Lena said. "Chocolate from the shop in Zug, and socks, and a letter. The letter said she was proud of how I'm handling things. I don't know what she thinks I'm handling. She doesn't know about the desync. She just knows there was a virus and the facility went to restricted operations for a while."

"Parents fill in the blanks."

"My mother fills them in with optimism. She's very Swiss about problems. Everything will be organized and then it will be resolved."

Kai laughed -- a brief, real sound that the cold compressed into something small. "My mom fills them in with worst-case scenarios and prayer. She lit a candle at St. Mary's when I called about the crash. I said, Mom, the wrist is already healing. She said, 'The candle isn't for the wrist, Kai.'"

"What was it for?"

"She didn't say. I think she was praying that whatever comes out the other side of all this is still her son."

The wind picked up, coming off the ridge to the north. It carried a fine grit of ice crystals that stung where they hit exposed skin. Lena turned her face from it and Kai did the same, which put them both facing the facility -- the cluster of buildings lit up against the mountainside, warm and institutional and sealed against everything outside. From this distance, in the dark, the whole complex looked small. Concrete and glass stuck to the side of a mountain that had no use for it.

They stood for a while without talking. The stars were thick overhead, the Milky Way a faint smear across the center of the sky, barely visible through the facility's residual glow. The air was sharp enough to hurt when she breathed deeply, and the silence had a solidity to it that the facility's constant mechanical hum never allowed -- out here, the quiet was the mountain's quiet, the kind made by distance and cold and the absence of machinery.

Kai pulled his left hand from his pocket and flexed it. The brace creaked in the cold. Lena watched him open and close his fingers -- a small, deliberate movement that was neither stretching nor fidgeting. He was doing something specific with the hand, his attention focused on it in a way that was different from the casual gestures of the rest of the evening.

"Your hand," she said.

He looked at her. "Sorry. Just -- it does this thing."

"What thing?"

He was quiet for a moment, and she could see him reaching for the right words -- not the technical version, not the dismissive one. "Some of the muscle in there is synthetic. From the reconstruction. And the synthetic stuff has its own timing -- it fires a little before I tell it to. So I've been trying to catch it. Feel the difference between when it starts and when I start. Hold against it for a beat."

"Can you?"

"A little. Maybe. It's like trying to hear one instrument in an orchestra when you don't know what that instrument sounds like by itself."

Lena looked at his hand. In the starlight, with the brace and the cold stiffening his movements, there was nothing visible -- no difference between a hand gripping under its owner's command and a hand gripping under the system's. The contest was entirely internal, entirely his. But she understood the impulse. She'd spent years lying in calibration rigs trying to feel where she ended and the link began.

"I should try that," she said.

"What?"

"Listening for the system's timing. In the pool. If I could feel when the link is about to take over, before it completes --" She stopped. "I don't know. It's probably different. The link is in my head, not in my hand."

"Probably. But the idea might be the same. There's a gap between you deciding and the system deciding, and the gap has a shape. You can feel the edges of it if you know what to listen for."

She turned this over the way she turned most things -- carefully, from several angles, not committing until she'd checked it against what she already knew. It was a useful way to think about the problem. Not as a malfunction to diagnose but as a boundary to map. She didn't know if it applied to the desync, which worked in her cognition rather than her muscles, in the space between thoughts rather than between movements. But the principle -- that the system's timing was a separate thing from her timing, and that the difference might be something she could learn to perceive -- was something she could hold.

"The gap has a shape," she repeated. "I'll think about that."

"Yeah, well. I've had a lot of time in bed with nothing to do but stare at my hand."

The wind came again, harder. The cold had passed through bracing into something that simply hurt, the kind that went through insulated fabric and settled in the joints. Lena's fingers ached inside her pockets and the skin across her cheekbones was numb and drawn tight.

"Now we should go in," she said.

"Yeah."

They turned and walked back along the access road, their boots crunching on the salted ice. The facility grew as they approached, its windows bright squares of warm light, the sound of the heating system becoming audible as they closed the distance -- that low mechanical hum that was the building's version of breathing, constant and unnoticed until you'd spent twenty minutes in genuine silence. At the side door, Kai pulled the handle and warm air poured out around them, thick and almost sweet after the thin cold.

Lena stepped inside. The corridor was empty and fluorescent-lit. Linoleum floor, the smell of cleaning solution and residual cafeteria grease. She pulled off the heavy jacket and hung it on its hook. Her hands were red and stiff, slow to close.

"Thanks for the walk," she said.

"Thanks for freezing with me."

She smiled. It was small and it was real, and the hallway's flat light made it look ordinary -- the same light that made everything in the facility look ordinary, functional, evenly observed. They stood for a moment at the point where the residential wing branched, her floor to the left, his to the right. The wind was audible on the other side of the door, a muffled pressure against the building's sealed walls. Neither of them moved toward their separate corridors. It was the natural pause of two people who'd been together in the cold and weren't yet ready to go back to being separately warm.

"Good night, Kai."

"Night."

She went left. He went right. The facility hummed around them, sealed and heated and running its systems through the long Alpine dark.


# Chapter 8

The Meridian delegation arrived on a Tuesday morning, three black SUVs grinding up the mountain road through overnight snow. Kai watched them from the cafeteria windows while eating breakfast one-handed — fork in his right, the left wrist braced and too swollen to grip anything smaller than a coffee mug. The vehicles pulled into the loading bay with coordinated timing, one after the other, as if they'd rehearsed the spacing. Three people climbed out of the first: two men in dark coats and a woman in a grey wool suit who crossed the icy concrete without slipping, a leather portfolio held flat against her chest.

"That's Jin-seo Park," Tomas said across the table, leaning forward to follow Kai's gaze. "Athlete liaison. She was at the Seoul orientation last year." He paused with his spoon over a bowl of porridge. "She's all right. She actually listens."

Kai set his fork down. His wrist throbbed under the brace — a deep tidal pulse synchronized to his heartbeat, the bone reknitting on a schedule the grafts had set without consulting him. He could feel the repair happening, which was its own specific wrongness: a low grinding deep inside the bone, something being filed into shape from within. The stitched laceration across his left cheekbone had stopped hurting days ago but still pulled when he chewed.

Outside the cafeteria windows, the sky was the flat grey of late February at twenty-one hundred meters, snow stacked along the ridgelines in wind-sculpted drifts. The second and third SUVs discharged their passengers: technicians or lawyers or whatever Meridian sent when they wanted to remind you who owned the calibration network your nervous system ran on.

Tomas was watching too, but with a different expression — attentive, assessing, the look of someone keeping track of the people who controlled his livelihood. He was twenty-one and already fluent in reading institutional arrivals. "They don't usually send this many," he said.

"No."

"You think it's about the virus?"

Kai finished his coffee. "Yeah, Tomas. I think it's about the virus."

---

An hour later, thirty-four athletes and most of the Kirchberg senior staff filled the second-floor conference room. The space had been rearranged overnight — training-schedule whiteboards stacked against the wall, the usual scatter of tables replaced by rows of chairs facing a long table at the front. A projection display glowed with Meridian's logo on the wall behind it. Printed binders at every seat. Bottled water stamped with the Meridian mark, which had not previously appeared anywhere in the facility's supply chain. Someone from the delegation had carried those up the mountain.

The fluorescent lighting buzzed at a frequency Kai had stopped noticing weeks ago. He sat near the right wall, three rows back, his chair angled so he could see the door and the front table at once — a habit left over from the first days after the crash, when he'd started tracking his exits. Lena was two rows ahead of him, her hair still damp from the morning swim, the faint chlorine trace that followed her everywhere. Margot stood against the far wall with her arms crossed, watching the room with that flat coaching attention she brought to everything she observed but did not participate in. Yusuf was in the front row next to Dr. Engel. Yusuf had his hands folded, still. Engel sat with her back straight and her mouth a thin line, the posture of someone who'd been briefed and hadn't liked any of it.

Jin-seo Park stood behind the front table. She was smaller than the role implied — compact, dark hair cut at the jaw, no jewelry except a slim watch she touched once, unconsciously, before beginning. She waited for the room to settle without asking for quiet. When the last murmured conversation died she did not begin with the virus. She began with something Kai hadn't expected.

"I know some of you," she said. "Several of you from Seoul. Others from your files, which isn't the same thing." A brief pause. "Twelve days ago, one of you hit a wall at sprint speed because his body kept running after his senses cut out. I've read the medical report. I've seen the telemetry. I want you to know that what I'm about to present isn't something Meridian assembled in the past twelve days. We've been tracking this vulnerability for eight months. The disclosure timeline was moved forward because of what happened to Mr. Brennan."

She glanced at Kai. The room followed her glance. He felt the attention settle on the stitched cheekbone, the brace, the shoulder he was holding a degree too carefully. The Hungarian weightlifter two seats ahead turned all the way around to look at him, then turned back.

"Eight months," the weightlifter said. "You knew for eight months."

"The initial detection was eight months ago. Characterizing the virus — what it does, how it propagates, what stops it — took the full interval. I'm not going to stand here and tell you that timeline was perfect. I'm going to tell you what we have now."

She tapped the projection screen. A schematic filled the wall: the calibration network architecture that connected every enhanced athlete at the facility, rendered in clean blue lines and white nodes. Kai had seen rougher versions of this on Yusuf's whiteboards, hand-drawn, coffee-ringed. This version looked like it had been built for an investor presentation.

"The virus propagates through the shared calibration network," Jin-seo said. "Every routine calibration, every firmware sync — there's a transmission window. The corruption targets the firmware layer that governs sensory processing. When it triggers, you lose everything. Sight, hearing, proprioception, spatial orientation. Total blackout. The autonomous subsystems in your enhancements don't pause during a blackout. Your legs keep driving. Your arms keep stroking. Whatever intensity the system was running, it keeps running."

She let that sit. The room was quiet in a way that had texture to it — not the absence of sound but the active suppression of it, thirty-four people holding their breath or their questions or both. Someone shifted in their chair and the creak was enormous.

"We've developed a firmware-level patch that neutralizes the virus's replication mechanism and restores stable sensory processing. Installation takes approximately four hours per athlete under calibration-bay conditions. Dr. Qadir's team would handle the technical procedure." She nodded at Yusuf. He didn't nod back. His hands stayed folded. "In controlled simulation, the patch eliminated the blackout trigger in every scenario we tested."

"What's the catch?" Margot said from the wall. Not loudly, but her voice carried the way a coach's voice carries — flat, projected, indifferent to whether the room wanted to hear it.

Jin-seo turned to her. "The patch requires full read-write access to your enhancement system's firmware layer. Not temporary access. The installation rewrites the base-level architecture of your sensory processing, and to maintain that patch — to ensure compatibility with future firmware updates — Meridian needs to retain that access. Permanently."

"And what does permanent access include, exactly?"

Jin-seo picked up one of the printed binders from the table and turned to a marked page. "I'm going to read the relevant provision directly, because the language matters and I'd rather you heard it precisely than got a paraphrase." She looked down at the text. The room went still.

"'In consideration of the Installation and ongoing maintenance of the Meridian Apex Enhancement Stability Protocol, the Athlete hereby grants to Meridian BioSystems, its successors and assigns, an irrevocable, transferable license to all biometric, genetic, neural, and physiological data generated by or through the Enhancement System, including but not limited to real-time performance metrics, neurological activity patterns, genetic expression data, and baseline physiological markers. This license shall survive termination of any sponsorship agreement, retirement from competitive athletics, or deactivation of the Enhancement System.'"

She set the binder down on the table. She did not look away from the room.

Silence. Then a Swedish cyclist in the second row said, "What does 'transferable' mean? They can sell it?"

"It means Meridian can sublicense the data to research partners, medical institutions, or other authorized entities under the standard data-sharing framework."

"But they could sell it."

"The framework includes —"

"Yes or no."

"Yes."

A ripple moved through the room. Not gasps — something quieter and more calculating, thirty-four people adjusting the same equation at different speeds. Kai watched the back of Lena's head. She hadn't moved. Her hands lay flat on her thighs, still, in that way she held herself when she was processing with her entire body.

"What happens if we don't sign?" Aïssatou Diop said from near the front. The 400m sprinter from the Côte d'Ivoire team. She sat with her hands at her sides, one resting on the armrest, the other on the binder she hadn't opened.

"Then Meridian can't install the patch. Without the patch, you remain vulnerable to the virus. The probability of a sensory blackout during high-exertion performance increases with each calibration-network exposure. I can't give you a specific number, but the risk is non-trivial and it's escalating."

"Can we still compete?"

"The Apex Games don't require the patch. You can compete unpatched." She stopped. Not for dramatic effect — she stopped because the sentence she had prepared reorganized itself behind her eyes into something she wasn't willing to deliver as a talking point. "But a blackout during the hundred meters, or on the apparatus, or in the pool — it could be fatal. That's not a negotiating position. That's what the telemetry data shows."

Dr. Engel leaned forward in the front row. "The medical assessment supports that characterization," she said carefully. "The blackout risk during high-exertion states is real. I've reviewed the clinical data." She paused, and the pause had the quality of someone choosing each word as if placing it on a scale. "I'm not in a position to speak to the contractual terms. But the physiological danger is well-documented."

She sat back. It was the kind of clinical confirmation that was helpful and insufficient at the same time — a doctor telling you the surgery was necessary without saying anything about the bill.

That was enough for the first wave. A German javelin thrower — tall, blond, an enhancement scar visible at his collarbone where the muscle-graft integration hadn't quite healed flush — picked up his binder, turned to the signature page, and signed. He did it the way you sign a credit card receipt: quickly, without rereading, because the decision had been made somewhere between "irrevocable" and "fatal." He closed the binder and pushed it to the edge of his seat.

Two more within thirty seconds. Then four. Then three together near the back row, who seemed to have agreed on something without speaking. The scratch of pens on paper was small and specific — a sound that carried in the fluorescent silence. Twelve binders pushed to the edges of seats before the room spoke again.

---

Twenty-two athletes had not signed. They sat with closed binders on their laps or open binders turned face-down, and the room had reorganized itself without anyone moving — a visible fault line between those who had decided and those who hadn't. The air-handling system cycled, pushing warm air through overhead vents, and the draft lifted the corner of a binder page on an empty seat.

A middle-distance runner from the Nigerian team — Adewale, Kai was fairly sure — raised his hand. He held it up like they were in a lecture hall, which in a way they were. "Is there a sunset clause? Any provision for the license to expire?"

"No," Jin-seo said. "The license is irrevocable."

"And after retirement?"

"The license survives retirement."

"So when I'm forty-five and coaching kids somewhere and haven't competed in ten years, Meridian still holds a license to my neurological data."

"The data generated by and through the Enhancement System, yes. In perpetuity."

Adewale closed his binder. He set it on the floor between his feet with a care that made the action feel deliberate.

A gymnast from the Brazilian team — Kai didn't know her name, but he'd seen her on the uneven bars, fearless at four meters — asked without raising her hand: "The contract says 'baseline physiological markers.' That's not enhancement data. That's my body before any of this. My genetics. Things that have nothing to do with athletics."

"The license covers all data generated by or through the Enhancement System," Jin-seo said. "Because the system interfaces with your baseline biology, the data streams are technically inseparable."

"Technically."

"Yes."

The gymnast looked at her binder as though it had changed shape.

Lena raised her hand. She waited for Jin-seo to acknowledge her, then spoke in the precise, slightly formal cadence Kai was learning to recognize — sentences assembled in her second language with a structural care that native speakers rarely bothered with. "If I accept the patch and stop competing, and Meridian determines that my neurological patterns have research value I don't know about — can I withdraw the license?"

"The license doesn't include a withdrawal provision."

"So no."

"That's correct."

Lena nodded once. She didn't close her binder. She didn't open it either. Her hands returned to her thighs, flat and still.

Someone farther back, a voice Kai couldn't place: "You're asking us to sell our bodies to stay alive."

The room's atmosphere changed. Not because the statement was new — everyone present had arrived at some version of it in the past twenty minutes — but because someone had said it out loud, and saying it out loud drew a line across the carpet between the front table and the rows of chairs, between the people presenting and the people being presented to.

Jin-seo's hand went to the edge of the portfolio on the table. She touched it the way someone touches a railing — steadying, not gripping. She started to say something in her professional register and stopped. Her mouth opened and closed. When she spoke again her voice was lower and the corporate cadence was gone.

"In 2034, my brother was competing in the Asia-Pacific qualifiers. Triathlon. He was enhanced — Meridian system, same generation hardware as some of yours. During the cycling stage, his system entered a cascade failure." She looked at the room. "That's when the autonomous subsystems stop coordinating and start issuing contradictory commands. One leg was getting a fire signal on the quadriceps while the other was locking the hamstring. At forty kilometers an hour, on a road bike."

She wasn't reading notes. She wasn't performing this.

"He went over the handlebars. He broke his neck, three ribs, his pelvis. He was alive for nine hours after the crash. The cascade was still firing in his legs when they got him to the hospital. They couldn't stop it because they didn't have access to his firmware. Meridian's protocols at the time required a corporate authorization code to reach the base layer, and the nearest authorized technician was in Singapore. Jun-ho died at two in the morning in a hospital in Busan while a Meridian engineer was on a plane."

She picked up her water bottle, unscrewed it, and drank. Her hand was steady. She set the bottle down.

"They changed the access protocols after that. Obviously. The provisions in this contract are partly a consequence of his death — they ensure that in a medical emergency, the system can be reached immediately, without waiting for authorization from a technician on the other side of the Pacific." She looked at the room. "I know what you hear when I read 'irrevocable, transferable license.' I hear it too. But I also hear the nine hours my brother spent dying because nobody could get into his system to shut it down."

The room was quiet for a long time. The kind of quiet where you could hear the hum of the projection equipment and the wind against the windows two floors up. Kai looked at Margot against the far wall. Her arms were still crossed but her jaw had set, and she was looking at the floor — not at Jin-seo, not at the binders, at the floor — with an expression that Kai couldn't read and wasn't sure she wanted anyone to.

Yusuf, in the front row, had his head tilted at the angle Kai recognized from calibration sessions — the angle that meant he was thinking about a system, taking it apart in his head, looking for the piece that didn't fit. He was staring at the network schematic still glowing on the projection wall, and whatever he was thinking, he wasn't sharing it.

Then Kai heard, to his left and one row ahead, the sound of a binder opening. A pen cap popping.

He knew before he looked that it was Tomas.

Tomas signed with the practiced, economical motion of someone who had filled out official documents before — sponsorship contracts, visa applications, the orientation forms from Seoul, the Meridian compliance paperwork that came in quarterly. He wrote his name in block letters next to the signature line, printed the date, and closed the binder. His face was composed. Not resigned. Composed. The face of someone who had done arithmetic and arrived at a number he could live with.

Kai knew parts of that arithmetic. Tomas's sponsorship from Meridian covered training, equipment, travel, medical support, and a monthly stipend wired to an account in Quito that his mother drew from for rent and his siblings' school fees. Two younger siblings, both still in school. The sponsorship contract had provisions about compliance with Meridian's technical requirements — standard language, the kind of clause nobody read until it mattered. Refusing the patch and its terms wouldn't just mean competing with a virus in his system. It would mean breach of contract. It would mean the stipend stopping. It would mean his mother opening the banking app on her phone and finding the deposit that came on the fifteenth of every month simply absent.

Tomas set the binder on the empty seat beside him and folded his hands in his lap. He stared straight ahead at Jin-seo with an expression that held no accusation, no acceptance, no visible feeling at all. It was the expression of someone who had been offered a choice that wasn't one.

He caught Kai looking at him. Something passed between them — not understanding, not solidarity, something smaller and harder to name. Tomas was twenty-one and his family ate because of what his body could do. Kai was thirty and had savings and endorsement income and a wrist that was repairing itself without his permission. They were in the same room but not in the same conversation. They were in the same building having different emergencies.

A few more athletes signed after Tomas. His signing seemed to work as permission or resignation or simply evidence that it could be done — that you could write your name on the line and then sit back and still be yourself, at least for now. Four more binders closed and pushed to the edges of seats in the next two minutes.

Then the signing stopped. The fault line held. Eighteen athletes sat with binders closed or untouched.

Jin-seo checked her watch — the same unconscious touch from the start, her thumb on the face, a gesture that looked less like checking the time than confirming something was still there. "I'll be at the facility through Thursday. The contract can be signed at any point before the team departs for Doha. If you have questions, I'm available. One-on-one, if that's easier."

She gathered her portfolio. The two men from the delegation, who had sat silently through the entire session, stood and began collecting signed binders with the practiced efficiency of people whose job was collecting signed documents. One of them wore thin surgical gloves, which struck Kai as either hygienic or symbolic and either way made his stomach turn.

He looked down at the binder on his own lap. It was open to the signature page — he didn't remember opening it. The line was blank. His name was already printed beneath it in small type, waiting for a pen to make it binding. Kai Brennan. The same line length as the provisions above it, the ones that said irrevocable and transferable and survive termination. Just a line. Just a name. Just everything your body had ever generated or would generate, in perpetuity, for a patch that might keep you alive.

His left wrist throbbed. The bone grinding itself back together inside the brace, the grafts reknitting tissue on their schedule, the body remaking itself according to instructions he hadn't written and couldn't override. He closed the binder and pressed his right palm flat against the cover, holding it shut.

Outside the conference-room windows, the Alps were white and enormous under a sky that was trying to clear. Snow blew off the high ridges in thin veils, carried by wind that had nothing to do with anyone in this room.


# Chapter 9

Yusuf talked to himself when he worked. Not the self-conscious muttering of someone performing eccentricity, just a steady half-voiced narration of what his hands were doing, the way some people hummed. Fragments of logic, technical shorthand, the occasional "right" that served as both punctuation and encouragement. The calibration bay at two in the morning was the one place it didn't matter. The diagnostic terminals threw blue-white light across his face and the desk and the cold coffee he'd poured three hours ago from the machine in the corridor — the one that produced an aggressively bitter espresso from beans that seemed personally hostile to the concept of flavor.

He had the virus architecture mapped across three displays. The propagation mechanism was elegant, which was the problem. It threaded through the calibration network's handshake protocols — the standard exchange that occurred whenever two enhanced systems synced during a tuning session. Every time an athlete lay in the calibration rig and Yusuf ran a routine adjustment, the network opened a brief two-way data channel. The virus nested inside those channels like sediment in a pipe.

"So if I can interrupt the handshake before the payload deploys..." He scrolled through the firmware's authentication layer, his glasses reflecting the scroll of code. "No, because the authentication happens after the channel's already open. Horse is already out. Right."

The partial patch concept was simple in theory. Instead of Meridian's approach — a complete firmware rewrite requiring permanent read-write access to every enhancement system — his design targeted only the virus's propagation pathway. Seal the handshake exploit. Quarantine infected firmware modules. Leave everything else untouched. No data surrender. No permanent corporate access to anyone's biological information.

Simple in theory. In practice, he was trying to build a pressure seal for a submarine from the inside of the submarine while it was underwater.

The calibration network's architecture was Meridian's proprietary design, which meant Yusuf was working from reverse-engineered schematics and his own six years of institutional memory. He knew the system better than anyone at Kirchberg — he'd calibrated hundreds of athletes through it, felt its patterns and quirks the way a mechanic knows an engine by the pitch of its idle. But knowing a system was different from knowing its source code, and Meridian didn't share source code. They shared APIs and documentation that described what the system did, not how.

He was building the patch by inference. Probing the firmware's behavior, mapping its responses, constructing a model of the underlying architecture from observable output. Like reconstructing a building's blueprints by knocking on walls and listening to the echoes.

Outside the bay's single window, the floodlit car park was empty. Beyond it, the mountains were shapes against a sky that was slightly less dark than it had been an hour ago. The days were getting longer — imperceptibly, a few minutes each week, the late-winter light stretching toward something that wasn't quite spring but was no longer the deep Alpine dark of January. Yusuf noticed this the way he noticed most things that weren't the problem in front of him: peripherally, as data.

"Right. This bit handles sensory routing." He highlighted a block of code on the center display. "And this bit talks to the motor subsystem. So the handshake has to pass through... here. Or here. Shit."

Two possible interception points. The first was cleaner but sat upstream of the sensory processing stack — patching there might introduce latency. A tenth of a second, maybe less, between the world and the athlete's perception of it. For a sprinter at full speed, a tenth of a second was the difference between gold and nothing. For a swimmer mid-turn, it was the difference between a clean rotation and a collision with the wall. The performance cost would be real and measurable, and the athletes who chose this patch over Meridian's deal would be choosing it with their eyes open.

The second interception point was messier, deeper in the firmware, closer to the autonomous subsystem that governed involuntary motor function. Patching there would preserve sensory fidelity but might interfere with the safety protocols that kept synthetic muscle from firing at full force during, say, a stumble. The protocols that caught you when you tripped.

Neither option was clean. He sat with the second interception point and thought about what it meant to touch the firmware layer that decided when a body caught itself. The biological data flowing through these systems — the neural patterns, the motor mapping, the proprioceptive feedback loops — was exactly the kind of data that could map the early-stage neural deterioration patterns in conditions like Farah's. Nobody was using it for that. Nobody would be using it for that once Meridian locked it behind a corporate license and a transferable data contract that let them sell it to whoever offered the most.

He pushed the thought aside. Not because it didn't matter but because it mattered in a way that would contaminate his engineering judgment if he let it, and right now the engineering judgment needed to be clean.

He chose the first interception point. The performance cost was honest — a measurable tradeoff, something he could explain to an athlete in plain language. The alternative was gambling with safety protocols he couldn't fully predict.

His hands moved across the keyboard with the particular fluency of someone building in a language he thought in. The code took shape in fragments: a conditional check here, a routing exception there, a quarantine protocol that would intercept the virus payload during the handshake and dump it into a sandboxed partition instead of allowing it to integrate with the athlete's firmware. The quarantine design was borrowed from standard cybersecurity practice — isolate the threat, contain it, neutralize it without touching the host system. Simple principle. The execution required mapping every variant of the handshake exchange the calibration network could produce, and there were, by Yusuf's current count, at least forty-seven.

He'd mapped thirty-one.

The patch would also sever any bidirectional data pathway that the virus could exploit — which meant, as an unavoidable side effect, that the neural coaching feed interface would no longer function. The feed relied on exactly the kind of open channel the virus used for propagation. Closing one meant closing the other.

Margot would fight this. He already knew that. The performance cost was a technical argument. Losing the feed was personal, and Margot's version of personal had a quality he didn't want to examine too closely.

He saved his work to two locations: the facility's development environment, where it would look like routine calibration tool maintenance, and his personal device — the encrypted archive in his quarters, holding weeks of biological data he had no authorization to possess.

"Right," he said to the displays. "That's enough for one night."

It wasn't. He didn't leave.

He opened the virus code instead.

---

He'd been picking at the virus architecture for days, in stolen hours between calibration sessions and staff meetings and the careful maintenance of his professional composure. The surface structure he'd already mapped — the propagation mechanism, the sensory corruption patterns, the way it nested inside the handshake protocols like something designed to fit there.

Designed. That was the word that kept surfacing, and that he kept pushing back down.

The virus was sophisticated. Not in a showy way — no baroque complexity, no clever tricks for tricks' sake. Sophisticated the way a well-made tool was sophisticated: it did exactly what it needed to do, no more and no less. It propagated through the calibration network efficiently. It corrupted sensory processing selectively — targeting the input channels that governed sight, proprioception, and hearing while leaving the motor subsystem's autonomous functions intact. This meant the body kept moving during a blackout. The body kept running, swimming, jumping, doing whatever it had been doing at the moment the senses went dark.

This was, from an engineering perspective, an interesting design choice.

A natural virus — a mutation in the firmware, a cascade of corrupted data propagating through the network's shared infrastructure — would not be selective. It would corrupt whatever it reached, motor and sensory alike. An athlete hit by indiscriminate corruption would collapse: muscles seizing or going slack, the body shutting down mid-stride. Ugly. Painful. Unlikely to kill. The body would stop.

This virus did not stop the body. It blinded the pilot and left the machine running at full speed.

Yusuf had been thinking about this for three days. He'd been thinking about it the way he thought about all engineering problems: structurally, looking at the architecture rather than the symptoms. And the architecture told him something he did not want it to.

He opened the virus code at the byte level. Not the functional layer he'd been working with for the patch — the underlying implementation. The way the code was actually structured, beneath the behavior. He was looking at it now the way a typographer might examine a forged document: not reading the words but studying the letterforms, the spacing, the ink density.

Code had signatures. Development tools left traces in compiled output — memory allocation patterns, variable naming conventions, compiler artifacts, the particular way different toolchains handled error routines. These traces were usually invisible at the functional level. You had to go looking. They were fingerprints.

He pulled up the Kirchberg calibration system's firmware in a parallel window — Meridian firmware, maintained and updated quarterly by Meridian's development team, compiled with Meridian's proprietary toolchain. Standard reference material. Every engineer at every Meridian-affiliated facility had access to the compiled firmware. The source code was proprietary, but the compiled output was part of the working environment.

He put the virus's compiled output next to it.

Memory allocation. He checked the block sizes first — the fundamental units in which the code requested and managed system memory. Then alignment boundaries: the way data structures were spaced in memory, padded to fit the processor's preferred access patterns. Then the padding itself, the empty bytes inserted between data fields.

The pattern was the same.

Not similar. Not within the normal range of variance you'd expect from similar toolchains. The same. The same block sizes, the same alignment boundaries, the same padding behavior. The virus allocated memory the way Meridian's firmware allocated memory, because they were both built with the same development tools.

Yusuf's coffee sat untouched beside his right hand. The display light was giving his fingers a bluish cast, like they belonged to someone slightly less alive.

He checked the error-handling routines. Same nested exception hierarchy. Same logging format. Same method of handling buffer overflows — a particular approach that Yusuf had recognized years ago as Meridian house style, a standardized pattern their engineering team used across all their firmware products. It was efficient, well-documented internally, and distinctive enough that he'd been able to identify Meridian-compiled code in mixed environments just by reading the exception structure.

"Bollocks," he said quietly.

He kept going. Variable naming in compiled output was mostly stripped — the human-readable names replaced by machine addresses during compilation. But there were traces. Debug symbols that hadn't been fully cleaned from the production build. String references containing fragments of the original source paths. He found three. Two were generic — standard library references that could come from any development environment. The third was a partial directory path embedded in a debug trace: `/mbio/dev/tools/nlink/`.

mbio. Meridian BioSystems.

nlink. Neural link.

Yusuf took off his glasses and cleaned them on his shirt. The displays blurred into colored smears. He put the glasses back on and the code resolved into clarity, and he sat there looking at it.

This was not proof. He knew that with the precision of someone who had spent fifteen years in engineering and understood the difference between evidence and inference. Development tools could be stolen, leaked, pirated. An external actor could have obtained Meridian's toolchain and used it to build the virus — deliberately, to frame them, or incidentally, because Meridian's tools were genuinely good and widely available in certain underground channels. The debug path fragment could be a plant, or a remnant from a stolen development environment, or a coincidence. The memory allocation pattern was a toolchain artifact — it pointed to Meridian's tools, not necessarily Meridian's engineers.

Suggestive. Not conclusive. The distinction mattered because conclusive meant evidence and suggestive meant conspiracy theory, and the distance between those two things was the distance between being taken seriously and being dismissed as a paranoid engineer with personal reasons to distrust the company.

But.

If the virus was Meridian's work, then the data surrender contract wasn't a response to the crisis. It was the purpose of the crisis. Create the threat. Engineer the fear. Offer the solution. The solution happens to require permanent, irrevocable, transferable ownership of every athlete's biological data — the exact dataset Meridian had been trying to acquire through contract renegotiations that had stalled for two years before anyone at Kirchberg had ever heard of a calibration virus.

Yusuf did not say this out loud. He did not mutter it to the displays or whisper it into his cold coffee. He sat with it. The processing equipment hummed. The fluorescent tube nearest the window buzzed at a slightly different frequency from its neighbors, a mismatched ballast he'd reported to maintenance twice and that nobody had fixed. His hands, which had been steady all night, were very still on the keyboard.

He saved the analysis to his personal archive. He did not save it to the facility's development environment. He closed the virus code windows and opened his patch work instead, and for a long time he looked at the code without seeing it.

---

The door to the calibration bay opened at quarter past three. Yusuf didn't turn — the bay had a dozen entry points and technicians came and went at all hours for system checks. But the footsteps stopped, and the silence had the particular weight of someone standing still and watching.

"Dr. Qadir."

He turned. Petra Engel stood in the doorway with her clinic coat still on over a grey jumper he'd seen two days ago. Her hair, grey-streaked and usually pinned with architectural precision, was listing to one side in a way that suggested it had been taken down and put back up at least once since the original pinning. She had a paper cup from the corridor machine, which meant she'd made a stop on the way here and thought about what she was going to say while the machine dispensed its grievance.

"Dr. Engel." His voice shifted. He heard it happen — the Birmingham flattening out, the vowels tidying themselves, the professional register sliding into place. The tell. He knew it was a tell. He couldn't stop it. "Bit late."

"I've been reviewing facility access logs for the past week." She stepped into the bay and let the door close behind her. "Routine audit, given the circumstances."

"Sure."

"Your badge accessed this bay at eleven forty-seven tonight. It's now three fifteen." She took a sip from her cup and made a small expression of displeasure that might have been the coffee or might have been the situation. "You have no calibration sessions scheduled before eight tomorrow morning."

"Sometimes I work late. You know how it is."

"I do." She crossed the bay slowly, not approaching him directly but moving toward the secondary diagnostic station, putting herself in a position where she could see his displays. Yusuf did not minimize his windows. He'd already closed the virus analysis. What remained on screen was the patch code — explicable, if not exactly authorized.

Petra looked at the displays. She was a physician, not an engineer, but she'd spent a career working alongside calibration systems, and she read firmware architecture the way a literate adult reads a foreign language — not fluently, but enough to know what she was looking at.

"This isn't a standard calibration tool," she said.

"No."

"What is it?"

"A possible alternative approach to the virus." He kept his voice professionally warm, the register he used when explaining technical details to athletes during calibration. Measured, clear, slightly too polished. "I'm exploring whether we can target the propagation mechanism without requiring the full data access that Meridian's patch demands."

Petra was quiet for a moment. She pulled a stool from the secondary station and sat down, the movement slow and deliberate — no wasted motion, but no urgency either. She set her cup on the desk beside her and adjusted the stool's height, a small domestic gesture in the sterile bay, and Yusuf noticed that her hands were dry and cracked at the knuckles in the way of someone who washed them forty times a day and had stopped bothering with lotion.

"Exploring," she said. "On your own. Without institutional authorization. Using facility systems after hours."

"Yes."

"Is this the only unauthorized work you've been doing in this bay?"

The question landed with precision. Yusuf felt his face do something he couldn't control — a flinch in the muscles around his eyes, quick enough that most people would miss it. Petra was not most people. She was a physician who had spent decades reading faces for the micro-expressions that patients produced when they were about to minimize their symptoms.

"I've been running additional analyses on the virus code," he said. Clean, precise, technically true. The Birmingham was completely gone now. "The standard diagnostic approach isn't capturing the full picture."

"The access logs show you've been in this bay after hours on eleven of the past fourteen nights." She let that sit. "Including nights before the virus was formally identified."

The before. Eleven of fourteen nights. He could explain — he'd been tracking anomalies in the calibration network for weeks, correlated drift patterns across multiple athletes that had concerned him before anyone knew there was a virus. The explanation was true. It also opened a door to questions about what he'd been doing with those weeks of observation, and what he'd been doing with the data, and where the data was now.

"I've been concerned about network anomalies for some time," he said. "The virus diagnosis confirmed what I'd been tracking independently."

"And the data transfers to an unregistered device?"

His hands, which had been resting flat on the desk, went still. Not the stillness of composure. The stillness of a body that had decided to stop moving before the mind caught up, the way you freeze when you step on ice and feel the first shift.

"The system logs show external transfers from this station," Petra said. She wasn't accusatory. Clinical. "Small, regular, encrypted. IT security hasn't flagged them because they're focused on the virus response and the corporate delegation and the seventeen other things that are currently on fire. But eventually someone will audit the transfer logs the way I've audited the access logs, and they'll find them."

Yusuf looked at his hands on the desk. His glasses had slid down his nose. He pushed them up. The gesture gave him half a second.

"I've been backing up biological data," he said, and let the professional register drop. The Birmingham came back, and with it a looseness in his syntax that felt like setting down something heavy. "Athlete biometric data from calibration sessions. More than my job scope covers."

"Why?"

He could offer the professional justification. Redundancy, data integrity, concern about network stability. Petra would not believe it. She had come here at three in the morning with a paper cup of terrible coffee, which meant she'd been sitting on this for days, deciding what to do with it before deciding what to do about it.

"Personal research." He rubbed the bridge of his nose where his glasses sat. "There are applications for the biometric data that aren't being pursued. Neurological applications. Treatment research for degenerative conditions. Conditions that — yeah. It's personal. The data's there, it's being generated anyway, and nobody's using it because it doesn't serve Meridian's commercial priorities. And once that contract locks it down, nobody will ever use it."

"Personal research," Petra repeated. Not a question. A careful arrangement of words on a surface, looked at from different angles.

"Yeah."

She was quiet. She picked up her cup, looked into it, put it back down without drinking. Then she looked at the ceiling, where the fluorescent tube nearest the door was buzzing at its mismatched frequency.

"There was an athlete at the Stuttgart facility," she said. "Marius Koch. Decathlon. Twenty-four. His body rejected the synthetic muscle grafts — an immune cascade that the monitoring system classified as a calibration variance for eleven days before I escalated it." Her voice had lost its hedging. The qualifiers that usually softened her statements into clinical probability were gone. "I wrote three reports. I flagged the rejection markers. I recommended immediate removal of the grafts. The facility director forwarded my reports to Meridian's medical oversight team. Meridian reclassified the rejection response as within acceptable adaptation parameters and recommended continued training."

She paused. Not for effect. Because the next part was a fact she had carried for three years and it still needed a moment.

"Marius died fourteen days later. Systemic organ failure."

Yusuf didn't speak.

"I filed a formal complaint. The investigation concluded that protocols were followed. Protocols were, in fact, followed. The protocols were wrong. Marius was dead. The investigation closed." She looked at Yusuf, and the tiredness in her face wasn't the chronic background fatigue he was used to seeing. It was specific. The tiredness of someone who had been right and had watched it not matter. "I was overruled by people who had institutional reasons to minimize what the data was telling them."

"Petra—"

"I'm not going to report you."

The sentence was flat and simple. She said it the way she said medical facts — without cushioning, without invitation to discuss.

"I don't want to know the details of your personal research. I don't want to be in a position where I'm required to disclose information I haven't been given." She stood up from the stool. "What I will tell you is that the IT security team will audit those transfer logs. It may take two weeks. It may take two months. But they're competent and they're thorough, and they will find the transfers."

"I know."

"Then you should consider what you're going to do about that before they do."

She picked up her cup and walked toward the door. Stopped. She didn't turn around, but she wasn't leaving either. She was standing in the doorway the way someone stands when they've said what they came to say and now there's one more thing that wasn't part of the plan.

"If the alternative approach you're developing shows clinical promise," she said, "I'd want to see the data. Before it goes anywhere near a live system. That's not a request from the medical director. That's a request from a physician."

"Understood."

"Good night, Dr. Qadir."

"Night."

The door closed. The calibration bay returned to its hum, its fluorescent buzz, its blue-white light on the desk where Yusuf sat. His coffee was cold. His patch was sixty percent finished and the forty percent that remained was the part that could kill someone if he got it wrong. The virus code on his personal archive contained structural signatures that pointed, suggestively, inconclusively, damningly, toward the company that built the systems the virus was destroying and that had arrived within a week with a solution that happened to cost exactly what they'd been trying to buy for two years.

He couldn't prove it. He couldn't share it yet. He couldn't stop the shape of it from sitting in his head, solid and ugly, while the processing equipment hummed and the mountains outside the window emerged slowly from the dark as the sky began, just barely, to lighten.

He saved his work. He closed his displays. He sat in the bay for a while longer, in the hum, in the dark that wasn't quite dark anymore, and did not sleep.


# Chapter 10

The pool was empty except for Lena.

Margot settled into the coaching chair on the observation deck and pulled up the session display. Six forty-five a.m. The facility was still quiet — the sprinters didn't start until eight, the calibration bays were dark. Below the glass wall, the pool's surface was flat under the overhead lights, ten lanes of still water catching the fluorescent glare and throwing it back in long blue rectangles. Lena was on the far deck, pulling her cap on, tucking the last strands of hair behind her ears. The gesture exposed the neural link port at the base of her skull — a small circle, skin-colored, that disappeared again when she let her hair fall.

Margot strapped the feed interface to her left wrist. The connection pads found her pulse and warmed. She opened the session file on the console, logged Lena's athlete ID, and set the pacing target at 1:04 per hundred meters. Standard aerobic. Nothing that would flag.

She pressed connect.

The feed opened like a door into someone else's room.

First came the temperature — the air on Lena's skin, the deck concrete cool under her bare feet. Then proprioception flooded in: Lena's shoulders rotating in a warm-up stretch, the specific tension in her right latissimus dorsi from yesterday's session, the faint ache behind her eyes that was either fatigue or altitude. Margot knew it was altitude. She also knew it had been six weeks since Lena arrived, so altitude was a generous explanation.

Lena dove. The water hit Margot's borrowed sensorium as a wall of pressure and thermal shift — twenty-seven degrees, a degree warmer than the air, closing over Lena's body like a glove. Then the stroke began and Margot forgot everything else.

Lena's freestyle was not like other swimmers'. Margot had been on feeds with a dozen aquatic athletes over three years and none of them moved through water the way Lena did. The neural link processed hydrodynamic resistance as spatial information — the water's density rendered as a three-dimensional field that Lena's body navigated the way a bird navigates updrafts, adjusting angle and force and timing in continuous micro-corrections that no unenhanced swimmer could execute. Through the feed, Margot could feel each correction as it happened: the left hand entering the water two degrees steeper to catch a denser column, the hip rotating a fraction earlier to lengthen the pull, the kick adjusting its amplitude to match the stroke rate with the mechanical precision of matched gears.

It was beautiful. It was always beautiful.

Lena swam the first hundred meters in 1:03.8. Clean. The second hundred: 1:04.1. The telemetry on Margot's console showed a steady heart rate of 162, stroke rate of 68 per minute, neural link operating within normal parameters. Lena's flip turn at the two-hundred wall was textbook — wall contact, tuck, push, streamline, breakout at four meters.

The third hundred meters began and something changed.

Margot felt it before the data confirmed it. The feed carried a qualitative shift that was not in the numbers, not in the stroke rate or the heart rate or the lap time. It was in the texture of being inside Lena's body. The specific character of Lena's consciousness — the way her attention rested on the water as a medium to be read, the slight formality of her proprioceptive awareness, the deliberateness with which she initiated each stroke — began to thin.

Margot had felt flickers of this in earlier sessions. A moment of flatness, like a television losing signal for a frame. She'd noted it in the logs as minor telemetry drift and told Lena it was within acceptable parameters. It had been easy to believe because the flickers were brief and the swimming continued and the data looked clean.

This was not a flicker.

Lena's personality was leaving. Not fading — evacuating, the way air leaves a room when you open both doors. Margot could feel it happening through the feed in real time: the interior architecture of a specific person collapsing inward, the textures that made Lena *Lena* — her precision, her attentiveness, her awareness of the water as something she negotiated with rather than moved through — smoothing out into something featureless and fast. What took Lena's place was optimization. The neural link running the body the way a program runs a machine. Perfect stroke mechanics executing without hesitation, without doubt, without the wasteful friction of a person deciding to move.

The stroke rate climbed. Seventy per minute. Seventy-two. Lena's body was accelerating without anyone telling it to. The system had found its own pacing, independent of Margot's target, independent of Lena's intent, because there was no intent to be independent of. The system was alone in there.

Through the feed, the experience was — Margot couldn't have named it under oath. She'd spent three years chasing intensity through borrowed bodies. Tomás's hurdle clearance, the hamstrings loading and the explosive drive over the bar. The gymnast's spatial awareness during a release move, the body rotating in air with a certainty that felt like faith. The sprinters' anaerobic rush, the final twenty meters when the lungs are done and the legs are running on chemical afterburn. All of those were athletes pushing their enhanced bodies to the limit, and the feed translated the effort into borrowed sensation that Margot lived off the way a leech lives off blood.

This was nothing like effort. This was the boundary between person and system dissolving in real time, and Margot was at the edge of it, feeling both sides simultaneously. On one side: the ghost of Lena's presence, thinning to a residue. On the other: the system's optimization, seamless, efficient, devoid of any quality that could be called human. The water moved past Lena's body like a negotiated surrender. Every stroke was perfect because nothing was in the way.

Margot pushed the pacing target to 1:00.

She did this with her right hand on the console touchscreen, her left arm extended on the armrest with the feed interface pulsing warm against her wrist. The target update would reach Lena's neural link and adjust the system's pacing demands. Whether Lena would process the adjustment as a conscious decision to swim faster was — well. Lena wasn't processing anything.

Heart rate on the console: 178. 182.

Through the glass wall, Margot could see Lena's body cutting through the water. The stroke looked the same from above — long, clean, the catch and pull and recovery that Margot had coached for six weeks. From the observation deck, you'd think the swimmer was fine. Just fast. Just in the zone.

Through the feed, Margot knew the swimmer was empty.

185. The console flagged it. A yellow border around the heart rate number, the system's protocol marking sustained elevation above the aerobic threshold. The flag would show in the session log.

Margot's own breathing had quickened. She was sitting in the coaching chair on a dry deck above a pool, watching through glass, and her chest was moving with borrowed exertion. The feed translated Lena's cardiac output as a thick rhythmic pressure in Margot's sternum — not her heart, Lena's heart, the muscular contraction arriving through the neural interface as a felt event in Margot's chest. It was fast and strong and running without anyone at the controls.

She pushed the target to 0:58.

The system complied. Lena's stroke rate hit seventy-six. The flip turn at the three-hundred wall was explosive — the push-off drove Lena to breakout in under two seconds, the legs firing with a force that Margot felt as compression and heat in her own phantom quads. Through the feed, Lena's body was a single coordinated event: arms pulling, core rotating, legs driving, lungs cycling air with metronomic regularity. All of it synchronized, all of it purposeful, all of it unmanned.

188. 190.

Margot watched the heart rate number turn red. The system upgraded the flag to a protocol alert — sustained heart rate above 190 during a programmed aerobic session, no interval justification in the session plan. The alert would generate a notification to the medical team unless it was closed within sixty seconds.

She closed it.

The override authority was built into the feed interface — coaches could dismiss false-positive alerts during interval sessions, sprints, and high-intensity blocks. Margot used the override the way she always used it: as permission she'd granted herself.

193.

One more lap. Just one more. The feed was immaculate. The system's optimization had reached a plateau of efficiency that felt like floating in warm current — Lena's body moving through water without effort, without choice, without self, and Margot riding the sensation like a wave she would never generate on her own. Her own lungs hadn't worked at this capacity in nine years. Her own legs hadn't driven through water at competition intensity since the 2028 Olympics, where she'd touched the wall fifth and walked out of the natatorium and never gone back. Through the feed, she was in a body that could do what hers couldn't, and the body wasn't even occupied.

195.

Margot pulled the target back to 1:02.

She did this because at 195 the risk was cardiac, and even through the feed, even in the middle of the best session she'd ever had, Margot was still someone who had trained as an athlete and knew what happened to a heart above 195. It threw extra beats. It stuttered. It could stop.

The system adjusted. Stroke rate dropped. Heart rate began the slow plateau — 193, 191. Still red. Margot closed the second alert.

Lena reached the four-hundred wall and stopped.

She didn't touch the wall and turn. She reached it and hung there, one hand on the gutter, the other on the lane rope. Her shoulders heaved. Her cap had shifted, hair escaping along one side. Her mouth was open, pulling air in rough gasps that Margot could hear through the glass.

Through the feed, the reentry was sudden and ugly. Like watching a room refill with air — the flatness cracking open and something rushing back in, jagged and disorganized. Lena's consciousness returning to her own body, and the return was not smooth. It was confusion first, then a swell of something that the feed translated as cold despite the warm water, then the physical awareness of pain: her chest, her arms, her legs all registering an exertion that she had not been present to experience.

Margot disconnected.

The feed interface went dark. The warmth on her wrist died. Lena's body — the water, the muscle load, the heart's hammering pressure — all of it dropped away in a single neural cliff, and what remained was Margot in a chair on a dry deck, breathing her own air with her own lungs, weighing her own weight on her own bones.

Her hands were shaking. She pulled the interface off her wrist and set it on the console. The skin underneath was flushed and slightly raised where the connection pads had made contact. She pressed her thumb into the mark and held it there, the way she used to press a bruise after hard training — testing whether it still hurt, confirming she could still feel it.

Below the glass, Lena hadn't moved. She was hanging on the lane rope with both arms, her face half in the water, her back rising and falling. A full minute passed. Then Lena's head turned, slowly, scanning the deck as if she didn't recognize it. The pool was empty. Dara's session wasn't until nine. There was no one to ask.

"What lap is this?"

Lena said it out loud. To no one. To the water. Her voice came through the observation deck's audio pickup, thin and flattened by the pool's tile acoustics.

Margot sat still and watched.

After another thirty seconds, Lena pulled herself to the wall and climbed out. Her legs were unsteady on the deck. She sat on the pool edge with her feet in the water, her back curving forward, her elbows on her knees. She looked like someone who'd been woken from anesthesia and was trying to reconstruct the room.

Margot saved the session file. Heart rate peaked at 195 — the override closures were logged but required no further action unless someone audited the session manually. The telemetry showed a training swim with a moderate elevation in the middle split, attributable to intensity. The desync signatures were buried in the neural link data, visible if someone pulled the raw stream and ran a pattern analysis, invisible in the summary dashboard. Nobody would run the pattern analysis because nobody knew to look for it. Margot had been the one running the desync check in earlier sessions. She'd been the one who told Lena the drift was within parameters.

She closed the console, put the interface in her bag, and stood up. Her legs felt heavy and disconnected — the post-feed drop, the body remembering that it belonged to a thirty-five-year-old woman who hadn't competed in almost a decade. The observation deck smelled of chlorine and recycled air. The glass wall in front of her showed the pool, bright and still, and Lena sitting on the edge with her hand on the back of her neck, fingers pressing the neural link port the way she always pressed it — unconsciously, the way someone touches a tooth that aches.

Margot knew what she should do. Walk down to the deck. Tell Lena the session showed a sustained desync event — not drift, not parameters, but a genuine vacancy of consciousness lasting multiple laps. Flag it for Petra. Send the raw neural data to Yusuf, who was in the calibration bay building a patch that was supposed to fix exactly this and who needed to know how bad it had gotten if the patch was going to work. Make a call that protected the athlete, because that was what the coaching position existed to do.

She walked out of the observation deck and down the corridor toward her quarters.

The walk took four minutes. Linoleum floor, fluorescent ceiling panels, the smell of the facility: antiseptic, recycled air, faint coffee from the cafeteria two corridors over. She passed a sprinter stretching in the hallway, a woman she didn't know, young, both calves showing the faint surgical seams of graft integration. The woman nodded. Margot nodded back.

In her room, she sat on the bed and took the feed interface out of her bag. The device was the size of a thick watch — brushed steel housing, the connection pads on the underside still warm from her skin. She'd bought it four years ago when she transitioned from competition to coaching. The vendor had called it a NeuroSync Performance Monitor. Margot had known what it was. A way to feel a body that performed. A way back inside.

She turned the device over in her hands. The connection pads were faintly discolored from years of use — her wrist oils, her pulse, her heat. She put it in the nightstand drawer and closed the drawer.

She lay back on the bed and stared at the ceiling. The diagnostic display on the wall showed her vitals: heart rate 84, slowly settling. Respiration normal. Enhancement status: none. That line was always blank. Margot had never been enhanced. She'd competed in the last years of the Traditional Games, the 2028 Olympics in Brisbane, and she'd swum the 200 butterfly in a body that was entirely hers and that body had placed fifth.

The ceiling was white. The room was quiet. The feed session was twenty minutes behind her and the absence of it was already spreading through her like cold water filling a warm pool.

She knew Lena's desync was dangerous. She knew the two-lap vacancy was not drift, not parameters, not an artifact of altitude adjustment. She knew that a twenty-two-year-old swimmer who lost consciousness while her body continued performing at a heart rate of 195 was in medical danger that was not theoretical, not eventual, but immediate. She knew that the Kirchberg desync protocol required flagging a Class III event within the hour and grounding the athlete until cleared.

She also knew that flagging it meant Petra would pull the session log. The log would show the feed connection — duration, depth, the full seven minutes Margot had stayed in when the protocol called for disconnection at the first sign of Class II drift. It would show the pacing target increases. It would show the override closures on the heart rate alerts. It would show that Margot Voss, neural performance coach, had pushed a desyncing swimmer's heart rate to 195 while that swimmer was not present in her own body to consent, to resist, or to be afraid.

The drawer stayed closed. Her hands had stopped shaking. The flatness was settling in — the familiar post-feed emptiness that she'd stopped trying to name and started just enduring, the hours between sessions when her body was only hers and that wasn't enough.

She closed her eyes.

---

Lena spent the afternoon trying to find the two laps.

She sat at the desk in her quarters with the session data on the wall display — the telemetry Margot had filed after the morning swim. Stroke rate, split times, heart rate, neural link activity. The graphs were continuous. The heart rate showed a moderate elevation in the second half of the set, flagged by the system and attributed to training intensity. No anomalies in the neural link summary. No desync markers.

The data said she had swum four hundred meters without interruption.

Lena remembered two hundred and fifty.

The warm-up. The first hundred meters — smooth, the catch feeling clean, the water receiving her. The second hundred, the turn at the wall, the push-off, the breakout. The beginning of the third hundred. The flip turn at 250.

Then she was on the lane rope. Chest heaving so hard she could feel her pulse in her ears, in her wrists, in the spaces between her ribs. The far wall of the pool impossibly bright under the lights, and she didn't know where she was in the set. Not hazy. Not confused. Absent. She'd been lifted out of the session and set back down at a different point, and the distance between those two points did not exist as experience.

She had said something out loud. *What lap is this.* To the pool, because there was no one else, and the question was not rhetorical.

The telemetry said her body had swum those laps. Her arms had pulled, her legs had kicked, the flip turns had executed, the wall contacts had registered on the timing system. Her heart had been beating at 190. Her lungs had been cycling air. All of it had happened. She had not been there for any of it.

She'd felt something like this before — the evening in her quarters, weeks ago, when twenty minutes had passed while she stared at a paragraph without reading it. She'd come back to herself without any sense of having left. And before that, the training session where her flip turn had executed a rotation pattern from last season's firmware — a motor decision her neural link had made without her. Those had been small. A flicker. A moment. Margot had looked at the data and said *within acceptable parameters,* and Lena had accepted that because Margot had the data and the authority and Lena trusted both.

This was not a flicker. Two laps. At least three minutes, probably longer. Her body in the water, performing at competition intensity, while Lena was nowhere.

She touched the neural link port at the base of her skull. The implant's ceramic edge was smooth under her fingertip, skin-temperature, seamless with the bone beneath. She'd had it since she was four years old. It was as familiar as her own vertebrae. She pressed her thumb hard against the port and held it there, feeling the slight depression where the surface met the bone, as if pressure could tell her whether the thing was still hers.

She pulled up the Kirchberg desync protocol on the display. She'd read it before — everyone had, after the virus scare. Moderate desynchronization, Class II: intermittent divergence between neural link motor commands and conscious intent, duration under five seconds. Report to medical staff within 24 hours. Severe desynchronization, Class III: sustained divergence exceeding ten seconds, loss of voluntary motor control, or loss of conscious awareness during link-directed activity. Report to medical staff immediately. Ground the athlete. Cease all linked activity until cleared.

Three minutes was not a Class II event.

She closed the protocol and stood up. The room was small — bed, desk, bathroom, the wall display glowing softly with her numbers. Heart rate 72. Respiration normal. Neural link status: nominal. All the numbers said she was fine.

She found Margot in the corridor outside the coaching suite. Margot was coming from the cafeteria with a paper cup of coffee, her coaching bag over one shoulder, her hair down. She looked up when Lena came around the corner and her face did the coach scan — the quick assessment, automatic, reading the athlete for damage or fatigue or hesitation. It took less than a second.

"Hey," Margot said.

"Can I ask about this morning?"

"Sure."

They were standing in the corridor. Fluorescent light, linoleum floor, the low hum of the pool ventilation system audible through the walls. A calibration technician passed them pushing a cart of equipment, and they stepped aside to let it through. The cart's wheels squeaked. Margot sipped her coffee and waited.

"Something happened in the water," Lena said. "During the set. I can't remember the last part of the session."

She watched Margot's face. The scan was over. Margot's expression had settled into a careful neutral that could have been concern or patience or nothing.

"Can't remember how?" Margot said.

"I don't mean unclear. I mean it's not there. I remember the flip turn at two-fifty, and then I'm on the lane rope and the set is done. There's nothing between."

"Your telemetry looked clean," Margot said. The coffee cup was at her mouth, not quite drinking. "Heart rate was a little high in the middle split, but that's intensity. Your stroke was solid."

"I've been at this altitude for six weeks."

"Altitude's cumulative. Your body adjusts and readjusts — it doesn't stabilize linearly. Everyone's baselines shift."

"You were on the feed." Lena said it as a fact. "You'd have felt what was happening."

"I felt your stroke and your pacing. That's what the feed monitors. Your stroke was clean, Lena. Honestly, you looked strong."

"It doesn't feel like altitude."

"What does it feel like?"

The question was open and unhurried. The kind of question a good coach asks — giving the athlete room, not leading, not dismissing. Margot was good at this part. She'd always been good at this part.

"Like I wasn't there," Lena said. She heard the words and they were wrong — too simple for what had happened. "Like something removed me and put me back."

She didn't have language for it that was more precise. The absence was total. Not unconsciousness, which she'd experienced once during a childhood procedure and which had a quality of darkness and return. This had no quality at all. No darkness. No blur. No sense of duration. Just a cut in the film of her own experience, and on either side of the cut her body had been swimming and she had not.

"The desync markers in your session data don't show anything past the drift we've been tracking," Margot said. "Same range as last month. Yusuf's got the numbers. If it was spiking outside parameters, the system would've flagged it and it didn't."

"This isn't the same as the drift."

"It can feel bigger than the data shows. Especially right now, with everyone on edge about the virus. A sprinter came to me yesterday convinced her visual tracking was failing. Turned out she was dehydrated."

"I'm not dehydrated."

"I'm not saying you imagined it. I'm saying the session data doesn't match a Class III event. If it did, I'd pull you right now. You know that."

Lena did know that. She knew that Margot was direct and that she didn't waste language on reassurance she couldn't back up.
