Margot had been the one who flagged the desync in the first place, weeks ago — the telemetry divergence in the post-session review, the slight decoupling between Lena's conscious motor commands and the neural link's execution. She'd identified it, documented it, and told Lena it was within the parameters that Kirchberg and Meridian both considered acceptable for her level of enhancement integration. Lena had believed her because Margot had the data, and Lena had been raised to trust data. The Meridian developmental program had given her that: a faith in numbers that ran deeper than faith in sensation.

"Do you want me to flag it?" Margot said. "I can add a note to the session file and send it to Petra. She'll probably want a full diagnostic — you'd be off the water for two days minimum while they image the link. Maybe longer. Your call."

She offered it plainly. No inflection pushing Lena toward or away from the choice. Just the option, laid out with the consequences attached.

Lena thought about two days out of the water. The diagnostic — lying in the calibration rig while Yusuf's equipment mapped her neural link, the stillness of being read by machines. The results that would either confirm what her missing memory already told her or produce a clean report that contradicted her experience. She thought about what it meant if the diagnostic found something — Class III, grounded, her training block interrupted with the Apex Games four months away. She thought about what it meant if the diagnostic found nothing. If the data said she was fine and her memory said she wasn't, and she had to decide which to believe.

She had always believed the data. The data was what the Meridian program had taught her to trust — the numbers, the metrics, the objective record that existed outside the unreliability of feeling. When she was fourteen and thought she was swimming badly, the coaches had shown her the splits and the splits had been good. When she was nineteen and thought her stroke was changing, the biomechanical analysis had confirmed that the neural link's micro-corrections were within tolerance. The data had always been right and her feelings had sometimes been wrong, and she had learned the lesson the program intended: the system knows your body better than you do.

"I'll think about it," Lena said.

"OK." Margot took a sip of the coffee. The movement was small and ordinary and it closed the conversation — the cup coming down, Margot's posture shifting from attentive stillness back to the loose readiness of someone between tasks. "Let me know."

"Yeah."

"I'll be around."

Margot walked past her down the corridor. Lena heard her footsteps on the linoleum — even, unhurried, the steps of someone who had given an honest answer and moved on.

Lena stood in the corridor for a moment after Margot turned the corner. The ventilation hum was louder here, closer to the pool. Through the wall, she could hear the faint slap of water — someone had started a session in the far lanes. Dara, probably. Nine o'clock.

She walked back to her quarters. The corridor was long, the overhead lights steady, the floor reflecting a dull sheen. The mountains were visible through the window at the end of the hall — late-afternoon light on the ridge, the lower slopes dark with snowmelt and exposed rock. The days were getting longer. She could see it in the angle of the shadows on the peaks.

In her room she sat on the bed and looked at the wall display. Heart rate 68. Neural link status: nominal. The numbers were clean and steady and they told her she was fine.

She put her hand on the back of her neck. The port was there. Smooth, warm, hers since before she could remember. The link was how she processed the water. How she felt her stroke, calibrated her turns, understood the pool as a space her body could negotiate. It was the infrastructure of everything she had ever done as an athlete. It had been in her skull for eighteen years, longer than most of her memories, and she had never once had reason to think of it as something separate from herself.

Two laps. Her body in the water, her arms pulling, the walls passing, the turns executing. All of it happening. None of it hers.

She didn't flag the session.

She told herself she'd decide in the morning, that she'd sleep on it, that the data would still be there. She told herself that Margot had seen the telemetry and the telemetry was clean and Margot would know if something was wrong. She told herself these things the way the program had taught her — by trusting the system, by believing the numbers, by accepting that the infrastructure she'd been built around was more reliable than the feeling that something was missing.

Outside, the last of the afternoon light left the mountains. The snow on the upper peaks turned blue, then grey. Lena sat on her bed with her hand on the port and watched the light go.


# Chapter 11

The first time was in his room, because her room had a window that faced the floodlit outdoor track and neither of them wanted to see it.

His diagnostic display was on standby — a dim green pulse on the wall, the numbers minimized to a single status bar. Heart rate, bridge latency, graft integration. All normal. He'd stopped checking it every hour about a week ago, which probably counted as progress.

Lena sat at the head of the bed with her knees drawn up and her phone facedown on the mattress. She'd come over after the pool. Her hair was still damp, the chlorine smell so constant on her that he'd stopped noticing it except when she was close. They'd been talking about nothing — a movie someone had put on in the common room, whether the cafeteria coffee was getting worse or they were all just adjusting to the altitude — and the conversation had run down the way conversations do when neither person wants to leave but neither has a reason to stay that they're ready to name.

He was sitting at the other end of the bed. His left wrist was on his knee, the ache doing its low-grade thing, the bone reminding him it had been broken and reassembled on a schedule the rest of his body hadn't agreed to. The scar tissue on his cheek had smoothed to a pink shine. Six weeks ago he'd been bleeding on the track with his legs still twitching. Now he was sitting on a narrow institutional bed with a woman who smelled like chlorine, and the distance between those two states had no name he could find.

"You're staring at your wrist," Lena said.

"I'm not staring."

"You're looking at it the way you look at the display." She tilted her head toward the wall-mounted screen. "Checking."

He flexed his fingers, felt the ache shift from the wrist into the base of his thumb. "It does this thing where it almost feels normal, and then it reminds me it isn't."

"Almost normal," she said. "I know that one."

He looked at her. She was watching him with the kind of attention she usually gave to things she was trying to measure — the distance to a wall, the temperature of the water. Clinical, but not cold. Interested. He liked being looked at by her, which was a simple thing to feel and he let himself feel it.

"Come here," she said. Not a command. Information about what she wanted.

He moved up the bed. The mattress was narrow — built for a single athlete sleeping alone, not for two people trying to figure out how much space to leave between them. They ended up close enough that he could feel the heat off her shoulder. She reached over and picked up his left hand, turned it over, ran her thumb along the inside of his wrist where the line from the brace had faded to a thin white mark. The touch registered twice. The first was the raw signal — her thumb, his skin, the pressure and warmth of it. The second came a fraction behind, the system's interpretation: temperature mapped to two decimal places, pressure distributed across his wrist's surface area, her pulse detectable through her fingertip. He was used to the echo. Three years. But he noticed it now, here, because the second signal carried information about another person's body that he had not asked for and did not want.

He kissed her. That was also simple. She tasted like the peppermint tea from the cafeteria and her lips were slightly chapped from the pool chemicals, and those were details that belonged to him — things he noticed, not things the system measured and reported back. She kissed him back, and for a few minutes nothing was complicated.

Then it was. Not badly, not yet. He pulled her shirt over her head and she lifted her arms to help and it caught on her left ear and she said "ow" and laughed, and he laughed, and that was human enough. Her body was a swimmer's body — shoulders wider than her hips, muscle layered across her back in the specific architecture of lats and traps built by years of pull. He'd known this about her in the abstract. Touching it was different. His hand moved along her ribs and he could feel the individual muscles between the bones, the way her breathing expanded her ribcage laterally more than frontally. The system processed this too, feeding him biomechanical data about her thoracic expansion pattern that he did not need and could not stop receiving. He needed to know that she was breathing and that his hand was on her body and that both of those things were good. The rest was noise.

She unbuttoned his shirt with the deliberate precision she brought to most tasks — one button, then the next, her fingers working efficiently, which made him smile because most people either fumbled with buttons or turned them into performance and Lena just undid them like they were a problem she intended to solve correctly. When the shirt was open she put her palm flat against his sternum and held it there. He didn't know what she was checking. Maybe just that he was warm.

"Your heart rate is elevated," she said. Almost clinically.

"Yeah," he said. "That's happening."

She smiled. One of her small smiles, the kind that arrived and left without making a production of itself. He kissed the corner of it and felt her hand slide up to his shoulder — the right one, the one that had been dislocated — and when she pressed the joint he winced before he could stop himself.

"Still sore?"

"Little bit."

She lightened her grip but didn't move her hand. They were on their sides now, facing each other on the narrow mattress, and the logistics of the bed meant they were either touching everywhere or falling off. They touched everywhere.

He kissed the side of her neck and his mouth moved down to where her hair fell damp against her shoulder, and he pushed the hair aside. His thumb found the neural link port at the base of her skull — a small hard circle, body-warm, the engineered rim flush with the surrounding skin. She didn't flinch. It was as much a part of her body as the vertebrae below it. He traced the edge of it with his fingertip and the system told him the port's diameter and surface temperature and material composition, and he stopped listening to the system and kissed the skin beside the port instead, and she made a sound that was quiet and human and not available for diagnostic interpretation.

What happened next was not graceful because first times are not graceful, and the bed was too narrow, and at one point Kai's elbow hit the wall-mounted diagnostic display and it blinked from standby to active, throwing his vitals across the screen in green text — heart rate 118, neural bridge latency nominal, right shoulder ROM 94% — until Lena reached over his arm and switched it off without looking at it, which was the most thoughtful thing anyone had done for him in months. Then it was just the dim light from the hallway under the door and the faint mountain dark through the window and their bodies figuring out the logistics of a bed that had never been designed for this.

She was on top of him because his shoulder couldn't take his weight for long, and her knees were on either side of his hips, and his hands were on her waist, and this was the part where the body-autonomy question stopped being a calibration report.

The dual-track sensation was constant. Every point of contact delivered twice — the raw physical signal, then the system's cleaned-up version a fraction behind, like a translator who couldn't stop correcting the original. Her thighs against his hips: first the warmth and pressure and friction of skin, then the system's readout of contact area, force distribution, her muscle tension patterns mapped with clinical precision. He tried to stay with the first signal, the raw one, the one that belonged to him. It wasn't easy. The system didn't turn off because you wanted it to.

He'd never had sex with the echo before. Three years with the neural bridge and he had felt its doubling in every handshake, every training drill, every time someone bumped his shoulder in the cafeteria line. But here the doubling was different because the stakes of the raw signal were different. When she leaned down and kissed him, her hair fell against his face, wet at the tips, and the system reported the water's chlorine concentration and her exhaled CO2 level while his mouth was on hers. The clinical track ran underneath everything like closed captioning he couldn't disable — technically accurate, describing the same event, and not the same event at all.

His hands were on her hips and she was moving and he was moving with her and his right hand — the hand that worked fine, that had never been broken, connected to the same spinal neural bridge that governed everything below his neck — shifted. He didn't tell it to. His fingers spread slightly, redistributed across her hip, the grip adjusting from loose to firm with a mechanical smoothness that recalibrated the contact for optimal stability. The adjustment took less than a second. It was ergonomically perfect. His hand found the exact angle that held her weight evenly, that supported her movement without restricting it, and it did this the way a system does things: efficiently, without effort, without him.

He kept moving because she was moving and his body had gotten very good at continuing to perform things he had stopped commanding. But the system's signal was suddenly louder than the raw one, and the hand on her hip felt less like something he was doing and more like something he was watching from inside his own arm.

His hand had touched another person's body with a competence he did not generate.

It lasted a second. Maybe two. His fingers on her hip, holding her with a precision that was not his, while the rest of him tried to locate the boundary between the grip he'd intended and the grip the system had decided on. He couldn't find it. The muscle was his muscle. The nerve was his nerve. The bridge connecting them just happened to be faster and better at this than he was.

He moved his hand deliberately — loosened the grip, put his palm flat against her side, pressed it there with conscious force. That was him. He felt the decision travel from his brain down his arm and arrive in his fingers with the slight delay that meant a human being had made it.

Lena's breathing changed. She slowed. He looked up at her face and she was looking at him, and the quality of her attention had shifted. Not suspicion. Something closer to inquiry. Like she had registered the adjustment and was cataloguing it.

"I'm OK," he said. It came out flatter than he meant it to.

She nodded and kissed him and they kept going, and for a while it was fine, or close to fine, or close enough that they could both decide to treat it that way.

Then it wasn't fine.

It happened at an ordinary moment. Not a peak, not a crisis. She was beneath him — they'd shifted, slowly, carefully around his shoulder — and her hands were on his back and her eyes were open and she was present, fully, looking at him with an attention that was specific and warm and hers. And then she wasn't.

There was no sound, no seizure, no visible malfunction. She went still. Her hands stayed on his back but the pressure behind them went flat, like someone had stopped squeezing a held object. Her eyes didn't close. They stayed open, stayed pointed at him, but the person behind them — the particular quality of attention that was Lena, the sharpness and precision and faintly clinical interest and the warmth underneath all of it — went away. What was left looked like her. It had her face, her body, her hands on his back. But the presence was gone. The eyes looking at him were looking without seeing, the way a window looks at a street.

Kai stopped. He was still above her, weight on his left arm, right shoulder aching, and the woman underneath him was physically unchanged and completely absent.

"Lena?"

Nothing. Her eyes were open. She was breathing. Her body was warm. No one was home.

Two seconds. Three. He didn't know what to do. Pull away, touch her face, call someone. The options lined up in his mind and stayed there, equally available, equally useless. He stayed where he was. His arms held his weight. His right shoulder sent a slow ache up into his neck. These were his — the staying, the holding, the hurting — and he held onto them because they were decisions he had made.

Then she came back. He saw it happen. The eyes first — the quality of light behind them changed, like a lamp coming on in a room that had gone dark. Then her hands, which pressed into his back with renewed intention. Then her face, which registered where she was and what was happening and arranged itself into something that was not quite confusion and not quite fear but had the texture of someone finding themselves in a place they didn't remember entering.

"Hey," he said. His voice came out rough. "Hey. You went somewhere."

She blinked. Looked at him. Looked at her own hands on his back, as though confirming they were hers.

"How long?" she said.

"Few seconds."

She was quiet. He rolled off her, carefully, and lay beside her on the narrow bed. They weren't touching except at the shoulder, the mattress too small for distance but too narrow for pretense.

"Few seconds," she repeated. Not a question.

"Yeah."

She stared at the ceiling. He stared at the ceiling.

She took a slow breath and released it. Then another. The rhythm of someone re-establishing inventory — checking that the body answered to the right name. He recognized it because he'd done the same thing in the medical bay after the blackout, the same deliberate breathing, the same careful audit of what was still there.

"I'm here," she said. To herself as much as to him.

"I know."

The hallway light under the door made a thin gold bar on the floor. Outside, wind moved against the window with the flat, constant pressure of altitude.

---

Neither of them slept. They put their clothes back on — not all of them, enough — and lay in the dark. The diagnostic display stayed off. The wind outside was steady and without opinion. Somewhere down the hall a door closed softly. The calibration bay's low hum was there if you listened for it — always there, the base frequency of a building designed around machines that tuned human bodies.

"Are you cold?" he said.

"A little."

He pulled the blanket higher over both of them. The fabric was the same thin institutional weave as everything else in the athletes' quarters — designed for function, not comfort.

Kai flexed his left hand under the blanket, opening and closing his fingers. The wrist ache was there. It was always there. It sat in the bone like a tenant who had paid up and wasn't leaving.

"I should tell you what it's like," Lena said. She was on her back with her hands folded on her stomach, her hair drying now, the chlorine smell fading into something warmer and more her. She said it the way she said most things — a decision she'd made and was now carrying out.

"You don't have to."

"I know I don't have to. I want to tell you."

He waited. She was quiet for long enough that he thought she might have changed her mind, but she was composing, not retreating. Lena composed her sentences before she delivered them. He'd learned this.

"I don't pass out," she said. "Everyone asks that. They want to know if it's like fainting. It isn't. When you faint, your body stops functioning. Mine doesn't stop. It functions better."

She turned her head to look at him. The dim light from the hallway caught the edge of her jaw.

"It's like being set aside. Very gently. Something eases me out of the way — not forcefully, nothing hurts — and fills the space where I was. And whatever fills it is more efficient than I am. It doesn't hesitate. It doesn't second-guess. It doesn't wonder whether to move my left hand or my right hand. It just operates. And it adapts — if the situation changes, it adjusts. If someone speaks to me, it answers. And the answers are — correct. People don't notice." She paused. "That's what I can't — people don't notice. And then it eases out and I'm there again, and I don't always know how long I was gone or what happened while I wasn't."

Kai didn't say anything. His hand had stopped flexing.

"The worst part," she said, and her voice was steady in a way that sounded like it cost her something, "the worst part isn't the absence. I don't experience the absence. That's the whole point. I'm not there to experience it. The worst part is coming back and knowing that my body was doing things without me and that no one could tell the difference."

The wind gusted against the window. Something metallic ticked in the heating vent.

"No one," Kai said.

"No. If I'd come back and kept going tonight, you wouldn't have known. Would you? If whatever it was had just — continued. You wouldn't have been able to tell."

He wanted to say yes, he would have known, he would have felt the difference. But the question wasn't whether he'd noticed the moment she left — he had noticed that. The question was whether he'd have noticed when she came back, or whether the thing that replaced her was smooth enough that he'd have kept going, touching a body that performed like Lena and was not Lena in any way that counted.

"I don't know," he said.

She nodded. She didn't look hurt by that. She looked like she'd expected it, which was worse.

"What does it — what does the other thing feel like? When it's there instead of you."

"I don't feel it. That's the point. I only know it was there afterward. There's a gap. Not a gap in time — I don't lose hours, usually just seconds. A gap in continuity. Like a cut in a film. One moment I'm in the pool and the next moment I'm at the wall and two laps happened and I sort of remember them but they don't feel like mine. They feel like something I watched someone else do."

She said this with the clinical precision she always brought to descriptions of physical sensation. Her English was clean and slightly formal, each word chosen and placed. He wondered if that precision was a kind of scaffolding — whether describing the desync in structured language kept it at a distance she could manage.

"Margot said it's within acceptable parameters," Lena said. Her voice changed when she said Margot's name. Not angry. Careful.

"You believe that?"

"No." Simple. Definite. Something she'd measured against her own evidence.

They lay there. The bed was narrow and their shoulders were touching and the contact was nothing like what it had been half an hour ago. It was just contact. Two people in a small bed because neither one had left.

The heating vent clicked again. His stomach growled, which was stupid and perfectly timed and they both heard it.

"Sorry," he said.

"You didn't eat?"

"Half a protein bar. I got distracted."

She almost smiled. He could hear it in her breathing even if he couldn't see it in the dark. The moment was small and ordinary and he was grateful for it — proof that they were two people in a room and not two case files lying side by side.

"Can I tell you something?" Kai said. He hadn't planned to say it. The words arrived because she had given him something honest and his body — the part that still made decisions about trust and reciprocity, the part he was fairly sure was him — wanted to give something back.

"Yes."

"About the blackout. Not the crash. Everyone knows about the crash. The shoulder, the wrist, the face." He touched the scar on his cheek without thinking about it. "That's just injury. I've been hurt before. The part I haven't told anyone about is the other part."

He looked at the ceiling. It was flat and white and institutional and he focused on the seam where it met the wall because he needed something boring to look at while he said this.

"The blackout lasted thirty seconds. I was running the 200 and at the 140m mark everything dropped out. Not like going dark. Dark is still a thing — your eyes are doing something, just the wrong something. This was — I wasn't anywhere. I didn't have the equipment to be anywhere. No eyes, no ears, no skin, no sense of which way was up. Just absence. No body to be absent in."

Lena was quiet. He could feel the quality of her attention beside him — the specific way she listened, which was the same attention he'd watched leave her face and come back. Attention was something you could lose. He knew that now.

"And then I came back. Floor. Blood in my eyes. Shoulder wrong. Wrist broken. And my legs —" He paused. "My legs were still going. The synthetic muscle. Still firing, still trying to run a sprint pattern. I was lying on the track with my face in my own blood and my legs were running a race I wasn't in."

He heard her breathing change beside him. Shorter. More controlled.

"But here's the thing I can't stop thinking about. During those thirty seconds, my body ran. It ran well. The system decided sprinting was still the correct action and it drove my legs at competitive speed into a concrete wall. And Yusuf showed me the telemetry afterward. My split for that last stretch, before the crash — consistent. Good form. My body was running a solid 200m sprint and I wasn't in it."

"And that was terrifying," Lena said. Not a question.

"Yeah." He could hear his own breathing. A little fast for someone lying still.

"Three years," he said. "Since the rebuild. And most of the time I can pretend the system is like — I don't know, a really good pair of shoes. It helps me run. It doesn't change who's running. But then something like tonight happens, or the blackout, and the pretending stops working. Because shoes don't grab someone while you're not paying attention."

He let the word sit. "Not the void. The void was nothing — literally nothing, there's nothing to be scared of when there's nothing. The terrifying part was that my body did fine without me. It ran the race. It didn't need me there to run. So what's the Kai part for? I'm not being — I don't mean that as some big philosophical thing. I mean it practically. What does the person in the body contribute that the system can't already do on its own?"

He hadn't said this to anyone. He hadn't fully assembled it as a thought before now. It had been a physical thing — a tightness in his chest at three in the morning, a reluctance to run at full effort — and putting words on it didn't make the feeling go away but it gave the feeling an edge he could hold.

"Tonight," she said, after a silence. "Your hand."

He exhaled. "You noticed."

"Your grip changed. On my hip. It was very precise. The kind of precise that isn't — " She paused, searching for the word.

"Isn't me," he said.

"I was going to say 'isn't voluntary.' I don't know if it's you or not. Maybe it's part of you."

"It's not." He was sure about that much. "I didn't decide to do anything with my hand. I was just — touching you. And the system decided it could do it better. And it did. It adjusted my grip so it was optimized. Like I was holding a piece of equipment and the machine corrected my form."

She was quiet for a long time. Their shoulders were still touching. The wind was doing its flat, patient thing against the glass.

"So we're the same," she said. "Our bodies do things without us. Yours does it better than you would. Mine does it somewhere I can't follow."

"Yeah."

"That should make this worse." She said it as an observation. A datum she was evaluating. "Being with someone whose body has the same problem. It should make everything worse."

"Does it?"

She took a long time. He let her. The ceiling was still boring and his wrist was still aching and the woman beside him was present, right now, in a way he could feel, and he wanted her to take as long as she needed.

"No," she said. "It doesn't."

He reached for her hand and found it on the blanket between them. His fingers closed around hers. The touch registered in the usual way — the warmth and pressure first, then the system's precise measurement of her hand's temperature and the force he was exerting and the distribution of contact across both their palms. He held on to the first signal. The rough one. The imprecise one. The one that was his.

"Your hand's doing it again," she said. "The adjusting."

He felt it. His fingers had redistributed around hers, grip micro-correcting to equalize pressure. He hadn't told them to.

"Yeah."

"Don't be sorry."

"I didn't say sorry."

"You were about to." She squeezed his hand. Her grip was strong — a swimmer's grip, tendon and bone that had been pulling through water since she could walk. "It's your hand. Even the parts that aren't yours. It's still your hand."

He didn't know if that was true. But she was holding it, and she was present, and the room was dark. The diagnostic display stayed off. The hallway light made its thin gold bar across the floor. Outside, wind moved against the window with the constancy of altitude, and the mountains were behind the glass somewhere in the dark — enormous, old, not caring about any of this.

His fingers kept making their small corrections against hers. She kept holding on. They didn't sleep.


# Chapter 12

Yusuf had pushed the conference table against the far wall and arranged chairs in a rough semicircle. It hadn't helped much. Eight people in a room built for six, and nobody looked comfortable.

Kai sat between Lena and a Japanese sprinter named Yuki Tanaka, whose left hand still went numb without warning — a gift from the virus that Meridian's patch hadn't fixed because Yuki hadn't signed. On Lena's other side, two middle-distance runners Kai knew by event but not by name: a woman with close-cropped red hair who ran the 1500, and a South African in his late twenties who ran the 800. Behind them all, in a chair she'd pulled a full meter back from the group, Margot sat with her legs crossed and her arms folded, as though she'd come to audit rather than participate.

Jin-seo Park had the chair nearest the door. Lena had invited her, which had surprised everyone including Jin-seo, who sat holding a paper cup of coffee she'd stopped drinking twenty minutes ago.

Through the west-facing windows, the Bernese Alps stood in flat late-afternoon light. The lower ridges were patchy where snowmelt had started, grey rock showing through the white in irregular blotches. Water ticked off the eaves in an uneven rhythm that Kai had been trying not to listen to since he sat down.

Yusuf looked tired. He'd been working nights in the calibration bay — it showed in the smudge on his glasses, the shirt untucked on one side, the way his Birmingham accent sat heavier than usual, the professional polish thinned by fatigue. He had his laptop open on a stool.

"Right," he said. "So. The short version."

He turned the laptop toward the group. On the screen, a schematic Kai couldn't parse — firmware architecture rendered in color-coded subsystems, the visual language of a field Kai had never learned to read. He stopped trying after three seconds.

"I can build a patch that targets the virus's propagation mechanism. Just the propagation — it stops the thing spreading through the calibration network and neutralizes the cascade that causes blackouts." Yusuf tapped the screen. "It doesn't require full read-write access to your enhancement systems. Doesn't require the data license Meridian's asking for. It works at the firmware level, compartmentalized."

"Works how?" Yuki asked.

"By isolating the communication pathway the virus exploits. Your systems talk to the calibration network during tuning, yeah? The virus piggybacks on that conversation. My patch builds a wall around the pathway. Your systems still function, still calibrate, but the virus can't propagate through the shared infrastructure anymore."

"And the blackouts?"

"Should stop. The blackout cascade originates from the corrupted firmware — if the corruption can't spread, it can't trigger."

"Should," Margot said from the back of the room.

Yusuf looked at her. "Should. Yeah. That's the honest word."

He pulled up a second screen. Performance metrics — reaction times, force output, sensory acuity scores. Kai recognized the format from his own quarterly assessments.

"Costs," Yusuf said. "Three things you need to know. One: this hasn't been tested on a live human system. I've run it in simulation and on isolated hardware, but that's not the same thing. I can't promise it works. I can't promise there aren't side effects I haven't modeled."

The room was quiet. Water ticked off the eaves.

"Two: even if it works perfectly, you're looking at a performance reduction. Ten to fifteen percent. The patch limits some of the optimization pathways your systems use — reaction time, motor efficiency, sensory processing. You'll be slower. Less precise. Less augmented. Still enhanced, but the ceiling comes down."

"Ten to fifteen percent isn't making a final," the redheaded runner said. "That's not a medal conversation. That's a first-round exit."

"Yeah," Yusuf said. "Could be."

"And three?" Kai asked.

Yusuf took his glasses off and cleaned them on his shirt. He always did this when he was buying time — the gesture that meant the engineer was choosing how to say something. Kai had catalogued it across weeks of calibration sessions without really meaning to.

"The patch permanently severs the neural coaching feed interface."

Margot's chair scraped the tile. A small sound, involuntary — her body reacting before her face could decide not to. She caught it, recrossed her legs, and her expression settled into the flat assessment she used in telemetry reviews and debriefs, the one that said she was processing rather than feeling. But the sound had already happened, and Kai had heard it, and probably Lena had too.

The word *permanently* sat in the room. Margot's left hand had gone to her right wrist — to the feed interface, the device she wore like a second pulse. She moved the hand away after a moment, set it on her knee, and the motion was deliberate in a way that told Kai the first gesture hadn't been.

"The feed uses the same bidirectional data pathway the virus does," Yusuf said. "I can't wall off the virus pathway without closing the feed connection. They're not separate channels — they're the same architecture. So if you take my patch, no more real-time coaching feed. Permanently."

He put his glasses back on and looked at Margot. Everyone looked at Margot.

She didn't move. Her arms stayed folded across her chest and her face stayed flat. Whatever was happening behind that face wasn't available to the room.

"OK," she said. "So it's untested, it costs double digits in performance, and it dismantles the coaching infrastructure these athletes have been training with for years. Before the biggest competition of their lives."

"That's what I just said, yeah."

"And your alternative to Meridian's patch — the one that's been tested, that's backed by a full engineering team, that addresses the virus without gutting the athletes' support systems — is this."

"Meridian's patch comes with a contract that signs over every piece of biological data your body's ever generated. Forever. Transferable. They can sell it."

"I know what the contract says. I've read it." Margot uncrossed her arms and leaned forward. "What I'm saying is that your patch is a worse solution for the athletes. More risk, more cost, for the privilege of keeping data that most of them don't even know how to interpret."

"It's their data."

"It's their bodies. And you're proposing to experiment on them."

Lena hadn't spoken. She sat with her hands in her lap, her back very straight — the posture she adopted when she was organizing her thoughts into a sequence she could trust. The neural link port at the base of her skull was visible where her hair was pulled back, a small circle of dark composite flush with the skin.

"How would the patch interact with existing desync symptoms?" she asked.

Yusuf turned to her. His voice shifted when he spoke to Lena — slower, more careful, the register he used during calibration sessions when an athlete was in the rig and needed to trust the process.

"Honestly, I don't know for certain. It should stabilize the desync by interrupting the feedback loop between your neural link and the corrupted network. But desync is cumulative. The changes your system's already made to your neural patterns — those don't reverse just because you wall off the cause."

"So the damage stays."

"The damage to date stays. The progression stops. That's the theory."

"Or the patch locks in whatever state my system is in right now."

"That's also possible. Yeah."

Lena nodded once. Her hands were still in her lap but she'd laced her fingers together, pressing her thumbs against each other in a small rhythmic motion. Kai had learned to read this gesture over the past weeks. She was checking — confirming that her hands were doing what she'd told them to do.

His own wrist ached. The deep ache, the one that lived where the bone had knitted too fast, synthetic grafts accelerating a repair that should have taken eight weeks into three and leaving something not quite right in the architecture. He pressed his thumb into the spot without thinking. A habit now.

"What's Meridian's performance hit?" he asked.

"None," Yusuf said. "Their patch is designed to maintain full optimization."

"Because they keep full access."

"Because they keep full access. Yeah."

The South African runner rubbed the back of his neck. "So the choice is: give up our data and compete at full capacity, or keep our data and compete at eighty-five percent with a patch nobody's ever tried on a living person."

"That's the choice," Yusuf said.

"That's a shit choice."

"Yeah. It is."

Margot stood. Not to leave — she walked to the window and stood with her back to the room, looking at the mountains. The late light caught the feed interface on her left wrist, the wrist-mounted device she used to connect to athletes' neural systems. Personal equipment, not facility-issued. She wore it the way some people wore watches they'd stopped looking at.

"You built this in the calibration bay," she said, not turning around. "On institute time. Using institute resources. Does Petra know?"

Yusuf's expression tightened slightly. "That's not—"

"Does the facility director know?"

"This isn't about facility authorization."

"It's about whether anyone's checked your work." Margot turned. "You built a firmware patch in your spare time. You want to install it in people's nervous systems. And the only quality control is you telling us it ran clean in simulation."

The room shifted. Kai could feel it — the weight of agreement moving toward Margot, not because anyone liked her position but because her points were real. The patch was untested. Yusuf was one engineer. Nobody in this room had the technical literacy to evaluate his work independently.

"She's not wrong," Yuki said quietly. Her left hand was resting on her thigh, the one that sometimes went dead. She pressed it against the muscle as if confirming something.

The South African runner leaned forward in his chair. "Why'd you build this?"

Yusuf looked at him.

"You're the calibration engineer. You could've recommended Meridian's patch, filed your reports, gone home clean. You didn't have to spend your nights building something nobody asked for." He paused. "So why?"

Yusuf's posture changed. Kai watched it happen — the shoulders settling, the spine straightening, the Birmingham receding from his voice as his professional register engaged. The tell. When Yusuf sounded his most composed, he was furthest from honest.

"Because Meridian's solution is architecturally excessive for the problem it solves. The data access they're requiring isn't necessary for—"

"That's the technical answer," the runner said. "I'm asking the other one."

The room waited. Jin-seo's paper cup made a faint sound as her grip tightened on it.

Yusuf looked at the screen on his laptop. At his hands. At the window where Margot was still standing. Then back at the group.

"My sister has Voss-Kessler syndrome."

Nobody moved.

"It's a progressive neural deterioration condition. Rare. No treatment. She's twenty-nine and her speech is already—" He stopped. Started the sentence in a different direction, his voice flatter, the accent almost gone. "The biological data generated by enhancement athletes — your data, the stuff Meridian wants to lock under their license — some of it has direct application to neurological treatment research. Not theoretical. Specific pathways, specific neural mapping data, that could contribute to understanding how Voss-Kessler degrades motor and speech function."

He took a breath that was slightly too controlled.

"If Meridian locks that data under their contract, the research pipeline closes. Their license is irrevocable and transferable. They're not going to share it with academic researchers working on a condition that affects four hundred people worldwide. It's not profitable enough." He looked around the room. "So yeah. I built an alternative that doesn't require giving Meridian permanent ownership of your biological information. And yeah, part of the reason is I don't want them owning the data that might help my sister."

The silence that followed wasn't the one Yusuf had wanted. Kai could see it on his face — the jaw that didn't quite relax after closing, the slight surprise behind his glasses. He had expected the revelation to land as sympathy, or at least understanding.

What it landed as was arithmetic.

The redheaded runner glanced at the woman next to her. The South African was rubbing his chin, his expression shifting from curiosity to something more guarded. Yuki was looking at the laptop screen as though the schematic had changed meaning.

Because the equation had changed. Yusuf wasn't offering them an alternative out of principle. He was offering them an alternative that happened to protect the data he needed. His patch didn't lock information under Meridian's contract — and now they knew exactly why that mattered to him.

Kai watched Yusuf stand there absorbing it — the room recalculating him in real time, the sympathy he'd expected converting into something colder and more practical. The engineer who'd worked nights to build them an option, who'd just told them about his sick sister, and the room's first response was to wonder what his angle was. It wasn't unfair. That was the worst part. It wasn't unfair at all.

---

Jin-seo spoke first.

"I'm sorry about your sister."

She said it plainly — not performed warmth, not corporate condolence. She'd set the cold coffee on the floor by her chair. Her hands were in her lap now, fingers laced, one thumb pressing the heel of her other hand in a small controlled motion. She was thirty-three and had the posture of someone who'd spent her working life in rooms where she was the youngest person and the least authorized to speak.

"Thank you," Yusuf said.

"And I understand why you built it. I do." She paused. Chose her next words carefully — the care of someone who'd learned that in rooms like this, imprecise language cost people things they couldn't get back. "But what you're asking these athletes to do is accept a firmware modification that's never been installed in a living human system, built by one engineer with a personal stake in the outcome, to solve a problem that Meridian has already solved with a tested, validated patch."

"Meridian's patch comes with—"

"I know what it comes with. I've heard every version of the objection at this facility and at four others." Her voice didn't rise. It stayed level, carefully controlled, and Kai noticed how similar that control was to Lena's precision when Lena was holding herself together. "The data license is real. The autonomy cost is real. I'm not pretending those aren't serious. But your alternative also has costs, and one of them is risk. Untested risk. Applied to human beings."

She looked at Lena, then Kai, then the others.

"My brother died from a cascade failure in 2034. Enhancement system, shared network, propagation event — very similar architecture to what's happening here." Her voice stayed level but the muscles around her mouth changed, just slightly — not a tremor, a tightening, something held in place by practice. "He was twenty-six. He was running the 1500 and his legs stopped working at the 800 mark. The rest of his system kept firing. His heart rate hit 240 before the emergency shutdown engaged." She paused. "It didn't engage fast enough."

The room was still. Through the glass, the shadows of the mountains had lengthened across the snowmelt.

"So when someone tells me they've built a fix that hasn't been tested on a live system, and they want to install it in athletes about to compete at the highest level, and their primary motivation turns out to be personal — I can't support that. I won't pretend the risk is acceptable."

"Meridian's patch has its own risks," Yusuf said. His accent was thicker now, the professional register breaking under the pressure of being opposed by someone whose argument was better than his.

"Meridian's patch has been through full clinical validation."

"Clinical validation funded by the company that—" He stopped himself. Whatever he'd been about to say, he pulled it back behind his teeth and swallowed it.

Jin-seo caught the shape of the missing words. "That what?"

"Their patch has been validated. You're right."

"Yusuf. That what?"

"It's not relevant to this conversation."

The room registered the gap — something Yusuf knew and wasn't sharing. Kai watched Margot, who was watching Yusuf with the focus of someone filing information for later use. She'd noticed the gap. She didn't push on it. Pushing would have meant joining Yusuf's side of the argument, even temporarily, and Margot was too precise for that.

"I think it's relevant," Jin-seo said, "if you're asking people to refuse a validated patch in favor of yours."

"I'm not asking anyone to do anything. I'm presenting an option."

"You're not neutral and you've just explained why."

"Nobody in this room is neutral." Yusuf's voice dropped, the Birmingham flooding back. "You're not neutral. You work for Meridian."

"I'm here because Lena invited me."

"You're here because getting holdouts to sign is literally your job."

"My job is making sure nobody else dies the way my brother died."

Her voice cracked on *brother*. A hairline fracture in the composure. She didn't smooth it over or correct for it. She let it sit in the room, and the room sat with it.

Margot came back from the window. She didn't sit down. She stood behind her empty chair with her hands on the backrest.

"Can we talk about what actually happens to these athletes?" she said. "Not the data philosophy. Not the principle. The bodies."

Kai looked at her.

"If they take Yusuf's patch and it fails during competition — what happens?"

Yusuf was quiet for a beat too long.

"Same as competing unpatched. Virus-triggered blackout, potential sensory failure, autonomous system engagement."

"Which could kill someone."

"Yes."

"And if it does work but the performance hit is real — they're at the biggest competition of their careers running at eighty-five percent. For some of them that's the difference between a contract renewal and not having one. For some of them that's—" She looked at Yuki. At the runners. "That's money. That's families. That's whether they keep doing this or go home."

"I know that," Yusuf said.

"Do you? Because you're an engineer. You go home the same either way. Your salary doesn't change based on your reaction time. You're not the one walking out there at eighty-five percent trying to explain to your federation why your qualifying splits fell off a cliff."

"Margot—"

"I'm serious. You've got your reason for building this. Fine. But the costs aren't symmetrical. The athletes carry all the risk. You carry none."

Kai's wrist throbbed. He pressed his thumb into the bad spot and thought about what he was going to do — not about the argument, not about who was right, but about the actual physical question of whether he would let Yusuf install firmware in his spine or sign a document that gave a corporation ownership of his body's information or do neither and walk into the Games with a virus in his hardware that could blank his senses at full sprint. None of the options were good. All of them involved someone else's hands inside the part of him he couldn't verify was still his.

Margot was good. Every point she made was legitimate, every objection grounded in real consequence for real people, and underneath all of it was the thing she would not say: the patch severed the feed, and the feed was the only thing keeping her present in any way that mattered to her, and without it she was a thirty-five-year-old former swimmer with a fifth-place finish and nothing on the other side. Kai saw it. He wasn't sure anyone else did.

Lena spoke. The room turned toward her — not because she'd raised her voice but because she'd been so quiet that when she spoke the contrast was sharp.

"I want to understand the desync interaction better."

"OK," Yusuf said.

"If the patch works as designed, my neural link's current desync pattern becomes the permanent state. The damage stops progressing but doesn't reverse."

"Correct."

"And the desync has already changed how I process language. How I experience continuity." She paused, and the pause had a different quality than hesitation — she was checking, making sure the next sentence was one she'd chosen. "There are gaps. Periods where I'm not present and the system operates without me."

"I know."

"So the person who comes out of your patch is different from the person who went in. Not because of the patch. Because of what the desync already did before the patch stops it."

"That's — yeah. That's accurate."

"And Meridian's patch?"

"Meridian's maintains the full integration. It'd stabilize the network without interrupting the link's optimization pathways. So your desync symptoms might improve as the link reoptimizes around the fixed network. But—"

"But the link would still be making decisions I don't initiate."

"Yes."

Lena's thumbs pressed against each other. The small rhythmic check. Her hands were doing what she'd told them to do. For now.

"So one option locks in the damage and makes me less than I was. And the other might fix the damage but keeps running the system that caused it."

"Yeah," Yusuf said. His voice had gone quiet. "That's it."

Nobody offered Lena a solution. There wasn't one to offer. Kai put his hand on her knee, briefly, and she let it rest there for a moment before shifting forward in her chair, the gesture not a rejection but a returning to herself, to the problem she was sitting inside.

The argument didn't end. It scattered.

The South African runner said he'd think about it, which meant he'd sign Meridian's contract within the week. Kai could hear it in the tone — the man had already made his calculation and was being polite about it. The redheaded runner asked Yusuf technical questions about the isolation architecture, questions Yusuf answered with the careful patience of someone who needed allies and was losing them. Yuki sat with her intermittently dead hand and stared at the firmware schematic on the laptop as though she could learn to read it by force of will.

Jin-seo stood, bent to pick up her cold coffee, looked at it, and dropped it in the bin by the door. She paused beside Lena's chair.

"I'm in room 214 if you want to talk."

Lena nodded. Jin-seo left.

Margot left next. No announcement, no exit line. She picked up her jacket from the chair back and walked out. The feed interface on her wrist caught the hallway light, and then the door closed behind her.

The redheaded runner left. The South African runner shook Yusuf's hand and left. Yuki stayed another minute, flexing her left hand open and closed, open and closed, before she stood and went without saying anything.

Yusuf closed his laptop. He stood in the half-empty room and looked at the chairs arranged in their ragged semicircle, and for a moment his face did something unguarded — not disappointment exactly, but the particular tiredness of someone who had bet on honesty and watched it buy him nothing.

"I'm in the calibration bay tonight," he said. To the room, but he was looking at Lena. "If anyone has questions."

He left. The door closed. Through the glass, the mountains stood in the last of the light, the snowmelt still ticking off the eaves in its uneven rhythm.

"You OK?" Kai asked.

Lena didn't answer immediately. She was looking at her hands in her lap. The thumbs had stopped moving.

"Both options are a different person," she said.

Kai didn't have anything to say to that. He sat with her in the emptying room, thumb pressed into the knot in his wrist where the bone sat wrong, while the light went off the mountains and the water kept ticking off the eaves and the argument hung in the air the way arguments do when nobody wins them — not fading, just settling, becoming part of the room's weight.


# Chapter 13

The calibration rig's contact surface had gone from cold to warm to tacky with Lena's sweat in the first two hours. By the fourth hour, she had stopped noticing the dampness against her back. The fluorescent panels above her hummed at a frequency the system would normally have filtered out — a sixty-hertz electrical buzz, constant and trivial — but Yusuf had disabled her automatic sensory filtering before the procedure began, and now every ambient sound arrived unprocessed. The equipment drone. The intermittent click of Yusuf's keyboard. The building's ventilation pushing air through the ceiling vents with a thin whistle that made the bay sound, in the deep quiet of 3 a.m., like a place trying to breathe.

"Stage four," Yusuf said. He took off his glasses and cleaned them on the hem of his shirt, a habit she had noticed he performed at transitions — as if each new stage deserved a clear view of the monitors. Without the glasses his face looked younger and slightly unfinished. "This one remaps the motor pattern library. It's the longest stage. Probably ninety minutes, maybe more."

"What will it feel like?"

"Honestly? I don't know exactly. The simulation modeled it as pressure — behind the eyes and at the interface." He touched the base of his own skull, mirroring the location of her port. "The link's going to run hot again. Same as before, but sustained. I'll be watching thermal output the whole time."

"And if it gets too hot?"

"Then I pull the stage and we've got a half-rewritten motor library, which is — yeah. Let's not do that."

"Okay."

"Ready?"

"Yes."

The heat began at the port, as it had in every stage, blooming from the two-centimeter circle of metal and ceramic at the base of her skull. It followed the neural pathways like liquid filling channels — not a metaphor she was choosing but the closest description she could construct for the sensation of heat traveling along paths that were supposed to carry signal, not temperature. The warmth reached her temples. It pushed into the space behind her eyes, a dull, building pressure that was not pain and not quite comfort, occupying a register of sensation she did not have a casual word for.

Then the heat kept going. Past the cranial pathways and down, through the cervical interface where the link's tendrils connected to her spinal cord, branching into the motor networks — the fine wiring that governed her hands, her shoulders, her core, the entire chain of muscles that fired in coordinated sequence during her freestyle stroke. The heat traced the architecture of her enhancement the way water traces the shape of a vessel: revealing, by filling, a structure she had never felt the outline of before.

"How's that?" Yusuf asked.

"Six. Seven." She was using his scale — one to ten — which had become a shorthand over the previous stages. Seven meant manageable, uncomfortable, not yet at the threshold where she would lose her ability to hold still. "Still seven."

"Good. Stay with me."

She stayed. The ceiling tiles had ninety-four perforations each; she had counted the first row during stage one. The water stain in the far corner of the ceiling was shaped, if she was generous, like the outline of Lake Zurich. She was not being generous, but she had been looking at it for four hours and her mind wanted to make it into something recognizable. The dual need — to observe the ceiling in fine detail and to endure the heat spreading through her motor pathways — created a split-attention state that was almost meditative. Part of her was counting ceiling holes. Part of her was feeling the firmware rewrite touch muscles she used daily but had never felt individually, had never known the system addressed as separate units. The small muscles between her ribs. The flexors of her toes. The deep rotator cuff that stabilized her shoulders during the catch phase of her stroke.

The heat in these small muscles was different from the larger pathways. More intimate. The sensation of something being individually addressed, individually rewritten, like having each finger held up and examined one at a time.

Twenty minutes into stage four, her vision split.

She could see the ceiling — tiles, stain, fluorescent panels — and simultaneously she could see something else layered over it. Numbers. The distance from her eyes to the ceiling tile surface: 271.4 centimeters. The luminosity of each fluorescent panel rendered as a value she could read but did not understand the unit of. The temperature of the air in the bay: 21.3 degrees Celsius. The data refreshed every second, the numbers shifting in tiny increments as the ventilation pushed air through the space.

She was seeing the system's raw sensory buffer. The unprocessed data that the neural link had been collecting and interpreting for eighteen years, feeding its analyses into her perception without ever showing her the source material. The firmware rewrite was remapping the access permissions to her visual processing layer, and for the duration of the remap, both layers were visible — the processed world she had always lived in and the quantified world the system had always lived in, overlaid, occupying the same visual field.

"Lena." Yusuf's voice arrived through both layers. She heard it as sound and simultaneously perceived its decibel level, its frequency composition, the distance from the source to her right ear versus her left. "Your heart rate jumped. What's happening?"

"I can see what the system sees." Forming words was harder than it should have been. The motor coordination for speech was being remapped, and each syllable required a fractional extra effort, like speaking with a numb lip. "Numbers. Distances. Everything measured."

His typing stopped. Then started again, faster. "That's the raw buffer surfacing. I expected some bleed-through but not full overlay. Can you still see the room? The normal room?"

"Both. I can see both."

"Right." He was quiet for a moment. "It should resolve when stage four completes. How long — hold on." She heard him checking. "Seventy more minutes. Maybe eighty."

She closed her eyes. The system's data layer remained — it did not use her eyelids, it used the visual processing enhancement wired into her optic nerves. Numbers floated against the inside of her closed lids: the rig's surface temperature, the distance to the nearest wall, Yusuf's thermal signature reading as a warm, hand-shaped concentration to her right. She opened her eyes again. Both views together was less disturbing than the raw data alone.

Time became a thing she endured rather than tracked. Yusuf talked, not because she needed information but because — she understood later — the sound of his voice gave her something continuous to hold while the two visual layers competed for her attention. His professional register held for the first half-hour, then began to fray. "Your motor library is massive. I've calibrated dozens of athletes and I've never seen this density of stored patterns. Eighteen years, Lena. Your system's been learning how you swim since you were four and it's kept everything." He paused. "Every training set. Every competition. Every drill they ran you through in the developmental program. It's all in here and I've got to rewrite around every single one."

"Is that a problem?"

"It's a lot of data. It's going to take longer than ninety minutes." His Birmingham accent had surfaced — the vowels shifting rounder, the t's softening. He was tired. They both were. "Maybe another hour past what I estimated. I'll know better once we're through the competition-level patterns."

"I can wait."

"Yeah, I know you can." Something in his voice that wasn't professional. Then: "I've got a thermos somewhere. There's tea, if you can drink lying down. It'll have gone cold by now."

"I'm fine."

"Right."

She lay still and watched the dual perspective and felt the heat and waited. The system's data view showed her things she had never known she knew. The rig's vibration frequency: 14.2 Hz, transferred through the contact surface into her spine. Yusuf's breathing rate: 16 breaths per minute, elevated from his resting rate, which her system had apparently catalogued during past calibration sessions. The ambient light wavelength distribution, a data set that meant nothing to her as numbers and everything to her as the harsh, familiar whiteness of a room she had lain in hundreds of times before.

Eighteen years of calibration sessions. She had been in rigs like this one since she was small enough that her feet didn't reach the end. The Meridian facility in Zug had a pediatric calibration bay with smaller rigs and a mural of fish on the ceiling — she remembered this suddenly, the fish, bright tropical species painted in acrylic, with their Latin names underneath in tiny lettering that she had learned to read before she learned to read German. She had lain under those fish while her system was installed and tuned and upgraded and expanded, and she had never felt the architecture of it because the architecture was designed to be invisible.

Now it was visible. The heat showed her the shape of what she was letting go.

In the fifth hour, the heat spiked. Not a gradual escalation but an abrupt jump at the port — the sensation of metal held against a heating element, concentrated and sharp. Her hands clenched the armrests before she could override the reflex.

"Don't move." Yusuf's voice was tight. "I see it. Forty-two point seven. I'm adjusting write speed."

The heat radiated through her jaw and into her teeth. She could feel individual molars aching, the referred warmth settling into the roots like something pooling. She focused on the ceiling stain. Lake Zurich. The eastern shore. The villages she had visited on school trips from the Zug facility, back when the world outside the program had seemed like a place other people lived.

"Coming down," Yusuf said. "Ten more seconds."

She counted in German, which she did under stress — *eins, zwei, drei* — and at eight the heat began to ease, and at twelve it returned to the sustained, tolerable warmth she had been enduring all night.

"Sorry." His voice was quieter now. "The competition patterns are written at higher density. More data per neural node, more processing heat during rewrite. The simulation didn't model it accurately."

She unlocked her fingers from the armrests, one at a time. Her palms had left marks in the rig's padding.

"We can take a break," he said.

"No."

"Lena, it's—"

"If we pause, the thermal residue dissipates and you have to rebuild to operating temperature when you restart. That's another twenty minutes of heat before you can write anything." She didn't know where this knowledge came from — whether it was information she had absorbed from eighteen years of calibration sessions or something the system had stored that she was now accessing without its usual mediation. "Don't pause. Keep going."

He kept going.

The later stages had different textures. Stage five remapped the sensory integration layer, and during this stage the dual perspective collapsed. For twenty minutes she could only see the system's view: coordinates, values, measurements. No color. No texture. No depth — just flat numerical representations of three-dimensional space. She described this to Yusuf in short, precise sentences because precision was the only cognitive mode that felt stable while her visual world had been reduced to a spreadsheet. He told her it was within parameters. She believed him because she had no other option.

When her normal vision returned, it came back wrong at first — colors oversaturated, edges too sharp, the fluorescent panels painfully bright. Then it settled, adjusted in a way that felt organic rather than computed, and the room was a room again.

Stage six was the last, and it was the strangest. It rewrote the autonomous routines — the subsystems that fired her muscles in patterns she hadn't selected, that adjusted her posture before she registered a slouch, that managed her breathing rate without waiting for her conscious input. The rewrite didn't produce heat or pressure. It produced absences. Small motor functions going offline one at a time — her left hand relaxing on the armrest, then her right, then the muscles of her lower back that had been holding a slight arch she hadn't known she was maintaining. She settled into the rig with a sudden weight. Her body had been held in a low-grade state of system-managed tension for her entire life, and the tension was releasing now, and she sank into the padding like someone putting down luggage they had forgotten they were carrying.

"That's the autonomous layer going into restricted mode," Yusuf said. "It'll still keep you from falling. Injury prevention stays active. But it won't initiate motor patterns anymore." He paused. "It won't be swimming for you."

"Good," Lena said.

Outside the bay's windows, the sky had shifted from black to a dark grey with a band of green-white light along the eastern ridge. Not sunrise, but its approach — the Alps catching the first light at their peaks while the valley stayed dark. She had watched dawn from this facility hundreds of times and had never noticed the green tint in the pre-dawn light because the system's visual processing had always corrected for ambient color temperature, presenting her with a color-neutral view.

The green was real. The system had been editing it out her entire life. She stared at it.

Yusuf disconnected the cables from her port with the precise, careful movements she recognized from eighteen years of being calibrated — the specific way he looped the cables, the small tug-test on each connection before withdrawing. He was the best calibration engineer she had worked with. She had always known this as a fact. She knew it now as a feeling: the trust of having let someone spend nine hours inside the architecture of her mind and arriving on the other side.

It was six forty-three in the morning.

She sat up slowly, and the room moved in a way she was not prepared for. Her vestibular system — working alone, without the smoothing algorithms that had balanced her for eighteen years — sent an unfiltered spatial report, and the report was blunt. She caught herself with one hand on the rig's edge and waited for the tilting to settle.

"Easy," Yusuf said. He brought her a cup of water from the small station in the corner.

She drank it. The water was room temperature and tasted like water. She had expected something more specific and there was nothing more. It was just water.

***

The diagnostics took an hour. Reaction time panels, sensory acuity sequences, motor coordination tests, cognitive load assessments. Lena pressed buttons, tracked objects, touched her nose, answered arithmetic questions. The tests were identical to ones she had taken hundreds of times before; the results were not.

Reaction times: pre-patch average of 173 milliseconds, post-patch average of 192. Eleven percent slower.

Visual resolution: dropped from the enhanced range into what Yusuf called "normal high." She could no longer read the small print on the pharmaceutical labels across the bay; she hadn't known she could until she couldn't.

Auditory spatial processing: markedly reduced. A sound to her left registered as *to the left* rather than *to the left, 2.3 meters, fifteen degrees above horizontal*.

Proprioception: the largest change. The system's continuous body-position model was gone, and the raw proprioceptive input felt coarse — like trying to read a book after someone had lowered the resolution until the letters were legible but soft.

"I feel like I'm wearing thick socks," she said. "All over my body. Everything."

Yusuf wrote this down. Then he set the clipboard on the counter and looked at her directly. "The desync markers are gone. Completely. No residual firmware corruption, no anomalous pattern initiation, no autonomous motor firing. Your system isn't going to do things without you anymore." He let that sit for a moment. "That's what we were trying to fix, and it's fixed."

She nodded. She was sitting in the rig with her feet flat on the floor. The tile was cold through the thin soles of the facility slippers she had worn down here last night. She could feel the cold and only the cold — not the tile's thermal conductivity, not the temperature gradient between the floor surface and the deeper substrate, not the calculated rate of heat loss from her skin. The information stopped at sensation and did not continue into data. The floor was cold. That was what she knew.

This was when she noticed the silence.

It was not an absence of sound. The bay was as noisy as it had been all night — the equipment drone, the ventilation, Yusuf turning a page on his clipboard. What was gone was something she had never identified as a presence because it had never been absent. The system's ambient processing — the constant sub-perceptual hum of the neural link running its optimization routines. Evaluating her posture. Modeling her fatigue. Pre-computing the motor sequences she might need in the next seconds. Adjusting her sensory filters to suppress irrelevant input and sharpen the rest. It had been running since she was four. It had been running when she learned to read and when she took her first exam and when she stood on her first podium and when she lay awake in her room in the Meridian facility in Zug and listened to the building settling and thought she was listening with her own ears to a quiet building, when in fact she was listening through a system that was simultaneously processing the building's acoustic signature, filtering out the furnace cycling, boosting the frequencies that indicated proximity of a person in the hallway.

It had always been there. She had mistaken its activity for the sound of her own mind thinking.

Now it was gone, and her head was quieter than it had ever been.

She sat with it. The quiet was not peaceful and it was not distressing. It had a shape — she could feel the outline of what had been there by feeling what was not there, the way she might locate a missing tooth with her tongue. The silence had contours. It occupied the cognitive space where the ambient processing had lived, and the space was simply empty now, and the emptiness had a weight to it because it was new. She had never experienced this particular nothing before.

"It's quiet," she said.

Yusuf looked at her. "The bay?"

"In my head." She touched her temple. "It's quiet in my head. I didn't know it was loud."

He took off his glasses and rubbed his eyes with the back of his hand. He looked wrecked — face slack with fatigue, shirt wrinkled from the night, stubble he wouldn't normally have allowed in the calibration bay.

"Yeah," he said. "I modeled that. The ambient processing uses measurable cognitive bandwidth. When it goes offline, the bandwidth opens up. I expected you'd notice." He put his glasses on. "I didn't know how it'd feel."

"It feels like something left the room," she said. Then, more carefully: "No. That's not what I mean. It feels like something was always in the room and I didn't know it was there, and now it's gone and I can tell it was there because I can hear that it's not."

Yusuf wrote something on the clipboard without responding.

She stood and walked to the door. The corridor from the calibration bay to the pool wing was the same corridor she had walked hundreds of times — concrete floor, overhead fluorescents, the chlorine smell strengthening as she approached the natatorium. The walk felt different. Not harder, not easier. Simpler. Her feet struck the floor and the floor returned its information — cold, hard, slightly textured — and that was all the floor had to say. No analysis of her gait. No optimization of her stride length. No preemptive adjustment to account for the remaining distance. She walked the way people walked, one step following the next, and halfway down the corridor she stopped and stood still because the simplicity of it was so unfamiliar that she needed a moment to let it register as real.

***

The pool was empty. Morning training had been suspended for the procedure; the schedule board would show her slot as a blank. The water was the standard twenty-seven degrees — she knew this from the posted temperature on the wall, not from a system analysis — and the chlorine hit her in the usual way. She let the word stand. Usual.

She changed in the locker room and walked to lane three. The tile at the pool's edge was wet and she felt the grout lines under her bare feet with a sharpness that was both more and less than what she was accustomed to. The physical texture without filtering was rougher, more present — she could feel each ridge of grout, each slight variation in the tile's glaze. But the spatial information was gone. She could not tell, without looking, how far the pool's edge was from her toes. She would have to look and estimate and be wrong sometimes, the way people were wrong about distances.

She dove in. The water closed over her and the acoustic world changed — the rush and bubble of the entry, then the muffled underwater envelope — but the quality of the muffling was different. Flat. Undifferentiated. Before the patch, the system had processed the underwater acoustic environment in real time, filtering the noise of her own entry to surface the useful frequencies — other swimmers' positions, the acoustic markers for lane placement and wall proximity. Now the water just sounded like water. She was inside it and it surrounded her and that was what she knew.

She swam. Freestyle, easy pace. Her arms reached and pulled and she was the one reaching and pulling. Every stroke was an instruction she issued and her body executed, with no system stepping in to refine her hand angle or optimize her shoulder rotation or modulate her kick frequency. Her hand entered the water at whatever angle her own coordination — her own coordination, learned and imperfect and hers — produced. Her body rolled and she breathed and the breath came when she needed it, not a fraction of a second before.

The water was less textured. She kept returning to this word and finding it accurate. Before the patch, swimming had been high-resolution: temperature gradients at different depths, pressure differentials across her palm during the pull, the turbulence pattern left by her own return wake from the previous length. All of it sharpened, processed, presented as a dense field of information she could act on mid-stroke. Now the water was warm, resistant, chlorinated. It held her and she moved through it and it told her what it was and nothing more.

She did four laps and hung at the wall. She was breathing — not hard, it was easy pace — but consciously, because the system's respiratory management was gone and each breath was now a decision her body made and she felt herself making. The small lag between needing air and taking it, the gap where the automation used to live.

She pushed off and swam eight more laps. The strokes felt ordinary. She had arrived at the word and it was the right one. She was swimming the way a person who knew how to swim swam. Patterned, competent, unremarkable. Her time was slower. She didn't need a clock to know. She could feel the shortfall in each stroke — less reach, less force, more drag on the recovery — small decrements that would add up to seconds over four hundred meters, and seconds at this level separated the podium from the middle of the results sheet.

She stopped at the wall again and crossed her arms on the lane rope. The observation windows along the deck were dark. The pool was hers and the silence in her head was hers and the morning was hers and none of it was helping her figure out whether what she felt was loss.

The door to the pool deck opened and Dara came through, already suited. She was re-tying the long braid she always wore down to the pool — redone tighter so it would fit under her cap. She had a red elastic she'd been using for as long as Lena had known her, frayed enough that it should have been replaced months ago, and she wound it around her wrist twice after pulling it free. She also had her water bottle — the dented aluminum one she wouldn't replace, the one she filled with a specific ratio of electrolyte mix that she'd argued with the nutrition staff about — and she set it in its usual spot on the bench behind lane four before looking up and seeing Lena.

"You're in the water already." It came out as flat observation, which was the way Dara said most things.

"I wanted to swim."

Dara walked to the edge of lane four and set her towel on the bench. She adjusted it so it hung evenly, a small habitual gesture — Dara liked edges aligned, preferred things parallel, and had preferences about how her equipment was arranged that were either personality or program or some mixture that she herself would not have been able to separate. "How was it? The procedure."

"Long. Nine hours."

"But it worked?"

"The desync is gone."

"Good." Dara pulled her cap on, tucking the braid underneath with practiced efficiency. She pushed her goggles up to her forehead and looked at Lena. "Your times?"

"Slower. I can feel it."

"How much slower?"

"I don't know yet. A lot, maybe."

Dara didn't respond immediately. The pause was not contemplative — it was processing. She evaluated information about athletic performance through a framework where slower meant worse and the purpose of a body was the time it produced. This was not Dara being limited. This was Dara being integrated. The system and the self had reached an equilibrium in her that the question *is this me or the machine* did not disturb.

She dove in and swam a warm-up lap.
