Her entry was clean and economical, her stroke the Bergen program's signature pattern: metronomic, seamlessly paced, each movement transitioning into the next with a precision that had no visible human hesitation in it. No moment where the body paused to consider what it was doing next. She swam the way the system wanted a person to swim — perfectly, without any gap between intention and execution.

Lena watched from the wall. The difference between Dara's stroke and her own was visible now in a way it never had been. Before the patch, Lena's system-assisted stroke had been comparable — two highly optimized swimmers executing at similar efficiency levels. Now the optimization was gone from Lena's side and the gap was not about speed. It was about the quality of the movement. Dara moved like a single continuous process. Lena moved like a person making a series of decisions.

Dara stopped at Lena's end and pushed her goggles up. Water streamed down her face and she didn't wipe it.

"Can I ask you something?"

"Yes."

"Why did you do it?"

"Do what?"

"This. The patch. All of it." Her voice was level and the question was not rhetorical or unkind. It was genuine in the way a child's question is genuine — arising from a place where the answer really was not obvious. "You're slower. Your senses are worse. You said it's quiet in your head and you didn't even know the quiet was there before, so it's not like you were suffering from the noise. You had the desync, okay, that was bad. But now you have less. Less of everything. Why would you choose that?"

Lena looked at the water between them. Lane three and lane four, separated by the bobbing plastic of the lane divider.

"The desync was taking pieces of me," she said. "I was in the pool and then I wasn't in the pool and I couldn't remember what happened between."

"But the system was still swimming. You were still fast."

"That's not the same thing."

"It's the same result."

"No," Lena said. "It isn't."

Dara's brow furrowed. The confusion was real — a genuine cognitive impasse, not performed incomprehension. She lived inside a system that had merged with her selfhood completely enough that the distinction Lena was drawing — between a body that performs and a person who decides to perform — did not map onto her experience. She wasn't refusing to understand. She couldn't find where the understanding would attach.

"Okay," Dara said. She pulled her goggles down. "I hope it's worth it."

She pushed off the wall and began her set. Stroke after stroke, metronomic, flawless. Lena watched her go.

She stayed at the wall. The water lapped against her chest. The pool was quiet — Dara's stroke receding down lane four, the ventilation pushing warm air along the deck, the faint chemical bite of chlorine in the air above the surface. The silence in her head sat where it was going to sit for the rest of her life.

She didn't know what Dara meant by *worth it*. She didn't know what she would have meant by it either. She was slower. Her senses were blunter. The water felt like water and nothing more. Her strokes were hers and they were ordinary and for the first time in twenty-two years there was nothing in her head but her own thinking, and her own thinking was quieter and smaller and less certain than what had been there before.

She pushed off the wall. Her hand entered the water. Her arm pulled and her body rolled and she breathed and kicked and moved through the water the way a body moved through water when the body was the only thing moving. It was not fast. It was not remarkable. It was hers.

She swam another lap. And another. The ordinariness did not lift. It was not going to lift. This was what swimming would be now — just swimming, just her in the water, just the work of moving from one wall to the other. She didn't stop to decide whether the feeling behind her sternum was grief or relief or something that didn't separate cleanly into either. She kept swimming.


# Chapter 14

Tomas counted his steps the way his first coach had taught him — *uno, dos, tres* — the rhythm locked in before his body had ever been touched by engineers, back when counting was the only technology available to a thirteen-year-old on a dirt track outside Quito. Three years of neural integration hadn't erased it. The enhancement handled stride frequency to the hundredth of a second now, smoothed his approach pattern into something that felt less like running and more like a machine discovering what running was for, but the counting stayed. His one piece of untouched equipment.

The indoor track smelled like hot rubber and recycled air. Mid-morning. A group session — four athletes spread across the facility's straight and curve. Kai was doing light sprint work on the far lane, his stride still favoring the shoulder that had healed wrong after the blackout crash five weeks ago. Two other athletes Tomas knew only by event — a pole vaulter from the Dutch program, a middle-distance runner whose name he kept forgetting and felt bad about — occupied the curve. The high ceilings swallowed the sound of their footfalls and returned it as a faint percussion, a dozen rubber impacts overlapping into something that was almost rhythm.

Tomas had called his mother last night. She'd wanted to know about the weather, so he'd described the snowmelt off the peaks — how the meltwater ran in channels beside the road down to Meiringen, how the Alps looked different now that the ice was pulling back, exposing gray rock underneath that had been hidden all winter. His sister Valeria had grabbed the phone to announce she'd made the regional soccer team, and he'd promised to video-call her first match. His brother Diego, who was nine and not interested in sports, had shouted something about a math test from the other room. Tomas hadn't caught the words. He'd told his mother the food was fine and the training was going well and he loved her, and she'd said *cuídate, mi amor*, and he'd hung up and taped his lead ankle the way he always did — three wraps, tight, the tape pulling at the fine hair above the joint.

The ankle tape was still there now, under his compression sock, a small pressure he could feel with every stride.

Fourth hurdle. Clean. His lead leg snapped forward and the trail leg swept through with the delayed precision that still surprised him sometimes — the enhancement smoothing his clearance into something aerodynamically ideal, a pattern his body hadn't been able to produce before the grafts, a shape the air seemed to have already agreed to let him through. He loved this part. Not the way the senior athletes loved it, which was not at all, which was resigned tolerance of a body that performed without being asked. Tomas still felt wonder at the intersection of his effort and the machine's. Three years hadn't dulled that. The hurdle behind him rattled faintly on its base from the wind of his passage.

Fifth. Clean. The approach was good, his stride frequency settled, his arms pumping in the tight pattern Margot had drilled into him. He could feel her presence in the feed — a small warmth at the base of his skull, a faint sense of being watched from inside. Margot monitored his cardiovascular output and pacing targets in real time through the coaching interface. He didn't think about it. It was like knowing your heart was beating. True, constant, not worth attention.

Sixth hurdle. Twelve meters from the seventh. His body drove forward and the counting continued underneath everything — *uno, dos, tres* — and his heart rate was climbing, had been climbing for the last three hurdles, pushed past training range into a zone his body reached for because the coaching signal wanted it there. He didn't notice. The push felt like effort. Like desire.

Seven meters from the seventh hurdle, the world stopped.

Not white. Not void. Not a fade or a flicker or a dimming — Kai had described his blackout to the group and Tomas had listened and prepared himself for something gradual, something with a shape he could recognize and fight. This was nothing like that. This was the world present and then the world not present. Everything — sight, sound, proprioception, the rubber under his spikes, the temperature of the air in his lungs, the faint chlorine smell drifting from the pool three corridors away — all of it, at once, ceased. Not replaced by darkness. Not replaced by anything. The inputs simply terminated. Like a wire cut.

His enhancement did not stop.

His legs kept driving. The autonomous motor subsystem, still receiving firmware commands through the corrupted calibration network, maintained the sprint pattern — full speed, maximum power output through the synthetic muscle grafts. His arms kept pumping. His body continued its approach to the seventh hurdle with mechanical precision and no one at the controls.

He did not clear the hurdle.

---

Kai saw it happen from forty meters away.

He was standing at the edge of the curve, toweling sweat off his neck, his left wrist aching the way it always ached now — the bone knitted too fast, the synthetic repair leaving a seam in the marrow that the cold indoor air found and pressed on. He'd been watching Tomas without particular attention, the way you watch someone young do something well — appreciating the form, the economy of effort, the eagerness that hadn't been beaten out of him yet.

Tomas's stride didn't break. That was the first wrong thing. From Kai's distance, the sequence looked almost normal for a fraction of a second — an athlete approaching a hurdle, legs driving — and then the geometry was wrong. Tomas's lead leg drove forward at full sprint power but did not lift. The hurdle caught him just below the knee. At that speed — close to nine meters per second, the enhancement driving him harder than his natural body ever could have — the impact was not a stumble. The hurdle's crossbar bent against his shin and his momentum pitched him forward and over, his upper body rotating around the point of contact with a violence that made the Dutch pole vaulter, twenty meters away, drop her water bottle.

Tomas went over the hurdle and down. He did not put his hands out. His arms were still driving the sprint pattern, pistoning at his sides. He hit the track surface chest-first, and the forward momentum drove his body along the rubber in a scraping slide, and then his torso buckled — his hips, still being driven forward by legs that had not received the instruction to stop, jackknifed over his upper body, and his spine compressed.

The sound. Kai heard the sound from forty meters away. Not a crack — something wetter, a compressed impact that carried through the acoustic dampening of the high ceiling and arrived at Kai's ears as a single dense syllable.

Tomas's legs stopped moving. Not gradually. They just stopped, the way a switched-off machine stops — mid-stride, the left leg extended, the right leg half-cocked in trail position. The rest of his body continued for another half-second, momentum carrying his torso forward in a loose slide, and then he was still.

Kai was running before he understood what he was doing. His own synthetic grafts fired his legs — the enhancement responding to his alarm faster than conscious thought, and for one sick instant his body was doing the same thing that had just destroyed Tomas, moving without permission, and he had to fight the urge to stop his own legs, to refuse the help, to insist on running at the speed of his own fear.

He reached Tomas in six seconds.

Tomas's face was turned to the side, his right cheek pressed into the track surface. His eyes were open. The blackout had ended — his senses had returned, Kai could see it in the way his pupils were tracking, the way his mouth was moving, trying to form words. He was conscious. He was present. The thing that had happened to his body had happened while he was gone, and now he was back and inside a body that had been rearranged.

"Don't move him," Kai said. He didn't know who he was saying it to. The Dutch pole vaulter was there, her hands hovering over Tomas's back, and the middle-distance runner was shouting something toward the medical bay corridor.

Tomas said something in Spanish that Kai didn't catch. Then, in English: "I can't — my legs. I can't feel my legs."

His voice was calm. Not because he was calm, but because the thing that had happened was so far outside any fear he had prepared for that his vocal cords defaulted to the register of minor confusion, of a missing shoe, of a wrong turn. He said it the way you'd say *I think I left my phone in the other room.* Flat. Informational. The terror would come later, when his brain finished processing what his body already knew.

There was blood on the track from his shin where the hurdle had caught him, and a longer smear of abrasion from his chest slide, visible through his torn training top. But the real damage was invisible, buried in his spine where the neural bridge had been.

Petra arrived in under two minutes, moving fast, her gray-streaked hair escaping its pin. She had a portable diagnostic unit in one hand and a spinal stabilization kit under her other arm, and she dropped to her knees beside Tomas with a practiced efficiency that Kai found both reassuring and horrible — this was something she had done before, or trained for, or both.

"Tomas, can you hear me? Don't try to move."

"I can hear you." His voice was still flat. "My legs, Dr. Engel. I can't feel them."

Petra's hands moved along his spine, not touching — hovering — while the diagnostic unit's sensor bar swept the length of his back. Kai watched the readout from where he knelt. He didn't understand the medical data, but he understood the color: the neural bridge status indicator, which should have been green, was gray. Not red for damaged. Gray for absent.

"The bridge is gone," Petra said. She was talking to the medical staff arriving behind her, not to Tomas. But Tomas heard.

"What do you mean gone?"

"The neural bridge hardware has been destroyed. The impact —" She stopped herself. Looked at Tomas's face. His eyes were tracking hers with the focus of someone who understood that the next sentence would determine the rest of his life. She didn't finish the sentence. She said, "We're going to stabilize you and get you to imaging. Don't move."

Tomas lay on the track and looked at the ceiling, which was white acoustic tile under fluorescent strips, sixty feet up. The hum of the ventilation system. The rubber smell of the track surface against his uninjured cheek. His hands were flat on the floor beside his head and he could feel the rubber's texture against his palms and that feeling stopped at his waist as completely as if someone had drawn a line across his body with a marker and said: *below this, nothing.*

He was twenty-one. His legs had been the entire architecture of his life — the thing he was for, the reason Meridian paid for his family's apartment in Quito, the reason his sister could play soccer on a real pitch instead of the street, the reason his brother could fail math tests without it mattering because Tomas was going to the Apex Games and the sponsorship money would cover everything. His legs. His legs, which had carried him over six hurdles thirty seconds ago with a grace that still made him feel like he was being let in on a secret about what a human body could become, were now silent below the waist and would remain silent.

He signed the contract. He'd signed Meridian's contract because he couldn't afford not to. They'd installed the patch. The corporate fix, the one that traded his body's data for his body's safety, was supposed to prevent this. He'd surrendered everything they asked for — every biometric reading, every neural pattern, every genetic marker — and the virus had come through anyway, the blackout seizing him mid-stride and throwing him into a hurdle at nine meters per second, and his legs were gone.

*Cuídate, mi amor.*

He did not cry. He opened his mouth and closed it and opened it again and what came out was a sound that wasn't Spanish or English, that wasn't language at all, that was just the noise a body makes when it understands that the thing it was built for is finished.

---

Margot had felt everything.

She was in the coaching suite, fifteen meters from the track entrance, her feed interface live on her wrist, the neural connection open and streaming. Tomas's sensory world arrived at her nervous system with the half-second delay of the feed translation — enough lag that she couldn't react in real time, not enough that the experience felt secondhand. She was inside his effort. His hamstrings loaded on the approach, and she felt the loading as a phantom tension in her own legs, a ghost of athletic power that her body hadn't produced in years. His heart rate climbed — 168, 172, 176 — and she'd pushed it there, nudging his pacing targets upward through the coaching interface because the intensity was good, the rush was clean, his cardiovascular output at 178 bpm produced a cascade of sensation through the feed that lit her up in ways her own flat, unplugged nervous system could not.

She knew what she was doing. She always knew. The knowing had never been enough to stop.

The sixth hurdle. Tomas's clearance was beautiful through the feed — the explosion at the bar, his body airborne for a fraction of a second, the landing shock traveling up through his legs and into her borrowed proprioception. She pushed the pacing target again. A small nudge. His heart rate hit 182. His cardiovascular system was running hot, blood pressure elevated, the vessels in his spine and legs under more strain than training range called for. She registered this the way an addict registers the third drink: noted, not acted upon, already reaching for the fourth.

Then the feed went blank.

Not static. Not interference. The sensory stream from Tomas's neural system simply stopped, as if the other end of the connection had been unplugged from reality. One moment she was inside his effort — the rubber under his feet, the air in his lungs, the rhythmic pulse of his heart at 182 — and the next she was alone in her own body, her own unstimulated nervous system, the coaching suite's fluorescent light flat on her skin. The feed display on the wall showed all channels zeroed. Heart rate: still transmitting, 184 and climbing. Neural input: nothing. Sensory relay: nothing. Motor pattern: active. Active on all channels, his body doing things at full output with no sensory data to guide it.

She should have disconnected. The protocol for a feed disruption was immediate severance of the coaching link. She knew this. Her hand moved toward the interface on her wrist.

She didn't disconnect.

Because the feed channels were coming back. Not Tomas's senses — those were still dark. But the motor pattern data was streaming, and through the coaching interface's sensory relay she could feel what his body was doing. The legs driving at sprint speed. The approach to the hurdle. The impact.

She felt the impact.

Through the feed, at full sensory resolution, her nervous system received the translation of a human body hitting a hurdle at nine meters per second and going down. The shin strike first — a sharp crack of pain that radiated up through her left leg as if she'd been the one to hit the bar. Then the chest impact, the slide, the scraping abrasion that the feed transmitted as a hot strip of friction across her sternum and ribs. And then the compression.

The spinal compression arrived through the feed as a dense, total sensation that Margot had no analogy for. A closing. A compaction of the nerve pathways that carried information between brain and legs, translated through the neural interface into something she experienced as the feeling of her own lower body being — *folded.* Not in half. Inward. As if the space her legs occupied in her proprioceptive map was being compressed into a line and then into a point and then into nothing.

Then the nothing. The legs going silent. Not painful — that was the worst part, worse than the impact, worse than the compression. The absence of pain. The feed channel that had been streaming Tomas's lower-body sensation went flat, and Margot's nervous system received the flatness as a phantom paralysis that settled over her own legs like a cold sheet.

She yanked the interface off her wrist. The disconnect was brutal — the feed severing mid-stream, the sudden lurch from Tomas's ruined sensory world back into her own. She screamed. The sound came out of her before she decided to make it, a raw sound that did not contain words, that was the vocal equivalent of ripping a plug from a wall.

She was on the floor of the coaching suite. She didn't remember falling. Her legs were fine — she could feel them, she could move them, she pressed her palms against her thighs and felt the muscle and bone underneath, whole, intact, her own. But the phantom was still there. A layer underneath the real sensation, a ghost of paralysis that the feed had written into her nervous system and that her body had not yet overwritten. Her legs worked and they also felt dead, both things true at once, and she sat on the floor with her hands clamped on her thighs and her breath coming in short wet sounds that were not quite sobs.

The interface lay on the floor beside her, the wrist mount's display still showing Tomas's vitals. Heart rate: 171, dropping. Neural bridge status: gray.

She'd had his heart rate at 182 when the blackout hit. Training range for hurdle work was 155 to 165. She'd pushed him twenty beats above threshold because the rush was better at 180, because his cardiovascular effort at maximum output produced the specific flavor of borrowed intensity that she couldn't get any other way, and when the virus struck and his body crashed and his spine compressed, the elevated blood pressure had been there, the stressed vessels had been there, the cardiovascular system running hot and hard had been there underneath the impact, and she didn't know — she couldn't know for certain — whether the outcome would have been different if his heart rate had been at 160, whether the spinal blood flow would have been less catastrophic, whether the neural bridge might have survived a lower-energy impact. She couldn't know for certain.

But she knew enough.

She sat on the floor of the coaching suite and looked at her hands, which were shaking. Her hands, which had adjusted the pacing target. Her hands, which had pushed the slider upward on the interface because the feedback at 182 felt better than the feedback at 165. Her hands, which were still attached to her body, which still worked, which could still feel the cold tile floor underneath her and the sweat drying on her forearms and the faint hum of the ventilation system pushing processed air through the room where she sat with the knowledge that a twenty-one-year-old's legs were gone and she had been inside them when they went.

---

Three hours later. The calibration bay.

Kai went to check his own system logs because the alternative was standing in the medical bay corridor looking at the closed door behind which Petra and her team were imaging Tomas's spine, and he couldn't do that anymore. He'd stood there for ninety minutes already, his wrist aching, his shoulder aching, his body remembering its own crash with a precision that felt less like memory and more like rehearsal. Tomas's blackout was the same virus that had taken Kai's senses at the 140-meter mark five weeks ago. The same corruption in the shared calibration network. The same firmware eating through the infrastructure that kept their bodies from killing them.

Tomas had signed Meridian's contract. Had received their patch. The corporate fix — the one that required permanent data surrender, the one that was supposed to be the solution — had not stopped the virus from reaching him.

The calibration bay hummed. The sound was always there, the low-frequency drone of processing equipment that Kai had learned to tune out in his first week at the facility. Tonight it sounded different. Not louder. More present. As if the machines were doing something they shouldn't be.

He sat down in the calibration rig — the same reclined chair where Yusuf had first shown him his proprioception drift, weeks ago, when the body-self boundary had been a data point rather than a lived catastrophe — and pulled up his system logs on the rig's display. His neural bridge status. His graft performance. His sensory integration metrics. All green. All normal. His body continued to function within parameters that did not predict the future.

He was about to close the interface when he saw the transfer log.

It was buried in the system's administrative sidebar — a chronological record of data flowing to and from his enhancement hardware during calibration sessions. Most of it was routine: diagnostic uploads to the facility server, firmware update confirmations, the small exchanges that happened whenever an athlete's system connected to the calibration network. But there were other entries. Timestamped during his calibration sessions, data packets flowing to a destination address he didn't recognize — not the facility server, not Meridian's cloud, not any system address the rig was supposed to communicate with.

Days of biometric files. His neural bridge telemetry. His proprioception logs. His graft performance data. His genetic baseline readings. All of it, flowing to an external device, during sessions where he'd been lying in this chair with his eyes closed while Yusuf adjusted his systems.

Kai sat in the calibration rig and looked at the transfer log. He scrolled back. The entries went back weeks.

"Yusuf." He said it to the empty room. Then he stood up.

Yusuf was in the back office of the calibration bay, a small glass-walled room that looked out onto the main calibration floor. He was sitting at his desk with his glasses pushed up on his forehead and a cup of tea going cold at his elbow. Three monitors. One showed Tomas's crash diagnostic — the neural bridge failure cascade, rendered in a color-coded timeline that Kai couldn't read. The other two showed something else. Code. Pages of it. Comparisons. Side-by-side columns with sections highlighted in yellow.

"Yusuf." Kai stood in the doorway. "I found the transfer log."

Yusuf looked up. His face did the thing it did when he was deciding whether to perform competence or abandon it — a brief flicker of professional polish crossing his features and then failing, the Birmingham underneath showing through like a bone through skin.

"Right," Yusuf said. "Yeah."

"You've been copying my data. During calibration. All my biometric files, neural logs, graft performance — everything. To some device that isn't in the system."

"Yeah."

"How long?"

"Since February." Yusuf's hands were on the desk. His engineer's hands, steady and precise. They were not steady now. "Since I got here. Since before the virus, Kai. I was doing it before any of this happened."

Kai stepped into the office. The door didn't close behind him because it was a sliding door and the track was stuck, had been stuck since March, and Yusuf had never filed a maintenance request because the open door made the room feel less like a box. Kai didn't sit down. He looked at the monitors. The code on the two right-hand screens meant nothing to him, but the yellow-highlighted sections had a visual pattern — recurring structures, repeated sequences.

"Tell me what I'm looking at," Kai said.

"Sit down."

"I'm fine standing."

Yusuf took his glasses off his forehead and put them on. The gesture was familiar — Kai had watched him do it a hundred times before calibration work, the small adjustment that meant he was shifting into technical mode. But his hands shook when he settled the frames on his nose, and the shift looked less like competence and more like a man putting on a costume that didn't fit anymore.

"My sister Farah has Voss-Kessler syndrome," Yusuf said. "Progressive neural deterioration. There's no treatment. There's barely any research, because the condition's rare enough that nobody funds it. But the biological data from enhanced athletes — the neural integration telemetry, the graft interface metrics, the way your systems interact with your native tissue — some of that data maps onto pathways that are relevant to Voss-Kessler. Not directly. It's adjacent. But it's the most detailed neural-interface data set in the world, and it's being generated right here, every day, in every calibration session."

"You've been stealing our data for your sister."

"I've been copying biometric files to a personal archive, yeah. I'm not going to dress it up. I took the data because if I didn't take it, it was going to end up behind Meridian's corporate license and nobody outside their R&D department was ever going to touch it again."

Kai looked at the cold cup of tea. It had a skin on it, a thin milky film. The office smelled like old tea and cable insulation and the faint metallic ozone of overworked processing units.

"Tomas is paralyzed," Kai said.

"I know."

"He signed their contract. He got the patch. The fix. The thing that was supposed to make it safe."

"I know." Yusuf turned to the right-hand monitors. The code comparisons. The yellow-highlighted sections. "Sit down. Please."

Kai sat. The office chair was a cheap swivel model with a torn armrest, and it creaked under his weight.

"I've been analyzing the virus code since February," Yusuf said. "Since before Meridian arrived with their offer. The virus propagates through the calibration network — you know that. What I've been trying to understand is how it's structured. Where it came from. How something this targeted ended up in a shared infrastructure that Meridian designed, maintains, and controls."

He turned one of the monitors toward Kai. The yellow-highlighted code sections were arranged in parallel columns — left and right, with lines connecting matching patterns between them.

"Left column is the virus's core propagation code. The part that interfaces with the calibration network and spreads between connected systems. Right column is from Meridian's proprietary firmware development framework. This is their standard architecture — the building blocks they use to write all their enhancement software. The calibration tools, the neural bridge firmware, the graft integration protocols. All of it built on this framework."

Kai looked at the columns. He didn't read code. "What am I seeing?"

"Structural signatures. The virus's propagation mechanism was built using Meridian's own development tools. The code architecture, the way functions are nested, the naming conventions, the memory allocation patterns — it's their framework. Not copied from it. Built in it. The way you'd build a product, not a hack."

"You're saying Meridian made the virus."

"I'm saying the virus was built using Meridian's tools by someone who had access to Meridian's development environment. That's not the same thing, but it's — yeah. It's close enough that the gap is mostly legal rather than factual."

The calibration bay hummed around them. The sound that Kai had stopped hearing weeks ago was back, filling the space between Yusuf's words, and it sounded different now. Not mechanical. Institutional.

"Tomas signed their contract because he couldn't afford not to," Kai said. "His family needs the money. He gave them everything they asked for. His data, his genetic baseline, all of it. And their patch didn't stop the virus from destroying his spine."

"No."

"And you think that's because they made the virus. That the whole thing — the blackouts, the contract, the data surrender — was a setup."

"I think the virus was engineered to create conditions where the data surrender became the only rational choice. I think the patch is real — it does suppress the virus's propagation — but I think the fact that it requires full data access is the point, not a side effect. And I think —" Yusuf's voice shifted, the professional register cracking and the Birmingham breaking through underneath, faster, less controlled. "I think the patch didn't save Tomas because the virus is still in the network. The patch suppresses propagation, it doesn't eliminate the code. It's a leash, not a cure. It keeps the virus dormant as long as Meridian's servers are authorizing the suppression. Which means they can pull the leash whenever they want."

"You can't prove this."

"No. I can show structural similarity between the virus architecture and Meridian's development framework. I can show that the patch's suppression mechanism requires ongoing authorization from Meridian's cloud. I can show that the data lockdown would bury every piece of biological information that anyone outside Meridian could use for independent research. But I can't prove intent. I can't prove anyone at Meridian sat in a room and decided to create a virus that would paralyze a twenty-one-year-old kid from Quito."

Kai looked at the monitors. The code comparisons blurred in his vision — he didn't have the technical literacy to evaluate what Yusuf was showing him, and he knew it, and Yusuf knew he knew it. Which meant this conversation was not about evidence. It was about trust. Whether Kai trusted the man who'd been stealing his data for months, who'd lied to his face during every calibration session, who'd built an unauthorized personal archive out of information that belonged to Kai's body — whether that man's analysis of a corporate conspiracy was worth believing.

"You lied to me," Kai said. "Every session. I'd lie in that chair and close my eyes and you'd copy my data to your private archive and you never said a word."

"Yeah."

"You built an alternative patch that severs the feed interface — the thing Margot needs — and you never told anyone that your motivation was your sister's data access, not our safety."

"I told the group about Farah. In the argument."

"After you'd already built the patch. After Lena had already volunteered to test it. You told us when the information couldn't change any decisions we'd already made."

Yusuf took his glasses off. Rubbed his eyes. The gesture was so ordinary and so human that Kai felt something shift in his chest — not forgiveness, not sympathy, just the recognition that the man sitting across from him was not a villain or a whistleblower but a person who'd been making bad decisions for understandable reasons and who was now sitting in the wreckage.

"Tomas's neural bridge is gone," Yusuf said. His voice was quiet. The Birmingham was soft in it, the vowels rounded, the consonants blurred. "I ran the crash diagnostic. The virus triggered a cascading corruption in his sensory firmware, same as it did with you in February. His enhancement's autonomous motor system kept his body in the sprint pattern. He hit the hurdle at full speed. The fall compressed his thoracic spine and the impact destroyed the bridge hardware. The grafts kept his cord from severing — he's alive because of the synthetic reinforcement — but without the bridge, there's no neural pathway to his lower body. The biological cord was already degraded from the graft integration process. The bridge was compensating. Now it's gone and there's nothing to compensate."

"Is it permanent?"

"The bridge hardware has to be surgically replaced. Which means another full integration procedure, months of rehabilitation, and a new neural pathway that has to be grown from scratch. Even then, the probability of full restoration is —" Yusuf paused. Calculated or pretended to. "Low. The tissue damage from the compression complicates regrowth. He'll walk again, maybe. He won't run hurdles."

Kai stood up. The office chair rolled backward on its cheap casters and bumped the wall.

"You should have told me," he said. "From the start. About the archive, about Farah, about the virus code. All of it."

"If I'd told you about the archive, you'd have reported me. The data would have been locked down. My sister's —"

"Don't." Kai's voice was flat. The humor was gone, completely, the way it went when the things he was feeling exceeded the register of irony. "Don't make this about Farah right now. A kid is paralyzed. The corporate patch that was supposed to protect him didn't work. And you've been sitting on virus analysis that suggests Meridian engineered the thing that destroyed his legs. You've been sitting on it."

"I didn't have enough to — "

"Enough for what? Publication? A lawsuit? Enough for Tomas, lying in the medical bay trying to figure out how to tell his mother he can't walk? He might not need your standard of evidence, Yusuf. He might just need to know."

Yusuf didn't answer. He looked at his monitors — the code comparisons, the yellow-highlighted sections, the parallel columns that documented something that might be corporate sabotage or might be coincidence or might be the kind of pattern that an anguished engineer found because he needed it to be there. His hands were on the desk. They were shaking.

"What do you want to do?" Yusuf asked.

Kai looked through the glass wall of the office at the calibration bay beyond — the rigs where athletes lay down and trusted the system to maintain them, the humming machines, the fluorescent lights. Three corridors away, Tomas lay on an imaging table and learned the dimensions of his new life. One floor above, Margot sat with the phantom of his paralysis still alive in her legs. Outside the building, the spring thaw was pulling the last of the winter off the mountains, and the meltwater ran downhill the way it always did, finding the path of least resistance to the valley below.

"I don't know," Kai said. And he meant it. The trust was gone — not just in Yusuf, not just in Meridian, but in the entire apparatus of optimization that had rebuilt his body and called it improvement and could not prevent a virus it may have designed from destroying a young man whose only crime was being too poor to refuse the contract. He didn't know what to do with that. He didn't know what to do with any of it.

He left the office. The sliding door stuck behind him, half-open, the light from Yusuf's monitors spilling across the calibration bay floor in yellow columns that looked, from the right angle, like the bars of something.


# Chapter 15

Margot's legs were fine.

She sat on the edge of her bed in the grey light before the sun cleared the eastern ridge, feet flat on the cold tile, and her legs were fine. Both of them. She could feel the tile's chill through the soles of her feet, could feel the mattress edge pressing into the backs of her thighs, could feel the small muscles in her calves adjusting to keep her upright. Everything worked. Everything had always worked.

Underneath that — underneath the tile and the mattress and the ordinary fact of a body sitting on a bed at six in the morning — was the other thing. The phantom. Tomas's phantom. Through the feed she had felt his neural bridge break. Not as data, not as a line dropping on a screen, but as sensation transmitted at full resolution through the bidirectional pathway that connected her nervous system to his. The compression in his lower spine. The signals traveling down through his legs and then the signals stopping. A wall of nothing that started at his waist and went all the way down, and she'd felt it from inside — the legs going dead, the nerve pathways going quiet, the specific silence of a body that can no longer feel half of itself.

She stood up. The phantom hit on her third step — a skip in the signal, a gap, as if for one fraction of a second her left leg's proprioception went blank. She grabbed the desk. The gap closed. Her leg was fine. She took another step. Fine. Another. Fine. And then on the seventh step, the right leg this time, the same skip — a microsecond of borrowed deadness that made her weight shift wrong and her hand shoot out for the wall.

She leaned against the wall and waited for it to pass and it passed and she was fine and Tomas was in the medical bay paralyzed from the waist down and she was fine.

The feed interface sat on her nightstand. She'd dropped it there sometime after she'd gotten back to her quarters — she didn't remember when exactly, the hours between disconnecting from Tomas's feed and arriving in this room were compressed and dark and not entirely present in her memory. The device was small. About the weight of a watch. The sensor pad on the underside that read her neural activity while the cable connected to the athlete's system port. A scratch on the face from a doorframe in Stuttgart, three years ago. The indicator light was dark.

She went to the bathroom and turned on the shower. The water took thirty seconds to get hot — the facility's plumbing ran through the building's central spine and the coaches' wing was at the end of the line. She stood under the cold water first, then the warming water, then the hot water, and she felt all of it. Her skin. Her temperature. Her lungs expanding with steam. This was the ritual — the post-feed reset, the thing she'd done after every session for three years. Her own body's sensory recalibration. Usually it marked the end of the feed's presence in her system, the return to the flatness of her own unplugged existence. Today the water was just water. The ritual didn't land.

She turned it off and toweled dry and dressed. Grey pants, a pullover, socks. She looked at herself in the mirror — the bathroom mirror was small and the lighting was fluorescent, designed to help you check for visible injuries, not to be kind. A thirty-five-year-old woman with a former swimmer's shoulders and hips that had widened past her competition frame. The body that had placed fifth at the Olympics and then stopped mattering. The body she'd spent three years escaping through the feed.

She picked up the interface from the nightstand and turned it over in her hands. The sensor pad was smooth, slightly concave, designed to cup the wrist. Her right wrist had a faint indentation from wearing it — three years of daily use, hours at a time, the device pressed against her skin while she lived inside someone else's effort. She set it down again. Her pulse didn't change. That was how she knew the thing between her and the device was already over. Not because she'd decided to stop. Because something structural had shifted, the way a bone breaks — not gradually but all at once, and the sound it made was silence.

She sat on the bed and looked at the window. The spring light was different from what she'd gotten used to — brighter, the sun's angle higher, cutting across her desk at a slant that reached the far wall for the first time since she'd moved in. Outside, the snow had retreated above the tree line on the south-facing slopes. Brown earth below, mud and scree, the skeleton of the landscape visible after months of burial. The drainage channels along the building's foundation carried snowmelt in a continuous murmur, day and night.

Twelve beats per minute. That was the number. She'd checked the telemetry from yesterday's session — it was still cached on the interface, the last session it would ever record — and the number was precise. Tomas's heart rate had been running twelve beats per minute above his target zone when the blackout hit. She'd put it there. She'd pushed his pacing parameters because the feed resolution sharpened above the safety margin, because his body's exertion at that intensity made the borrowed sensation so clear that her own body disappeared, because that disappearance was the thing she needed.

Twelve beats per minute. An elevated cardiovascular load during a virus-triggered blackout. The blood pressure higher. The tissue under more mechanical stress. The impact on his spine arriving in a body that was already running hotter than it should have been. She didn't know if those twelve beats made the difference between a recoverable injury and a permanent one. A biomechanics team could model it and they'd give her a probability distribution, a confidence interval, a range of outcomes with varying likelihoods. Not an answer. The answer was locked inside a single event in a single body at a single moment, and that moment was gone and the result was a twenty-one-year-old kid from Quito who couldn't feel his legs.

She didn't cry. She wasn't going to cry. Crying would have been a reaction to her own distress, and her distress wasn't the thing that mattered. What mattered was in the medical bay, behind a closed door she could see from the corridor window if she walked to the end of the hall, and it would never walk out of that room on its own legs.

The phantom skipped again, her right leg this time, and she sat still until it passed.

---

Petra sent for her at nine. The notification appeared on the wall-mounted display in her quarters — a blue rectangle with Dr. Engel's name and a room number and a time that was thirty-two minutes from now. The display also showed Margot's own biometrics — resting heart rate, sleep quality index, enhancement interface status. The interface status read INACTIVE. It had read ACTIVE every morning for three years.

She walked to the medical wing. Four minutes through the connecting corridor, past the windows that faced the south slope where the snowmelt was turning the access road to mud. Two athletes passed her going the other direction — a middle-distance runner whose name she knew and a gymnast whose name she didn't — and neither of them looked at her. Either they hadn't heard yet or they had heard and were choosing not to look. Both options sat in her stomach the same way.

The phantom hit at the junction between the residential wing and the medical wing — the left leg, the proprioceptive skip, the fraction-of-a-second gap. She caught the wall and kept walking. A maintenance worker at the far end of the corridor glanced at her and looked away.

Petra's office was glass-walled, adjacent to the medical bay proper. Through the glass, if you looked past Petra's desk and through the interior window on the far wall, you could see the corridor that led to the recovery rooms. Tomas's door was closed. It had been closed since they brought him in yesterday.

Petra was standing when Margot entered. That was wrong. Petra was a deliberate sitter — she positioned herself at eye level, a clinical habit that communicated equality even when the power was entirely hers. Standing meant she hadn't settled. Or hadn't been able to.

"Close the door."

Margot closed it.

Two screens on Petra's desk showed data Margot recognized instantly. Feed session logs. The format her interface generated automatically every time she connected to an athlete: heart-rate tracking, respiratory rate, neural feedback markers, session duration, connection quality. Weeks of sessions, displayed as time series. Colored lines.

"Sit down," Petra said.

Margot sat. The chair was the standard medical-office type — padded, adjustable, designed for patients receiving news. She'd sat across from Petra in this chair twice before, both times for routine coaching certifications. Those meetings had lasted fifteen minutes. This one would be different.

Petra turned the left screen so Margot could see it clearly. "Your feed session logs with Tomas Reyes. February eighth through yesterday. Thirty-one sessions."

The data was clean, organized by date, each session a separate track. The target heart-rate zone — the zone Margot had filed in her session plans — was marked in green. The actual heart rate was marked in red. The red lines were above the green lines. In every session. Every one.

"Average deviation of twelve beats per minute above the prescribed training zone," Petra said. She spoke with the precision of someone reading findings, not accusations. Clinical. But her eyes were not clinical. "The pattern is consistent. Not sporadic, not variable. Consistent across the full six-week period."

"Yes," Margot said.

Petra waited. When Margot didn't add anything, she tapped the screen and scrolled to a second dataset. Different athlete. Same pattern: target below, actual above, the gap steady and systematic.

"Lena Morozova. Twenty-three sessions. Average deviation of eight beats per minute." Another scroll. "David Kim. Fourteen sessions. Average deviation of fifteen beats." Another. "Annika Brandt. Nine sessions. Eleven beats."

Four athletes. Weeks of sessions. Systematic elevation. No documented medical justification for any of it.

"I'm not going to ask you why," Petra said. "The telemetry is clear enough."

Margot looked at the screen. The colored lines, the gap between what she'd promised and what she'd done, rendered in clean data that anyone with access could read. It had always been findable. The logs were generated automatically. She'd never deleted them. She'd never even considered deleting them. That fact said something about her that she didn't particularly want to examine — that she'd been so certain no one would look, or so indifferent to the possibility, that she hadn't bothered to cover anything.

"I've also reviewed the network pathway data with Yusuf," Petra said. Her voice shifted — less formal, harder. "The neural coaching feed creates a bidirectional data channel. When you connect to an athlete's system, the pathway opens in both directions."

Margot's hands were in her lap. She watched them not move.

"Every feed session you ran opened an additional propagation route for the virus through the shared calibration network." Petra was not hedging now. The qualifiers she used in most professional contexts — *likely*, *consistent with*, *it's possible that* — were gone. "Yusuf has confirmed that the feed pathway is a viable transmission vector. The virus could propagate through a coaching connection the same way it propagates through calibration."

"I know," Margot said.

"When did you know."

"February. After the facility went to restricted operations."

"After Kai Brennan's crash."

"Yes."

"You identified the feed as a potential virus vector in February. And you continued running sessions through yesterday."

"Yes."

The silence that followed was the silence of a doctor who has heard the answer she expected and is now recalculating the scope of the damage. Petra sat down — slowly, as if the act required more deliberation than usual. Her chair creaked. She took off her glasses and cleaned them on the hem of her sweater, a gesture Margot had seen before, a thing Petra did with her hands when the rest of her needed to hold still.

"Thirty-one sessions with Tomas. Twenty-three with Lena. Fourteen with Kim. Nine with Brandt. Seventy-seven potential virus exposures beyond the shared calibration network." She put her glasses back on. "Seventy-seven sessions where you knew you might be increasing the viral load on athletes whose systems were already compromised."

Margot didn't answer because there was no answer. The number was the answer.

"Your coaching privileges are suspended effective immediately," Petra said. "Your feed interface authorization is revoked at the network level. IT will deactivate your connection credentials by end of day. You're not to connect to any athlete's neural system for any purpose. Can you confirm that verbally."

"I understand. I won't connect."

"You'll retain access to your quarters and common areas. You're not to enter the coaching suite, the calibration bay, or any training environment without my explicit authorization."

"OK."

Petra looked at the closed door of Tomas's recovery room, visible through the layered glass walls. Margot followed her gaze. The door was the same institutional white as every other door in the medical wing, but it was the only one that was closed in the middle of a weekday morning.

"The heart-rate elevation during yesterday's session," Petra said. Her voice was quieter now. More careful. "His cardiovascular load was elevated when the blackout triggered. I've asked the biomechanics team to model whether the elevation contributed to the severity of the spinal outcome."

"It might've," Margot said.

"It might have. Yes." Petra's face did something then — a contraction around the eyes, a brief crumpling that she corrected quickly enough that it almost wasn't visible. But Margot saw it. The face of a woman who had spent years in institutional medicine and still couldn't look at preventable damage without flinching.

"I flagged a rejection risk at the Stuttgart facility four years ago," Petra said. "An enhancement incompatibility in a young gymnast. I wrote the report, filed it through proper channels. Meridian's medical oversight board reviewed my findings and downgraded the risk classification. She died eight months later from the condition I'd identified."

Margot said nothing.

"I'm not comparing what you did to what they did. The mechanisms are different. But the pattern —" She stopped. Started the sentence on a different track. "I've spent four years knowing that I should've made more noise. Should've refused to let the institution's process overrule my clinical judgment. That's what I'm doing now."

"I know what you're doing," Margot said.

Petra nodded once. She looked old in the fluorescent light — the grey streaks in her hair, the lines around her eyes, the particular fatigue of someone who had been tired for years and knew she would be tired for years more.

"You should go," Petra said.

Margot stood. The phantom skipped in her left leg and she caught the edge of Petra's desk with her fingertips. Petra's eyes went to the catch, the recovery, the hand on the desk.

"What was that?"

"Phantom. From the feed. From yesterday. It'll pass."

"When did it start?"

"When I disconnected from Tomas."

Petra's expression went still. "You're carrying phantom sensation from his injury?"

"His legs going dead. It comes in waves. My nervous system stored it."

Petra opened her mouth, closed it, opened it again. "I can order a neural scan. If the phantom is persisting—"

"It'll fade," Margot said. She didn't know if this was true. She said it because the alternative — sitting in a scanner while someone mapped the ghost of Tomas's paralysis in her own neural pathways — was a kind of medical attention she hadn't earned.

She left Petra's office and walked back down the corridor and the phantom hit once more, in the right leg this time, near the junction with the residential wing, and she stopped and stood there with her hand on the wall until it passed and then she kept walking.

---

Kai found her outside.

The facility had a service entrance on the north side — a loading dock that the maintenance crew used for deliveries, a concrete apron bordered by a rusted safety rail that overlooked the valley road and the lower slopes where the snow had retreated. From the dock you could see the road switching back through the tree line all the way down to where it leveled off toward Meiringen. It was the one spot at the facility where you could stand outside and not see any part of the building. Margot was sitting on an overturned equipment crate near the rail, not wearing a jacket, her breath condensing in the spring air.

He'd been with Yusuf most of the morning. The calibration bay, the screens, the data. The structural signatures in the virus code that matched Meridian's proprietary development frameworks — the correlation that was clear enough to an engineer and useless in any context that required proof. Suggestive, Yusuf kept saying. The word engineers used when they were certain but couldn't demonstrate it in language that would survive a legal challenge or a press conference. Five weeks to the opening ceremony and they had a conspiracy they could think but not speak, a paralyzed kid in the medical bay, and a corporate entity that had sold the cure for a disease it had probably created.

And then Lena had told him about Margot's suspension. Flat, factual, the way Lena delivered information when she was trying not to feel it yet. Petra pulled her feed logs. The heart-rate data. The sessions with Tomas, with Lena, with others. The suspension.

With Lena. The words sat in Kai's chest like something swallowed wrong. Margot had been plugged into Lena in the pool. Margot had watched Lena's personality thin to nothing through the feed and had pushed her heart rate higher because the dissolution was its own kind of rush, and then Lena had surfaced unable to remember two laps of her own life, and Margot had looked at her and lied.

He'd asked Lena how she was and she'd said fine and he'd known she wasn't fine and he'd also known that pushing her on it right now would be the wrong thing. So he'd left the question and gone to find Margot.

His left wrist ached. It always ached now — the bone healed too fast by the synthetic grafts, a wrongness in the joint that flared with barometric shifts. Spring in the Alps was all pressure changes. He flexed his hand open and closed as he walked across the loading dock toward Margot and the ache was a useful thing to notice, a physical problem with a physical location, not the other kind.

She looked up when she heard him. Her face was flat. Not composed. Flat. The humor that she usually carried like a blade — the sharp, self-aware darkness that made people uncomfortable when they realized she wasn't entirely joking — was gone. What was left was the face of someone who had stopped constructing anything. She didn't look guilty. She didn't look sorry. She looked like what she was.

"Lena," he said.

Her eyes didn't change. "Yeah."

"You were in the feed when she desync'd in the pool. You saw how bad it was."

"I saw."

"And you told her it was within acceptable parameters."

"That's what I told her."

He stood there. He wasn't going to do this from above — standing over her, looking down. But he also wasn't going to sit next to her, because sitting next to her would be a kind of comfort and comfort wasn't what he'd come with. So he stood at the edge of the dock, three feet from her crate, close enough to talk and far enough to not be sharing anything.

"She lost two laps. She was in the water and she wasn't there and you were inside her system watching it happen and you pushed her heart rate up."

"I pushed it up."

"Because the desync made the feed better."

Margot looked at him and her face didn't flinch and that was the worst part — the absence of flinch, the fact that she could hear this said back to her and not react, not because she was hard but because she'd already said it to herself so many times that hearing it from someone else was just a repetition. "The desync sharpened the resolution. When her personality thinned, the sensory data got clearer. More intense. I — yeah. It made the feed better."

"And you knew her desync was worse than anyone had reported."

"I knew it was bad. Worse than her charts showed. Worse than what I'd told her."

"And you didn't say anything."

"No."

"Because saying something meant losing the feed."

"Because saying something meant admitting how deep I was in it. Which meant losing the feed. Which meant losing —" She stopped. Not a dramatic stop. A recalibration. She was stripping the sentence of anything that might sound like a reason instead of a fact. "Which meant being done. And I wasn't ready to be done."

A truck engine murmured somewhere below, on the valley road. The sound carried in the thin air, distant and mechanical, and then faded as the truck rounded a switchback. The mountains across the valley were still mostly white but the snow had pulled back from the rock faces and the dark stone underneath was wet and glinting in the midday sun.

"Tell me what it is," Kai said. Not a question. Not exactly a demand. The tone was flat in the way his voice went flat when the humor was completely gone, when there was nothing between the words and the feeling underneath them. "The feed. What it is for you. I want to hear you say it."

Margot looked at her hands. The right wrist, bare now, the indentation where the interface had been.

"You plug in," she said. "You connect to their system. Their sensory data comes through. Not a simulation — the actual signals, translated. When they run, you feel the legs. When they swim, you feel the water."

She paused to breathe. The air was cold. She'd been outside long enough that her lips were dry and her fingers had gone pink.

"When the heart rate goes up, the resolution sharpens. Everything gets more present. Their body feels more real than yours does. At a certain point, and it's different for each athlete, your own body disappears. You forget you're in a room somewhere with a device on your wrist. There's just their body, their effort. The feeling of competing at that level. The muscles, the lungs, the focus."

"And that's what you wanted."

"Every time. For three years."

"Three years."

"I started in Stuttgart. Before I came here. First time was an accident — I set a calibration wrong and the athlete's heart rate spiked and the feed got sharp and I — I noticed. What it did. How it felt." She wasn't looking at him. She was looking at the valley, the road, the distant line of the tree cover. "I did it again. Figured out the numbers. Got specific about it. Every athlete's got a threshold where the feed peaks. I learned each one."

"Tomas's was twelve."

"Twelve above target zone. Yeah."

Kai's wrist ached and he pressed it against the cold safety rail and the metal bit into the bone and the sharpness was clarifying.

"When I stopped competing," Margot said, and her voice was the flattest thing he'd ever heard from her — no humor, no edge, no self-awareness performed for anyone's benefit, just a person reciting the shape of a problem she hadn't solved, "I didn't stop wanting it. That's not how it goes. You just stop being allowed. Your body isn't fast enough or your ranking drops or you're thirty-five and seven kilos past your race weight and your best stroke time is a joke. But the wanting doesn't know any of that. The wanting still thinks you're in the water."

She looked at the wrist where the interface had been.

"The feed was the closest thing. Someone else's body doing what mine used to do. The intensity. The physical fact of competing at that level. Through the feed it was real." She said this without inflection. "Through the feed I was still in it."

He sat down on the edge of the dock. Not a decision — his body needed to be lower. The concrete was cold through his pants and his wrist was cold from the rail and the cold was fine. He could feel the cold.

"And the virus," he said. "You knew the feed was a vector."

"I figured it out in February. The bidirectional pathway. Every time I connected, there was a propagation risk."

"And you kept connecting."

"I kept connecting."

"Seventy-seven sessions. That's what Petra said."

"That sounds right."

He looked at her. She looked back. Her face was red from the cold and her eyes were dry and she was giving him the plainest version of herself she had, which was the truth without anything around it. No defense. No performance of remorse that would give him something to accept or reject. Just the facts of what she'd done, laid out in simple sentences by a person who'd run out of more complicated ways to say them.

"I'm not going to tell you I didn't know what I was doing," she said. "I knew. I'm not going to tell you I couldn't stop. I could've. I didn't because stopping meant being just me, in my own body, with my own senses, and that wasn't enough." She paused. "That's the whole thing. There's no deeper version."

He didn't forgive her. The word didn't enter his mind. Forgiveness would have been a transaction, something exchanged, and what Margot had done to Lena was not a thing with a price in forgiveness. She had been inside Lena's system. She had watched Lena disappear inside herself. She had used that disappearance for her own gratification and then lied about it. And Lena — Lena, who checked whether every sentence she spoke was one she'd chosen to say, who touched the neural port at the base of her skull the way you'd touch a scar, whose trust in the people calibrating her body was the only trust she had because the body was the only thing she'd ever known — Lena had believed her. Had accepted the lie because the lie came from a coach, and coaches were the system, and the system was what Lena was.

"I'm sorry," Margot said. The word was plain. Not weighted, not performed, not asking for anything in return. An acknowledgment of a specific damage from the person who'd caused it. "About Lena. About what I did to her."

Kai stood up. The concrete had left a cold strip across the backs of his thighs and his wrist throbbed with the pressure change and the mountains were there, patient and indifferent.

"Yeah," he said.

He went back inside. Behind him, Margot stayed on the crate in the cold. He didn't look back to see whether she was still sitting there or whether the phantom had caught her leg again. He walked through the loading dock entrance and back into the facility's heated corridors and the warmth on his face felt like returning to a building where everything was wrong and would stay wrong and the opening ceremony was five weeks away and Tomas's door was still closed.


# Chapter 16

The light changed over four weeks. Snow retreated up the ridges until only the highest peaks still held it, and the runoff cut new channels down the rock faces visible through the cafeteria windows. The terraces opened. Athletes who'd spent months under fluorescent light stood in actual alpine sun and squinted at the green that had replaced the grey.

The facility emptied in stages. Those who'd signed Meridian's contract left first — patched, data-licensed, their departures arranged through corporate travel. A sprinter from the Czech team. The pair of gymnasts from South Korea. The relay group, all four of them, boarding the shuttle to Zurich on the same Tuesday morning with matching duffel bags and matching firmware. Each departure thinned the cafeteria by a face, the morning track sessions by a body. By late June the residential wing was half-dark, most of the room displays glowing their real-time diagnostics to nobody.

The holdouts were a smaller group than the refusals in February. Time and Tomás had done their work. Most athletes who'd initially refused had come around — watched the footage of the crash, thought about their own bodies mid-stride or mid-tumble or mid-rotation, and signed. The remaining holdouts numbered maybe eight, spread across the Kirchberg campus and the satellite facility in Davos. Some ran Yusuf's partial patch. Some ran nothing and accepted the risk with a clarity that looked either brave or reckless depending on who was judging.

Kai ran nothing. He trained on the indoor track alone most mornings, running two hundreds in the facility's thin air, the rubber smell and the echo of his footfalls the same as they'd been in January except the light through the high windows was different now — brighter, longer, actual daylight instead of the winter's grey approximation. His wrist ached on cold mornings, the bone where the break had knitted too fast — a shallow wrongness, like the memory of an injury rather than the injury itself. He'd stopped wearing the brace weeks ago but the ache stayed. He ran with it. He ran paying attention to something most people couldn't feel: the synthetic muscle's autonomous subsystems, the tiny pre-movements in his quadriceps and calves that fired fractionally ahead of his conscious intent. He'd been training himself to notice the gap between what he told his legs to do and what they did first. He did not call this practice anything. He just ran, and paid attention, and ran again.

Lena swam. The pool was less crowded now, which was its own kind of strange — she'd trained in packed lanes her entire life, and the empty lanes beside her made the water feel wider and flatter, as though something had been subtracted from it. Her times were slower than her optimization peak by margins that would have been unacceptable six months ago. She swam them anyway. The partial patch held. The silence in her head where the system's ambient processing had been was becoming, if not comfortable, then at least recognizable — a space she could orient herself inside of, the way your eyes adjusted to a room after someone turned the light off.

Yusuf worked. He gave the partial patch to two more athletes in the third week — a hurdler from Jamaica and a pole vaulter from New Zealand, both holdouts who'd decided the risk of competing unpatched outweighed the risk of an untested firmware rewrite. The installations went smoothly. Both athletes reported the same things Lena had: blunted sensory acuity, slower reaction times, and a new quiet in their heads that they described in different words but that Yusuf recognized as the same phenomenon. He filed reports. He calibrated the remaining athletes on their adjusted schedules and answered technical questions from the Davos satellite team over encrypted channels. He called Farah on Thursdays and listened to her talk about the card game she was winning, her voice the same warm voice it had always been, her speech just slightly slower than it used to be in a way that he measured against last week and the week before that the way he measured everything — in increments, in data, in the precise language of decline.

Margot walked the corridors. She had nowhere to be and nowhere else to go. Her coaching suspension was total — no feed access, no training supervision, no athlete contact in any professional capacity. She'd been offered a transfer to Meridian's administrative center in Zurich, which she understood correctly as a polite suggestion that she leave, and she'd declined it with three words. She still had her quarters. She still ate in the cafeteria. She still walked the corridors of the facility in the mornings and evenings, her body moving through spaces that had been defined by her work and were now just hallways. She was sleeping badly. She'd taken to running the outdoor loop around the facility in the late afternoons — three kilometers of gravel path that switchbacked down to the treeline and back up, the altitude making her lungs burn in a way she didn't mind because it was at least a sensation she'd chosen. She was not drinking, which was something she noticed herself noticing, because it would have been easy and because the facility bar in the common room stayed open until eleven and nobody was watching.

The night before the team bus left for the Zurich airport, Yusuf made biryani.

---

His mother had sent a care package in March — a padded envelope that arrived battered from Birmingham with two bags of basmati, a jar of her own garam masala, saffron threads in a twist of cling film, a bag of fried onions gone slightly soft at the edges, and a note in Urdu that said *Eat properly.* He'd been saving it. The biryani was a production. He'd negotiated use of the cafeteria kitchen from the night cook, a woman named Ursula who watched him toast whole spices in a dry pan with visible skepticism and then left him to it. The rice soaked in salted water. The chicken — thighs, because his mother had opinions about breast meat that she had passed on to him genetically, or at least through repetition — had been marinating since the afternoon in yogurt and her masala and a quantity of green chilis that would clear out anyone who wasn't ready.

Kai was the first to arrive. He came in through the side entrance from the track, hair still damp from the shower, and stopped when the smell hit him.

"That's not cafeteria food."

"Nah." Yusuf was standing at the kitchen pass-through, stirring something with a wooden spoon that belonged to Ursula. "My mum's biryani. Or my attempt at it. She'd tell me I've done something wrong with the rice."

"Your mom sent you rice?"

"Rice, spices, guilt. The usual care package." Yusuf wiped his hands on a towel he'd tucked into his belt. "Grab plates, will you? The stack by the serving line."

Kai got plates. The cafeteria was mostly empty — two athletes from the French sprint relay eating protein bowls at the far end, a physiotherapist reading on her tablet near the window. The windows themselves were golden with late-evening light, the sun sitting low above the western ridge and making the snowfields there look like they were lit from underneath. Past eight and still fully bright. The days had gotten very long.

Lena came in from the pool side, her hair wet and carrying the faint chlorine trace it always did. She sat down across from Kai without ceremony, looked at the plates, and said, "Is that Yusuf's?"

"His mom's recipe. Allegedly."

"Good." She pulled the plate closer. "The cafeteria chicken's been the same chicken for three days."

"It's the same chicken every day," Kai said. "They just move it to different trays."

"That isn't true."

"I know. But it feels true."

Margot arrived last. She stood in the entrance for a moment — not hesitating, but registering the scene before joining it. She was wearing a grey sweatshirt with nothing on it, the kind of thing you put on when you'd stopped thinking about what you were wearing. She sat down next to Lena, which put her across from where Yusuf's plate was waiting.

"Biryani," Margot said, looking at the serving dish Yusuf was carrying to the table with both hands.

"Biryani," Yusuf confirmed.

"Your mum's?"

"My mum's spices, my execution. Don't hold her responsible."

"I never do." Margot took the serving spoon and started filling her plate. The rice was golden-brown in layers, the chicken underneath, the fried onions on top. The smell was cumin and saffron and the particular warmth of cardamom that wasn't sweet exactly but made you think of sweetness.

Yusuf sat down. "It needs raita and I haven't got any yogurt, so." He gestured at the table as though the absence of yogurt was a physical object they could all see.

"It's fine," Lena said. She was already eating. "It's really good."

"Don't sound surprised."

"I'm not surprised. I said it's good."

For a while they just ate. The biryani was good — the rice had that texture where each grain held its shape but gave under your teeth, the chicken was tender and spiced in layers that arrived at different points in the bite, and the chilis were enough to make Kai's eyes water on the second mouthful but not enough to stop him going back for a third.

"Christ," Kai said, after a particularly loaded bite. He reached for the water.

"The chilis?" Yusuf didn't look up from his plate.

"Yeah."

"My mum goes heavier. This is the mild version."

"This is mild?"

"For Birmingham, yeah."

Margot ate through the heat without comment, which tracked with everything Kai knew about Margot. Yusuf ate steadily, the way he did everything — focused, economical, not especially social about it. Lena ate with the same precision she brought to most physical activities, small bites, thorough. Margot ate like she was hungry, which she probably was. Kai had noticed she'd been skipping meals earlier in the month. He hadn't said anything because it wasn't his business and because Margot would have told him to fuck off.

The French relay athletes finished and left. The physiotherapist left. The cafeteria was theirs.

"So," Margot said. She set her fork down. "Big day tomorrow."

"Yeah," Kai said.

"Plane to Zurich. Zurich to Doha. Doha to forty degrees and whatever Meridian's built out there."

"You've been reading the briefing packet," Yusuf said.

"I've had time to read." Margot picked up her fork again, pointed it at nothing in particular. "I've had a lot of time to read."

The joke was in her delivery more than the words — dry, aimed at herself, the timing landing somewhere between funny and not. Yusuf exhaled a short laugh. Kai smiled. Lena looked at Margot with an expression that held several things at once and then she smiled too, and the four of them sat in it for a moment, the fact that it was funny and that nothing about the reason for it was funny, and the laughter was brief and real and didn't try to become anything larger than itself.

"What are you going to do?" Lena asked.

"While you're all in Doha?" Margot shrugged. "Walk the halls. Eat the chicken. Maybe finally figure out the night cook's name."

"Ursula," Yusuf said.

"See, you've ruined my one project."

Kai finished his plate and served himself more. The light outside was starting to deepen, the gold thinning toward amber as the sun dropped behind the ridge. The snowfields on the western peaks had gone from luminous to just white.

"My mom keeps sending me clips," he said. "Of the old races. The natural-category ones."

Lena looked at him. "Before the rebuild?"

"Yeah. She's got them all saved somewhere — her phone or some cloud thing. She sends them with no caption. Just the video."

"Does she want you to watch them?" Yusuf asked.

"I think she wants me to know she's watching them." Kai looked at the rim of rice on his plate. "She says she can tell the difference. Between those and the enhanced footage."

"Can she?" Margot asked.

"I don't know. Maybe. The old ones — I look different in them. Looser. I used to do this thing with my arms coming out of the blocks, this kind of — " He made a motion with his elbows that looked like nothing specific. "She says she can see the moment where I decide to kick, and in the new footage I don't decide, I just go."

Nobody said anything for a moment. Lena was watching him with that particular attention she gave to descriptions of bodies doing things their owners hadn't commanded — the stillness of someone hearing their own condition described in different terms.

"She's probably right," Kai said. "She's been watching me run since I was nine. She'd know."

Yusuf got up and brought back water for the table — four glasses from the stack by the serving line. He sat down and drank half of his.

"Farah's been on a streak," he said.

"Your sister?" Lena asked.

"Yeah. She plays this — I don't even know what it is. Some card game her flatmate taught her, like rummy but with rules I can't follow. She wins constantly. She rang me Thursday and spent twenty minutes explaining a hand she'd played. I understood none of it."

"Is she good at cards generally?" Kai asked.

"She's good at everything she decides to be good at. It's annoying. It's always been like that." Yusuf's voice was easy when he talked about Farah — not the careful tone he used when her condition came up in professional contexts, just a brother complaining about his sister with visible pride underneath. "She's got this thing where she pretends she doesn't know the rules so people underestimate her, and then she cleans them out. She did it to my cousin at Eid and he didn't speak to her for a week."

"Good for her," Margot said.

"It's proper evil, is what it is."

"I said good for her."

Yusuf smiled at his plate. The biryani was mostly gone. Remnants of rice clung to the serving dish in a golden-brown crust, and Yusuf scraped at it absently with the edge of the spoon — a gesture that said something about how dishes were done in his household, every grain accounted for.

Lena had been quiet for a few minutes. She was looking out the window at the mountains and the last of the light. Her hand went to the back of her neck, where the neural link port sat under her hair. She touched it and brought her hand back down, and it was unclear whether the gesture was deliberate or the unconscious check Kai had seen her make dozens of times.

"It's getting easier," she said. "The quiet."

Nobody asked what she meant.
