"The first week after the patch, I kept — checking. Like when you think you've left the stove on, except there isn't a stove. There never was a stove." She turned her water glass in her hand. "Something used to be running in the background and now it isn't, and my brain keeps reaching for it. I think for the first week I confused my own thoughts with symptoms. I'd be thinking something, and then I'd stop and think, was that me? Because I'd never had to ask that before. Everything was just — everything. The system and me were the same signal."

She set the glass down.

"Now I can hear the difference. Most of the time. My thoughts are slower, and they have edges — like, I can feel where one stops and another one starts. The system used to smooth that out. Everything flowed. And now it doesn't flow, and I keep thinking something's broken, and then I remember that's just how thinking works if you're doing it yourself."

"Is it better?" Kai asked.

Lena considered this for a long moment, her thumb against the side of the water glass.

"It's mine," she said. "I don't know if that's the same thing."

Outside, the light was going. The sun had dropped fully behind the ridge and the sky above it was streaked orange and pale rose in bands that faded upward into a blue so deep it was nearly the color it would be in twenty minutes when the stars came out. The mountains held the last of the day on their snow caps — bright against the darkening sky, almost phosphorescent — and then that faded too, and the cafeteria windows became mirrors, reflecting the four of them back at themselves — four people sitting at a table, plates pushed aside, the fluorescent strip above the serving line casting the only light in the room.

"Right," Yusuf said eventually. "Dishes."

"I'll do them," Margot said.

"You don't have to — "

"I know I don't have to. Let me do the dishes."

She stood and started stacking plates. Yusuf let her. Kai cleared the serving dish and the glasses, carrying them to the kitchen pass-through where Margot ran water into the industrial sink. The sound of it — water hitting steel, the clank of ceramic being set down — was ordinary. That was all it was.

Lena stayed at the table. She was looking at the dark windows where the mountains had been. Her hand went to the back of her neck again and she left it there this time, her fingers resting against the small circle of the port.

Yusuf dried as Margot washed. They didn't talk much — a few words about where things went, Margot asking which shelf the serving dish belonged on, Yusuf reaching past her to put it back because she couldn't quite get to it. The efficiency of two people in a kitchen who didn't need to discuss what they were doing.

When the dishes were done, Margot dried her hands on the towel Yusuf had been wearing in his belt. She folded it and laid it on the counter.

"Fly safe," she said. To the room, not to anyone in particular.

"Yeah," Kai said.

"Compete — " She stopped. Whatever she'd been about to say, she thought better of it, or she couldn't find the version of it that didn't sound like coaching. "Just. Yeah."

"Yeah," Yusuf said.

Margot left through the side entrance that led to the residential wing. The door swung shut behind her with the hydraulic hiss that all the facility doors made, a sound they'd heard ten thousand times and that Kai realized he wouldn't hear again for weeks. Then it was three of them.

Yusuf stretched his back and his spine cracked audibly. "I should pack. I haven't packed a thing. I've been making biryani instead of packing."

"Good trade," Kai said.

"Tell that to my bag at four in the morning." He pushed his chair back. "Night, both of you."

"Night."

"Goodnight, Yusuf. And thank you. For the food."

"Thank my mum. I'll tell her someone appreciated it." He raised a hand without turning around and walked out, and the door hissed shut again, and it was Kai and Lena in the empty cafeteria.

The fluorescent strip hummed. The windows were fully dark now. The smell of cumin and saffron and his mother's garam masala still hung in the air, fainter, thinning toward nothing the way smells did when the source was gone and all that was left was what the room had absorbed.

"You all right?" Kai asked.

"Yes." Lena brought her hand down from the back of her neck. "I was thinking about how many meals I've eaten in this room."

"A lot?"

"Since I was sixteen. So." She looked at the table, the surface wiped clean, the chairs pushed back to where they'd been before any of this. "It won't feel like this again."

She wasn't being dramatic. She was describing something she could see clearly — that whatever happened in Doha, the race, the result, whatever public reckoning followed everything that had gone wrong at this facility, the four of them would not sit at this table again in the same configuration. Margot would be somewhere else by the time they came back, if they came back. The investigation Yusuf's data might trigger would change what this place was, or close it.

"No," Kai said. "Probably not."

Lena stood and came around the table to where he was sitting. She stood next to his chair and he reached for her hand and she gave it to him — his left hand, the one with the wrist that ached, her right hand, cool from the water glass. They stayed like that for a while in the empty cafeteria, the fluorescent strip humming above them and the smell of someone's mother's cooking still faintly in the air.

Then they turned off the light and went to pack.


# Chapter 17

The ready room was underground. Lena could feel the weight of the venue above her — concrete and glass and steel, the forty thousand seats, the broadcast rigs that would carry this race to however many millions were watching enhanced women swim the 400m freestyle final in Doha on a Thursday evening. She sat on a bench against the wall with her towel across her knees and her goggles hanging from her left hand, and she tried not to think about how quiet her head was.

Seven other swimmers in the room. She knew most of them by body before she knew them by name — years on the circuit, in warm-up pools, in ready rooms like this one. Adélaïde Moreau stretched in the corner, her hip flexor work so fluid that each adjustment melted into the next, the system-assisted micro-corrections running underneath her movement like a current under smooth water. She had a saint medallion on a chain around her neck that she tucked inside her suit before every race, a habit Lena had seen at three different meets. Kaede Watanabe sat against the opposite wall with her eyes closed, lips moving slightly — running a kinesthetic rehearsal through her neural link, pre-swimming the race in her muscles before her body got wet. Watanabe's feet tapped the floor in the rhythm of her kick turnover, unconscious, metronomic. Nina Okoro paced along the far wall, her calves twitching with a calibrated readiness that looked less like nerves and more like a machine idling. She had tape on three fingers of her left hand, bright green, the kind you bought at a pharmacy.

All of them on Meridian's patch. All of them running integrated systems that smoothed the space between intent and action until there was no space at all — thought becoming motion, seamless, optimized, the body a tool that never surprised the person using it.

Lena touched the port at the base of her skull. A habit she'd had since childhood. The hardware was still there. Yusuf's patch hadn't removed it — it had just quieted the conversation. The system still ran, still processed sensory input and motor commands at some reduced level, but the ambient optimization layer that had been adjusting her stroke pattern, managing her fatigue distribution, smoothing her kick timing since she was four years old — that was silent. Had been silent for weeks now. She was still getting used to the quiet. Some mornings she woke and reached for the background hum of system activity the way you'd reach for a phone on a nightstand, and the absence startled her all over again.

"Morozova. Lane five."

The marshaling official was a short woman with a clipboard and an earpiece, her polo shirt dark with sweat at the collar despite the air conditioning. Lena stood, folded her towel, left it on the bench. The corridor to the pool deck was fluorescent and cold, the venue's cooling system working hard against the forty-two degrees pressing against the building from outside. She could feel the temperature differential in the walls when she walked close — cold air on her skin, the wall itself faintly warm, the desert trying to get in.

The pool deck opened up above her.

The lights were immense. The water was a rectangle of chlorinated blue-green under broadcast illumination so flat and bright that the surface looked solid, like tile. The noise hit her chest before her ears organized it — a wall of human presence, forty thousand people compressed by the venue's glass-and-steel architecture into a sound that was less like cheering and more like weather. Cameras tracked on rails along the pool edge. A drone hovered at the far end, its red indicator blinking, patient, waiting for something worth following.

Lane five. She walked to her block. The starting block was warm under her bare feet — the lights, the accumulated heat of an evening session in a building that was fighting the Doha summer and losing by degrees. She adjusted her goggles, pulled them down over her eyes, and the world contracted to the lane, the water, the wall fifty meters away.

The other swimmers took their blocks. She could see Moreau in lane four, the saint medallion tucked away now, her body coiled. Watanabe in three. In the old days — Lena's old days, the ones with the system running at full integration — she would have felt the block's surface with enhanced tactile resolution, would have calculated her dive angle through a blend of conscious intent and system optimization that made the entry feel inevitable, like falling into a shape that already existed. Now the block was a block. Her feet were feet. The dive would be hers and it would be imperfect and it would be hers.

The starting tone.

She hit the water. The shock of it — not the temperature, which was regulated to twenty-seven degrees, but the texture. Ordinary water. It had weight and resistance and it did not resolve into the granular sensory language she had lived inside for twenty-two years. Before the patch, the water had spoken to her — every eddy and pressure gradient a signal, the drag against her skin a continuous data stream her system translated into micro-adjustments of hand angle, kick depth, body rotation. Now it was water. Wet, dense, chlorinated, cold where the circulation pushed a current sideways across her lane.

She pulled. Her catch was clean — she could feel that without the system confirming it, which was its own small strangeness. Shoulder engaged, elbow high, the pull through to her hip with the forearm pressing the water backward. Her kick was her own rhythm, not the calibrated two-beat flutter the system used to impose. She breathed on the third stroke, rotated her head, got air that tasted like chlorine and the venue's recycled coolness, and put her face back in.

First fifty. She felt the wall coming, loaded her turn, flipped. The rotation was hers — a turn she'd practiced ten thousand times, the muscle memory older and deeper than any firmware version. She came off the wall underwater, dolphin kicked twice, broke the surface, and pulled.

Second fifty. She was close to the field. She could see Moreau's feet in lane four, churning at the same pace, the bubbles trailing behind her kicks in a tight symmetrical pattern that no unassisted swimmer produced. Watanabe was half a body length ahead but that was Watanabe — she went out hard and died in the back half, everyone knew this, the system couldn't fix what the system hadn't built.

Her lungs were working. She noticed this the way she'd been noticing it for weeks now — not the managed version the system used to run, distributing oxygen debt across muscle groups to delay the buildup of lactate, smoothing the burn until it was more signal than suffering. Just her lungs. Filling and emptying on a rhythm her body chose. She was breathing harder than she would have been at this pace six months ago. Effort felt like effort now. That was the patch's cost, or its gift, or both at once.

The wall. She flipped at a hundred meters and came off it.

Third fifty. The gap was opening. Not dramatically — tenths of a second per length, fractions that no spectator could see but that Lena could feel in the distance between herself and Moreau's feet. Moreau was pulling away. In lane six, someone — Lin, from the Shanghai program, Lena thought — was pushing a pace that would have been metabolically suicidal without system-managed oxygen cycling.

Fourth fifty. She flipped and pushed. Her shoulders were starting to burn. Not the clean, distributed signal the system used to produce — a calibrated message that said push harder without the ugliness of actual muscular failure. This was ugly. Lactic acid pooling in her deltoids, the specific grinding sensation of muscle fibers being asked to sustain a contraction rate they couldn't maintain. She pulled through it. Her catch was getting sloppy — she could feel the elbow dropping, the hand slipping wider, the tiny inefficiencies that the system would have corrected in real time and that her body could not correct on its own because her body was busy trying to keep swimming.

Fifth fifty. The field had separated. Moreau and Lin were a full body length ahead now and moving away. Watanabe had died exactly on schedule and was fading beside her. Lena was third, maybe. She wasn't sure. She didn't have the system's lane-position tracking anymore. She had her eyes, when she turned to breathe, and what she saw was the other swimmers' feet getting smaller.

She kept swimming. The water was ordinary and her arms were tired and her kick was losing the snap it had on the first two hundred meters. This was her body. This was what it did without the ambient layer that had been running since before she could form declarative memories. This was the version of Lena Morozova who swam the 400m freestyle alone, in the silence of her own head, every stroke a decision she was making in real time with a brain that was not being assisted.

Sixth fifty. Her shoulders burned. Seventh fifty — the wall, the flip, the push that was weaker than the last push, legs heavy now. Eighth fifty. Last length.

She could hear the crowd through the water — that muffled, distorted roar she'd known her entire life, the sound of thousands of people filtered through a layer of chlorinated liquid. They were cheering, but not for lane five. Not for the swimmer in third whose stroke had gone ragged, whose kick had lost its rhythm, who was clawing toward the finish wall with the gracelessness of someone whose body was doing all the work.

She reached. Her fingers hit the touchpad. She lifted her head and gasped — her mouth open, water running off her face, the air hitting her like something solid after the underwater world.

The scoreboard across the pool: **4:08.71**.

The number meant nothing to anyone who didn't know what it should have been. A year ago, on full integration, she'd swum 3:51.44 in the same event at a meet in Barcelona. The gap — seventeen seconds, an eternity in competitive swimming, the difference between a final and a development-squad time trial — was the cost of silence.

Moreau: 3:49.03. First. Lin: 3:50.88. Second.

Lena: third.

She hung on the lane rope and breathed. The water lapped at her chin. Her lungs were ragged. Her shoulders felt like they'd been packed with gravel. The crowd noise was enormous but it was pointed at the other end of the pool where Moreau was climbing out, one arm raised, the saint medallion swinging where it had worked free of her suit.

Lena pulled herself up and out. The air was cold on her skin — the venue's conditioning winning its fight against the desert in the immediate vicinity of the pool deck, where the water's evaporative cooling helped. She could see the sky through the upper glass panels: white, featureless, the Doha summer pressing its heat against the building like a palm against a window.

Someone handed her a towel. A volunteer, young, wearing an event polo. Lena took it. Wiped her face. Said thank you. The volunteer smiled at her with the uncomplicated warmth of someone who didn't know what any of this meant.

The medal ceremony was thirty minutes later. Lena stood on the third step of the podium, which was lower than the first step in a way that everyone understood. Her hair was wet and smelled of chlorine. The bronze medal was heavier than she expected, or maybe it only felt heavy because she had no system to distribute the awareness of its weight across her sensorium, flattening the signal into background noise. She felt the ribbon against the back of her neck, the metal disc against her sternum, the specific sensation of standing in front of thousands of people who saw a third-place finisher and thought they understood the story.

Moreau on the top step, golden, beaming. Lin beside her. And Lena, on the lowest riser, with a time that was seventeen seconds off her best and a head full of silence, and the knowledge that she had swum every meter of that race under her own power, in a body she had chosen to make quieter, and she had felt every stroke.

She didn't have language for what this was. She stood there and didn't reach for it.

---

Kai had been sitting in the call room for forty minutes and his left wrist had not stopped aching.

It did this. The persistent, dull complaint of bone that had been knitted too fast by synthetic grafts five months ago — the break from the wall at the curve of the indoor track at Kirchberg, the moment the system drove his legs at full sprint speed while his mind was in a void and his body met concrete at thirty-seven kilometers per hour. The wrist had healed in nine days. It had never stopped telling him that nine days was wrong.

He sat on the bench and pressed his thumb into the spot where the ache lived, a point on the lateral side of the scaphoid that felt like a small knot of wrongness buried in the bone, and watched the other sprinters.

Eight men. Seven of them on Meridian's corporate patch. He could tell by the way they moved — the small unconscious calibrations, the smoothness in their warm-up routines. Dekker, from the Dutch program, was doing ankle mobility work on the floor, and the range of motion was inhuman — the synthetic grafts in his Achilles firing a fluid sequence that made the joint look boneless. He had his headphones on and was mouthing the words to something. Okafor stood against the far wall with his eyes closed and his hands hanging loose at his sides, running a pre-race motor rehearsal through his neural bridge — Kai knew the posture because he'd done it himself, hundreds of times, letting the system pre-load the race into his muscles so the start felt like remembering rather than deciding. Okafor's shoulders had a surgical scar from a graft revision, Kai noticed, a thin line running under his left trap that the makeup team had clearly given up on.

Kai wasn't running any rehearsal. His system was what Meridian had installed three years ago: the spinal neural bridge, the bilateral synthetic muscle grafts in both legs, the visual processing enhancement. Unpatched. No Meridian firmware update. No Yusuf's partial patch. Nothing between him and the virus except the original hardware and whatever he'd taught himself in the months since his body ran him into a wall.

Two days ago, in the hotel room, Yusuf had tried one more time. He'd sat on the edge of the bed with his glasses pushed up on his forehead — the way he did when he was going to say something careful — and started: "You know the probability model. At this propagation level, the chance of a triggered event during a two-hundred-meter race is—"

"I know."

"Kai."

"I know what it is." He hadn't been able to offer anything more useful. The rebuilt body was the only body he had. Layering Yusuf's patch on top of the system that was already on top of his original biology would add another seam between him and whatever was left of himself underneath. He couldn't explain this in a way that sounded like anything other than stubbornness, and maybe it was stubbornness, and he'd decided that was fine.

The marshaling official at the call room door: "Two hundred meters, gentlemen. Lane assignments posted. Five minutes."

Kai stood. His wrist ached. His left cheek, where the lacerations from the crash had scarred into faint silver lines that the broadcast makeup team had offered to conceal, pulled slightly when he set his jaw. He'd told them no. The scars were from the last time his body left without him. He wanted to feel them when he ran.

The tunnel to the track was wide, well-lit, built for camera angles. The noise of the stadium reached him as pressure before it became sound — a physical thing, a vibration in the concrete floor and the walls, that climbed as he emerged into the open air of the track.

Not open air. The stadium had a retractable roof, closed against the Doha heat, and the air conditioning turned the enclosed space into something cool and strange — forty-two degrees pressing against glass above, twenty-three degrees on the track surface below. The light was flat and white, broadcast-quality, leaving no shadow on the synthetic rubber surface. Forty thousand people in tiered seats that rose up into the upper reaches of the stadium where the glass roof met steel. He could feel the crowd in his sternum.

Lane seven. Outside. He walked to his block, and the track surface gave slightly under his spikes — the standard synthetic compound that every sprinter in the world knew by feel, the particular combination of grip and return that was neither soft nor hard but something engineered in between. His blocks were set from yesterday's warm-up. He crouched, placed his feet on the pedals, and felt the familiar loading sequence begin: the synthetic grafts in his quadriceps engaging, calves pressurizing, the neural bridge routing the preparation commands through both his biological and synthetic motor pathways.

His body was ready. His body was always ready. That was the problem and it had always been the problem.

"On your marks."

He set. Hands on the line, fingers splayed against the warm rubber, weight tipping forward onto his fingertips and the balls of his feet. The crowd drew breath — forty thousand people going quiet at once, a sound like a room depressurizing. He could feel the neural bridge humming at the base of his spine, that faint vibration most enhanced athletes stopped noticing after the first week. He noticed it. He'd spent months learning to notice it. It was the system, alive, processing, running its preparation scripts, and for now it was doing exactly what he wanted it to do.

"Set."

Hips up. Weight forward. The synthetic muscle in his quads held a potential energy he hadn't consciously generated — the grafts loading themselves, pre-tensing for the drive phase, doing their job. His wrist sent its low pulse of ache up through his arm and he let it be there.

The gun.

His body exploded off the blocks. The first thirty meters were all drive phase — torso pitched forward, legs pumping, the acceleration that topped out at about 11.5 meters per second in an unenhanced human and didn't stop there in his. The synthetic grafts poured power into each stride, the neural bridge adjusting his foot-strike angle in real time, his visual enhancement sharpening the lane into a razor-edged corridor of red surface and white lines. The air resistance hit his face and chest.

Fifty meters. He was in the pack, not leading but close. The sprinters around him were moving at speeds that hadn't been possible before 2031, the year the first grafted athlete ran a sanctioned time. The sound of eight men running flat out on synthetic track — the rhythmic percussion of spikes hitting rubber, the explosive breathing, the faint whine of synthetic muscle cycling at max output in the legs nearest to him.

Eighty meters. The curve. He leaned into it, felt the centripetal load, felt the grafts in his outer leg adjusting the push angle to hold his velocity through the bend. This used to feel like flying. Before the crash, the curve at full speed had been the closest thing to transcendence — his body doing something impossible, smoothly, the seam between him and the system invisible because he wasn't looking for it. Now he felt the seam with every stride. His leg, then the system, then his leg again. Intent, then execution that slightly preceded intent, then his intent catching up. The rhythm of living with hardware that was always a fraction of a second ahead of him.

A hundred meters. The straight. The field strung out — Okafor in lane four had the lead, his stride absurdly long, the turnover rate so fast it looked mechanical because it partly was. Kai was fourth. Maybe fifth. The wrist ached with each arm pump, a tiny flare of wrong that tethered him to his own skeleton.

A hundred and twenty meters. Running. Himself. The scars on his cheek tightening in the stadium's dry air.

A hundred and forty. The crowd was a roar. His legs were doing what he told them to do.

A hundred and fifty.

A hundred and sixty.

His vision cracked.

Not like last time. Not the whiteout, not the void, not the total sensory annihilation that had dropped him into thirty seconds of nothing on the indoor track at Kirchberg. This was different. The track broke apart in front of him — the surface fracturing into geometric fragments that didn't connect, shards of red rubber and white lane line and the far wall of the stadium hanging in his visual field like pieces of a photograph that had been sliced and rearranged wrong. He could see things but not the relationships between them: a triangle of track surface floating above a slice of scoreboard, a fragment of the crowd suspended next to a disconnected piece of his own arm.

The sound went next. Not to silence — to a high, keening frequency that ate everything beneath it. The crowd, the footfalls, his own breathing, all of it replaced by a tone that seemed to originate from inside the bones of his skull.

His legs kept going.

He felt them. Not through normal proprioception, which was breaking apart, disintegrating into the same shattered unreliability as his vision — but through the thing he'd spent weeks and months learning to feel. The gap. The specific, identifiable boundary between his intent and the system's execution. It was there. His quadriceps contracting on a command he had not issued. His calves loading and releasing in a sprint cycle the neural bridge was running autonomously. Push phase, recovery, drive, push — the pattern executing at full power through legs that were no longer receiving instructions from the person they were attached to. The system's job was to maintain the motor sequence. The system did not care whether Kai was present. It had work to do.

His body hurtled forward through the broken geometry of his vision, the screaming tone, the synthetic muscle driving stride after stride, and underneath it all the bridge humming at the base of his spine like a motor bolted to his skeleton.

At Kirchberg, this was where he'd lost. His legs had run him into the wall because he couldn't find the boundary fast enough, couldn't separate his own motor commands from the system's, couldn't tell where he ended and the bridge began.

He found it now.

It felt like reaching through interference. His proprioception was frayed — maybe thirty percent of the signal he normally had, cutting in and out, giving him intermittent flashes of where his body was in space. Enough. He'd trained on less. In his room at Kirchberg, in the weeks after the crash, he'd sat on the edge of his bed and closed his eyes and tried to feel the synthetic muscle in his healing wrist making its tiny autonomous adjustments — the micro-movements that Yusuf's data had first shown him back in January, the system initiating motion a fraction of a second before conscious intent arrived. The same drift that had been a symptom. A malfunction. An erosion of the boundary between person and machine.

He'd spent hours mapping that gap. Sitting still, eyes closed, feeling for the moment his wrist moved before he told it to. Then trying to stop it. Not through the neural interface, not through any digital command pathway, but through his own biology — the original motor neurons, the un-enhanced wiring that Meridian's engineers had built their system on top of. Muscle against muscle. His intent, applied directly, overriding the system's execution through sheer biological stubbornness.

Most nights it hadn't worked. Some nights it had.

He applied it now.

His right quadricep was mid-contraction, the synthetic fibers driving his leg into the next stride at full sprint power. Kai's biological motor signal hit the same muscle with a different instruction. Stop. Not gradually — stop. The signals collided somewhere in the tissue where synthetic and natural fibers were interlaced, and the muscle did what muscles do when they receive contradictory commands: it seized. His right leg locked mid-stride, the momentum carrying his body forward while his foot caught the track surface wrong, and he pitched sideways.

He got his left foot down. The grafts in his left calf fired — they wanted to drive, to sprint, to continue the autonomous pattern — and he caught them before the contraction peaked. Earlier this time. He knew what the firing felt like now, that specific vibration of muscle activating on a signal he hadn't sent, and he clamped down on it with everything he had. His left leg took his weight, shaking, the biological and synthetic components fighting each other inside the same calf, and he did not fall.

He staggered. The track was still in fragments in front of him. The tone was still screaming in his skull. But his legs were his.

He took a step. One step, under his own power, his own intent, his own decision to lift his left foot and place it in front of him. Then his right. The grafts fought him — they wanted to run, they were built to run, their entire engineering purpose was to convert neural commands into forward motion and the neural bridge was still sending commands — and he overrode them again, catching each contraction as it started, applying his biological signal against the system's like pressing his thumb over a pulse he was trying to stop.

His vision was reassembling. The fragments drifted together — edges finding edges, the track surface resolving underneath him in patches, the white lane lines reconnecting. He could see the finish. Maybe thirty meters. The other sprinters had finished seconds ago. The crowd noise was coming back, the tone receding, and what replaced it was not cheering but a murmuring confusion, forty thousand people watching something they didn't have a frame for.

He walked. Each step was a negotiation. His legs wanted to run and he told them no and they compromised on something that looked like walking. His spikes clicked on the track. The air conditioning hummed against the sealed Doha heat. The broadcast cameras had cut away from lane seven long ago, chasing the leaders, but one camera — a handheld, operated by a woman in a press vest who was jogging alongside the track, her own face confused — was still on him.

He crossed the finish line.

The clock said 23.41. Almost six full seconds behind Okafor's winning time, a gap so enormous in elite sprinting that it looked like a timing error, like someone had wandered onto the track from the parking lot.

A race official jogged toward him — a man in his fifties with a laminated credential swinging from his neck and sweat stains darkening the armpits of his blazer, his face arranged in the careful concern of someone trained for medical emergencies. "Sir — Mr. Brennan — are you—"

Kai held up his hand. The left one. The one with the wrist that ached where the bone had been rebuilt too fast, the one the system had healed on a schedule he hadn't set. He waved the man off. He stood on the track, hands on his knees, breathing. The synthetic surface was warm under his feet. The stadium hummed and buzzed around him, the crowd already turning to the next event, the results graphic cycling on the scoreboard.

He was eighth. Last. His name at the bottom of the list, the time beside it an embarrassment to anyone who knew what those numbers were supposed to look like.

Nobody in this stadium understood what had just happened. Not the officials walking toward him with their careful concern. Not the broadcast commentators whose voices echoed off the glass in Arabic and English and French, narrating the story of a sprinter who malfunctioned at the 160-meter mark and limped across the finish — equipment failure, they'd say, a virus event, the risks of competing unpatched. Not the forty thousand people who'd seen a man stumble and stagger and walk the last thirty meters of a race that the winner had covered in 17.63 seconds.

They saw failure. A bad decision validated by a bad outcome. A man who should have taken the patch, who'd been warned, who'd chosen wrong.

Kai stood on the track. His wrist ached. His vision was clear. His legs were shaking, the synthetic and biological components settling back into their uneasy coexistence now that the crisis had passed, the bridge going quiet at the base of his spine.

His legs were his. For the first time since the crash — for the first time since January, since that first calibration session where Yusuf had shown him the data and the data had confirmed what his hand already knew, reaching for the coffee cup a fraction of a second early — his legs had tried to leave without him and he'd said no and the no had held.

The official was still hovering. Kai straightened up.

"I'm fine," he said. His voice sounded strange to him — hoarse, thin, the voice of someone who'd been screaming, though he hadn't been. "I'm good."

He walked off the track. The tunnel back to the call room was cool and quiet. His footsteps echoed. Somewhere above him, the stadium had moved on.


# Chapter 18

Kai sat at an outside table at a café on the Rue du Grand-Pont, his coffee half-finished and the afternoon showing no interest in moving. Lausanne in August: the lake below the old town catching light between buildings, tourists on the steep sidewalks with shopping bags and good shoes, a woman at the next table reading something on a tablet with the absolute attention of someone who did not want to be disturbed. The cathedral's tower was visible above the rooftops, grey stone against a sky that had been blue all day with the settled confidence of a sky that would stay blue until evening.

His wrist ached. It ached every afternoon around this time, a low persistent wrongness deep in the bone where the break from February had healed too quickly. The synthetic grafts had knitted the fracture in nine days. Normal bone would have taken six weeks. The result was a seam inside the bone itself — not a pain, exactly, but a tightness, a place where the architecture of his left wrist did not quite agree with itself. He rotated it. The motion was smooth. The ache held.

The email from SportsVision was still open on his phone, sitting face-down on the table next to the coffee. Third time they'd reached out. They wanted him for the commentary team for the next Apex Games cycle — Kai Brennan, who had finished dead last in the 200 meters in Doha, explaining the sport to an audience who understood the spectacle but not the seam between the athlete and the hardware. He'd be a body-autonomy expert now. The kind of athlete who became a talking head because the story of his failure was more useful than the fact of his performance.

He hadn't said no. He hadn't said yes. The not-deciding felt fine, which was new. For most of his career, indecision had been a condition to get rid of — another thing in the way of the training schedule, the race calendar, the next qualifying heat. Now the not-deciding was just a space he was sitting in, the same way he was sitting in this chair at this café watching Lausanne go by.

A waiter came. Kai ordered another coffee in English and the waiter answered in French and they understood each other fine. The waiter was maybe twenty, with the specific posture of someone who had been on their feet for eight hours and had three more to go.

His right hand picked up the phone. He hadn't decided to pick it up. The motion was the system's — the micro-anticipation of intent that his synthetic grafts ran ahead of his conscious nervous system, the proprioceptive drift that Yusuf had first flagged in a calibration session that felt like it had happened to someone else and had happened seven months ago. His hand held the phone. He looked at it. He put it down again, and that part — the putting down — was entirely his.

This was the daily version. Not a blackout, not the void at the 160-meter mark, not the moment in Doha when his vision fractured and his legs tried to keep running and he stopped them. Just a hand picking up a phone a quarter-second before the brain behind it had caught up. A shoulder that rolled in its socket with a mechanical smoothness — the dislocation repaired, the rotator cuff reattached, the grafts having done their work with the same indifferent efficiency that had knitted the wrist. His body working slightly ahead of him, all day, every day, in small ways that no longer frightened him and that he did not think he would ever stop noticing.

Someone had recognized him on the street yesterday. A man in running shoes, maybe forty, who'd said "tough break" with the sympathetic confidence of a person who had watched the race on a screen and understood the scoreboard. Kai had said "yeah" and the man had meant the race and Kai had meant everything else.

He thought about calling Lena. She was in Montreux, thirty minutes by train, swimming at a municipal center that charged her forty francs a month for a lane and did not ask about the hardware port at the base of her skull. They saw each other two or three times a week. It wasn't dramatic. They cooked, or she cooked and he stood in her kitchen and she put him to work chopping vegetables because she had no patience for someone standing around being useless. They talked, or didn't. When they were in bed the strangeness was still there — his hand adjusting with reflexes that weren't his, the occasional moment where her eyes changed and he waited — but it had become their strangeness, familiar and navigable, and the familiarity was better than the pretending they had both done with everyone else.

He didn't call. The afternoon was good and the coffee was coming and there was no reason to do anything about anything right now.

The waiter set down the cup. Kai thanked him. The cup was warm against his palms — both of them, the left where the bone disagreed with itself and the right where the grafts interfaced with his forearm at a seam he could feel if he pressed. The coffee was good. He drank it and his hand set the cup down on the saucer with a steadiness that was the system's, and his eyes tracked a boat on the lake far below through the gap between buildings, and the afternoon sat there, unhurried, the commentator offer on the table being undecided and the wrist aching and the body doing the small continuous work of being a body, some of it his and some of it not.

A pigeon landed on the back of the empty chair across from him and examined the table with the calculated patience of an animal that had learned where food came from.

"Nothing for you," Kai said.

The pigeon stayed. Kai drank his coffee. The afternoon didn't move.

***

The pool at the Centre Sportif was twenty-five meters, four lanes, and the ceiling was low enough that the humidity collected in visible patches on the concrete. Chlorine and the specific echo of water sounds in an enclosed space — splashing, the slap of a flip turn, the muffled turbulence of air bubbles on the exhale. Not the Kirchberg Institute's fifty-meter Olympic facility with its glass observation deck and temperature-controlled lanes and silent filtration. This was a municipal pool where the lane lines were faded yellow rope and the other swimmers were a retired man doing breaststroke with his head carefully above water and two teenagers who were not training so much as trying to splash each other while maintaining deniability.

Lena swam. Her stroke was clean but not optimized. The catch — her hand entering the water at the beginning of each pull — had a fraction of slippage that her pre-patch neural link would have corrected automatically, adjusting finger spread and wrist angle to maximize hydrodynamic efficiency. Now her hand entered the water the way her hand entered the water, with the minor imperfections of a body executing a motion it had practiced rather than a motion it had been programmed to execute. The difference was two seconds per hundred meters. She knew this because she timed herself, still, every session.

She was on her sixth two-hundred. Breathing every three strokes, which she chose, which she could feel herself choosing — the turn of the head, the intake of air, the return to the streamline, each element a decision she was making in sequence. The silence in her head, where Meridian's neural link had once run constant ambient processing — optimizing her sensory input, pre-filtering sound and sight and touch into the streamlined data feed she had lived inside for twenty-two years — was not silence anymore. She had stopped calling it that sometime in July. It was just the way her mind sounded when it was doing its own work. Messy. Associative. Occasionally blank for a half-second where a thought should have been. The blankness used to frighten her. Now she recognized it as the ordinary lag of a brain that was not outsourcing.

Halfway through the fifth fifty, her left arm smoothed. A micro-adjustment to her catch angle — the same optimization variant from last season's firmware that had surfaced during a training swim at Kirchberg eight months ago and started everything. Not a blackout. Not a desync episode. Just the system's residual pattern, the neural link running a corrective subroutine that Yusuf's patch had not entirely eliminated. It lasted less than a second. She felt it — the specific smoothness of a motion she had not selected, like a word appearing in a sentence she was writing that she hadn't chosen — and stopped at the wall.

She held the gutter and waited. Her breathing was elevated from the swim. The water lapped against the wall tiles in the uneven rhythm of a small pool with multiple swimmers disturbing the surface. She waited for the smoothness to come back. It didn't.

This was what the patch had given her: not a cure, but a margin. She could feel the difference now between a stroke she had chosen and a stroke the system chose for her. The difference was small — a texture in the motion, a quality of volition that she could not have described to anyone who didn't share her condition. But she could feel it, and when she felt the system assert itself she could stop. She could wait. She could push off the wall and swim the next length under her own power, with her own imperfect catch angle and her own two seconds per hundred meters of inefficiency that belonged to her.

She finished the set. The coach at the Montreux center — a woman named Isabelle who had coached junior national swimmers in the nineties and now ran the pool's public training program — was not watching closely. Isabelle didn't micromanage. She gave Lena a set at the beginning of each session and then sat in the bleachers reading a book about Mediterranean cooking and looked up when Lena touched the wall at the end of a rep to mark her time on a clipboard. Isabelle had never asked about the port at the base of Lena's skull. Lena suspected she thought it was cosmetic.

"Good set," Isabelle said, not looking up from the book. "One-oh-nine on the last one."

"Thanks."

"You want another?"

"No, I'm done."

Isabelle made a note on her clipboard. Lena climbed out and stood on the deck, water running off her suit onto the concrete. The retired breaststroke man was still going, his head held above the surface with the determination of someone who believed the pool was trying to get his hair wet. The teenagers had given up swimming entirely and were sitting on the edge of the far lane arguing about something on one of their phones.

In the changing room, Lena sat on the bench in her suit, dripping, and read the article on her phone.

Yusuf had published the analysis three days ago. Not in a journal — on ResearchGate, the open platform where engineers posted pre-prints and data sets, where the audience was anyone with the technical background to evaluate the work. The title was long and flat in the way of academic engineering: "Structural Analysis of Firmware Corruption Patterns in Shared Bio-Integrative Calibration Networks: Correspondence with Proprietary Development Tool Signatures." Under it, the raw data. All of it. The virus code analysis, the Meridian development tool signatures, the correlation metrics. His name and credentials and the Kirchberg Institute listed as his former affiliation.

The article had been live for three days and Meridian's stock had dropped eleven percent. Lena read this in the business section of the NZZ, which she'd pulled up after the ResearchGate link. Meridian had issued a statement denying the findings and announcing an internal review. The Swiss Federal Office of Sport had opened a preliminary inquiry. The European Athletics Enhancement Authority had requested the raw data, which was already public, which was the point.

It was not proof. Yusuf had written this in the article's conclusion with the precise hedging of an engineer who knew the difference between suggestive correspondence and definitive attribution. But the data was there, published, attached, downloadable. Anyone could run the analysis. Anyone could check. The information existed in the world now, and the corporate license that would have locked it behind Meridian's proprietary access — the license embedded in the data surrender contract that most athletes had signed — was no longer the only gate.

She texted Yusuf. Just: *Saw it.*

His reply came four minutes later: *Yeah. Farah says hi by the way.*

Lena sat with the phone in her hand. The changing room smelled like chlorine and the wood of the old benches and the lavender soap in the dispensers by the showers. Farah. Yusuf's sister in Birmingham, whose progressive neural deterioration had driven everything — the unauthorized data collection, the personal archive, the risk, the broken trust with Kai, the alternative patch that was running in Lena's head right now. The data Yusuf had published was the virus analysis, not the treatment data. But the treatment data — the biological information he'd collected from athletes, the information that might eventually contribute to Voss-Kessler research — that was accessible now too, or the path to it was. The investigation would open Meridian's archives. Researchers who weren't Meridian could reach the data. The gate wasn't gone but it wasn't locked.

She didn't know if it would help Farah. She didn't know enough about Voss-Kessler to know whether biological data from enhanced athletes had any therapeutic application. But Yusuf's text said *Farah says hi*, and the *by the way* carried everything he wouldn't say directly: that his sister was still there, still cheerful, still deflecting questions about her worsening speech with the stubbornness he'd described in the Kirchberg cafeteria the night before they left for Doha. That the data was free and the research could continue and this was the thing he had risked his career for and it was enough. Not a victory. Not proof. Enough.

Lena toweled off. The towel caught on the port at the base of her skull — the small circle of titanium, warm from the pool water, the interface through which her neural link connected to the outside world and through which Yusuf's patch had been installed during a night she remembered in full because she'd been conscious for every second of it, the heat along the interface, the pressure behind her eyes, the hours. She touched it with two fingers, the way she always did, the absent check of a thing that was part of her body but was not her body.

She got dressed and walked out into August in Montreux. The lake was enormous and flat and the sun was on it and the air smelled like warm stone and the jasmine that grew along the promenade railing near the station. She had a train to catch to Lausanne. Her hair was still damp from the pool and the chlorine smell followed her the way it always had, since she was four years old in a calibration pool in Zug, since before she knew that the system in her head was a system and not herself. The smell was the same. What she was inside the smell was different, or starting to be different, or might be different eventually, and she walked toward the station with wet hair and the silence in her head and the two seconds per hundred meters that were hers.

***

The treatment facility was in Schwabing, in a converted Jugendstil house on a street lined with linden trees that smelled like August. The house had high ceilings and decorative plaster molding and a garden out back where patients were encouraged to sit in good weather. The art on the walls was the kind chosen by someone who understood that institutional spaces needed art but not what good art looked like — watercolors of lakes, a photograph of mountains that could have been any mountains, a print of a Klee that had been hung slightly crooked and that nobody had straightened in the six weeks Margot had been here.

Six weeks without the feed. Forty-two days. She didn't count them the way people in recovery programs were supposed to count them — with accumulating pride, each day a brick in the wall between you and the substance. She counted them the way she'd counted laps: because the number was information, and information was what she had instead of certainty.

The program was called NeuroBalance, and it was one of three facilities in Germany that specialized in neural-interface addiction. Most of the other patients were feed-dependent — people who'd developed compulsive relationships with entertainment-grade sensory interfaces, the immersive sports simulations and full-body relaxation feeds that were marketed as wellness tools and worked as digital opiates. The feeds they described were crude by comparison. They had plugged into recordings. Margot had plugged into people.

She tried to explain this to Helga during their Thursday session. Helga was forty-three, Bavarian, precise in her English the way Germans often were — grammatically careful, slightly over-enunciated. Good therapist. Did not understand what Margot was telling her.

"The entertainment feeds are recorded," Margot said. She was sitting in the chair across from Helga's desk, which had a small succulent on it and a framed photo of a dog. "Recorded and processed and cleaned up. Like a movie of a body. What I did was live. I was inside someone's actual nervous system while they were using it."

"Yes, you've said this." Helga's pen rested on her notepad. "What I'm interested in today is the distinction between the experience itself and the need to repeat it."

"There's no distinction."

"Can you say more?"

Margot looked at the succulent. It was the kind that didn't need much water, which was probably the point of keeping it on a therapist's desk. "The experience is the need. They're the same thing. It's not like I had the experience and then afterward I wanted more. The wanting is the experience. You plug in and you're already wanting to plug in again. The first second of someone else's body is the craving and the fix at the same time."

Helga wrote something. The pen moved across the notepad with steady pressure.

"And the memory of the experience," Helga said. "Is the memory also—"

"Intact," Margot said. "I can build the whole thing from memory. Right now, if I wanted to. The way it felt to be inside Tomás's body when he cleared a hurdle — the hamstring load, the visual lock on the bar, the proprioceptive sense of being twenty-one and built for exactly this. I can feel it now. Sitting here. Not as strong as the real thing. Strong enough."

"And the crash?"

"That too."

"You can reconstruct that sensation?"

"Yeah." Margot held her gaze. "The compression. The silence when the signals stopped. His legs going dead. All of it. I thought that would fix it. For about a week after it happened I thought, okay, this is the thing — you can't want something that did this to someone. But you can. You can know exactly what the feed cost a twenty-one-year-old kid and still want it. These two things just sit next to each other in my head without canceling out."

Helga was quiet. She did this after Margot said things that didn't fit the therapeutic framework — the pause that was supposed to look like reflection but was actually recalculation.

"The wanting," Helga said. "Has it changed in quality over the six weeks? Not necessarily in intensity. In quality."

"No."

"Not at all?"

"It's the same. It was this size on day one and it's this size on day forty-two. Doesn't get bigger. Doesn't get smaller. It's just there. Like furniture."

"We'll continue Thursday," Helga said, and wrote something else, and Margot stood up because the session was over and because there was nothing more to say about it that she hadn't already said.

After group — which was four people in a circle talking about triggers and coping strategies, during which Margot said reasonable things about mindfulness techniques she didn't believe in and made eye contact with a twenty-six-year-old named Stefan who was trembling through withdrawal from an entertainment feed he'd used eighteen hours a day for two years — Margot walked.

August in Munich. Warm, not hot. The lindens on Leopoldstraße in full leaf, the air carrying that late-summer smell of heated pavement and green and the distant mineral note of the Isar. She walked south through Schwabing, past the university, past cafés where students sat outside with laptops and beer in the late afternoon, past the Siegestor with its stone horses catching the low light. She was wearing jeans and a T-shirt and running shoes and she looked like anyone. That was the thing about it. Nobody on this street could see that the woman walking past the monument was carrying the precise sensory memory of another person's spinal cord going silent.

She ended up at the Eisbach. She hadn't meant to come here today, but she kept ending up here. The Eisbach was a channel of the Isar that ran through the Englischer Garten, and at a certain point the channel narrowed and the current formed a standing wave that surfers had been riding for decades. She'd found it her second week and kept coming back.

Four surfers today. Young, most of them. One woman in a wetsuit despite the warm air, her board shorter than standard, shaped for the wave's specific geometry. The wave was maybe a meter high, clean, glassy in the late light. The surfers dropped in one at a time from the bank — a sideways leap, the board angling to catch the current, then the rapid adjustment of weight and balance on a wave that never moved forward. Stationary surfing. The wave held and the surfer held and the river kept moving underneath them.

The woman in the wetsuit was good. She cut back and forth across the wave face with the efficient fluidity of someone whose body was doing what it had learned to do — weight drops, ankle adjustments, the continuous proprioceptive reading of a moving surface. Not enhanced. Not linked. Just muscle and board and river and the accumulated hours that made the motion look easy. Her weight sank into a bottom turn and her arm swept out for balance and her torso rotated and for a moment the line of her body was a clean functional expression of physics and intention and practice.

Margot could see the biomechanics. The quadricep engagement, the dorsiflexion, the counter-rotation of the hips. She could see it because she'd spent fifteen years learning to read bodies the way other people read faces. She could almost feel it — dimly, the way you heard music from another room — because the neural pathways the feed had built were still there, still listening for input that wasn't coming.

She sat on the bank and watched. The surfers rode the wave and climbed out and rode it again. The woman in the wetsuit finally lost her line and splashed into the current and hauled herself out laughing, water streaming off her suit, her board tucked under one arm. She shook her hair and said something to the guy waiting to drop in and he laughed too and the sound carried across the water in the warm air.

Margot watched until the light changed and the surfers climbed out one by one and walked away with their boards under their arms and the standing wave kept going, the water folding over itself the way it did whether anyone was riding it or not.

She stood up and walked back to the facility because it was almost dinner. The evening was warm. The lindens smelled like summer. Her legs carried her through the park and along the Leopoldstraße and up the steps of the old house, and the wanting was there, the same size it had been that morning, which was the same size it had been the morning before, which was the same size it was every morning, ordinary and entire, and she walked.
